Divine Attributes
beginner

Maturidi's Distinction Between Names and Attributes
Maturidi distinguishes between essential names and essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's terminological distinction between essential names (al-asma' al-dhatiyya) and essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya).

Core Idea
Essential names are the adjectives; essential attributes are the underlying realities.

Maturidi, like many theologians, needed precise language to discuss God's nature. In this passage, he introduces a crucial distinction between the names we use to address God—like 'the Living'—and the attributes that those names refer to—like 'life'. Grasping this distinction is key to understanding his theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of Maturidi's distinction. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the terms he uses for each category.
2
Identify the two categories
Look for the two terms: 'essential names' (al-asma' al-dhatiyya) and 'essential attributes' (al-sifat al-dhatiyya). Notice how he defines each: names are words that are grammatically attributes, while attributes are the source words.
The category names are clearly stated.
3
Analyze the examples
The passage gives examples: al-Hayy, al-'Alim, al-Qadir, al-Rahman for names; al-hayat, al-'ilm, al-qudra for attributes. See the pattern: the name is the adjective, the attribute is the abstract noun.
This pattern shows the linguistic derivation.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this distinction matters. It clarifies that when we call God 'the Knowing', we are referring to the attribute of knowledge inherent in His essence. Also note Maturidi's devotional usage of many names.
This distinction avoids confusion between language and reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفهم من عبارات الماتريدي حول بيانه بالصفات الإلهية بأنه يطلق عبارة «الأسماء الذاتية» لكلمات هي من حيث صيغتها صفات، مثل الحي، والعالم، والقادر ، والرحمن؛ وأما المصدر لتلك الكلمات مثل الحياة والعلم والقدرة ، فهي كلمات يسميها «الصفات الذاتية». وفي كتابيه اللذين وصلا إلى يومنا الحاضر، نرى الماتريدي فيهما يستعمل عبارات تعبدية تصل في مناسبات عديدة إلى تعبيرات ،صوفية فيضيف حينئذ إلى الله تعالى عديدا من الأسماء
And we understand from Maturidi's statements regarding his exposition of the divine attributes that he applies the term 'essential names' (al-asma' al-dhatiyya) to words that, in terms of their form, are attributes, such as the Living (al-Hayy), the Knowing (al-'Alim), the Powerful (al-Qadir), and the Most Merciful (al-Rahman); and as for the source (masdar) of those words such as life (al-hayat), knowledge (al-'ilm), and power (al-qudra), they are words he calls 'essential attributes' (al-sifat al-dhatiyya). And in his two books that have reached us to this day, we see Maturidi in them using devotional expressions that on many occasions reach Sufi expressions, and he then adds to Allah the Exalted many names.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage clarifies Maturidi's terminology regarding divine names and attributes. He distinguishes between 'essential names' (al-asma' al-dhatiyya), which are words that are grammatically adjectives (like al-Hayy, al-'Alim, al-Qadir, al-Rahman), and 'essential attributes' (al-sifat al-dhatiyya), which are the abstract nouns from which these names are derived (like al-hayat, al-'ilm, al-qudra). The names are the terms used to address God in worship and description, while the attributes are the underlying realities. Maturidi's approach shows a careful semantic distinction to avoid confusion between the linguistic expression and the ontological reality. The passage also notes that Maturidi uses many names for God, sometimes in a devotional or even Sufi style, indicating the breadth of his theological language.

Key Terms

الصفات الذاتية
الحي
العالم

الرحمن
الحياة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinction implies that names are created or attributes are separate from God.
Correction: It is a terminological classification; names and attributes both refer to God's essence in Maturidi's theology.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is an example of an 'essential attribute' (sifa dhatiyya)?
A) The Most Merciful (al-Rahman)
B) Knowledge (al-'ilm)
C) The Powerful (al-Qadir)
D) The Living (al-Hayy)

Key Takeaway
Maturidi distinguishes between essential names (adjective forms like al-Hayy) and essential attributes (source nouns like al-hayat), a crucial terminological clarification for discussing God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by one of His names, do you consciously connect that name to the attribute it represents? How might this awareness transform your du'a?
?
Maturidi took care to distinguish between names and attributes so that we understand God correctly. Is there a name of Allah you recite often but have never stopped to reflect on the reality it describes? What would it mean for you to truly live in light of that attribute?

Divine Attributes

Maturidi's Verbal Distinction in Divine Attributes
Maturidi explains how to affirm attributes without implying likeness to creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method of affirming divine attributes while avoiding real anthropomorphism by distinguishing verbal similarity from conceptual likeness.

Core Idea
Using the same words for God and creatures does not imply real likeness—only verbal expression.

Maturidi addresses a central challenge in Islamic theology: how can we call God 'Knower' and 'Powerful' when we use the same words for humans? If we say God knows, does that imply He knows like we do? Maturidi offers a nuanced answer by distinguishing between the words we use and the realities they point to.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own reasoning come through.
Notice the reference to necessity (al-darura) and the witnessed (al-shahid).
2
Identify the problem of seeming similarity
Maturidi admits that using the same words for God and creatures suggests similarity on the surface. Find where he acknowledges this.
The phrase 'فلعله في الظاهر يوجب التشابه' points to this.
3
Understand Maturidi's solution
He says that necessity (al-darura) forces us to negate the concept of similarity from the witnessed realm. This means we deny any real likeness despite using the same words.
The key is that the similarity is only in speech (القول), not in reality.
4
See the example of multiple languages
Maturidi points out that the same attribute is expressed with different words in different languages, yet the meaning is not identical. This shows the words are just approximations.
This reinforces that the names are not literal descriptions.
5
Reflect on the Quranic principle
He concludes with the verse 'There is nothing like Him' as a foundational principle accepted by all, even anthropomorphists, implying that verbal similarity cannot imply real likeness.
This verse is the ultimate safeguard against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ذهب الماتريدي إلى القول بأنه احتمال لا نستطيع الابتعاد عنه، فلعله في الظاهر يوجب التشابه؛ ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفي المفهوم من الشاهد ليُنفى به الشبه. ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمّى به غير كنا نسميه» . غير أن التشابه هنا في القول فقط ؛ ألا ترى أن العبارة التي بها نسميه عز وجل عالِمًا وقادرًا في اللغات المتعددة مختلفة من غير أن كان ثمة اختلاف في المعنى. وهذا يعنى أن الأسماء التي نسميه بها فهي ليست إلا عبارات وألفاظ تقرب المعاني اللائقة بالله تعالى إلى الأفهام والأذهان. فقد أصبح بالتالي البيان الإلهي للصفات في قوله (ليس كمثله شيء) [الشورى: ١١/٤٢] مبدءا تبناه الجميع حتى التشبيه في مثل هذا التوصيف لذات الله تعالى
Al-Maturidi went to the view that it is a possibility we cannot avoid; perhaps it entails similarity in appearance, but necessity has allowed us to negate the concept from the witnessed realm in order to negate the likeness. And we name Him with what I mentioned out of necessity, and even if it were possible, we would have allowed naming Him with what nothing else is called, but we name Him [with these]. But the similarity here is only in speech; do you not see that the expressions by which we call Him, exalted, 'Knower' and 'Powerful' in multiple languages are different, yet there is no difference in meaning. And this means that the names by which we call Him are nothing but expressions and words that bring the meanings befitting God to minds and intellects. Thus, the divine statement about attributes in His words 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Q. 42:11) became a principle adopted by all, even the anthropomorphists, in such a description of the essence of God.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage explains Maturidi's approach to affirming divine attributes without falling into anthropomorphism. He acknowledges that using the same words for God and creatures admittedly suggests similarity on the surface. However, necessity (al-darura) forces us to negate the concept of similarity from what we witness (al-shahid) in order to remove any likeness. The similarity is only verbal, not real: the same attributes (knowledge, power) are expressed in different languages with different words, yet the meaning is not identical. The names we use are mere approximations that convey the appropriate meanings to human minds. Maturidi then cites the Quranic verse 'Nothing is like Him' (42:11) as a foundational principle that even anthropomorphists adopt in describing God, implying that a purely verbal similarity cannot imply real likeness.

Key Terms
التشابه
الضرورة
الشاهد
ليس كمثله شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the same words for God and creatures means God is like creatures in those attributes.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the similarity is only in the words used, not in the realities. The divine attribute is utterly transcendent, and the words are mere approximations.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does using the same names for God and creatures not entail real similarity?
A) Because those names are only used for God, not for creatures
B) Because the words are only expressions that approximate meanings, and the similarity is in speech only
C) Because creatures do not actually have knowledge or power
D) Because God's attributes are completely unknown

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that divine attribute names are verbal approximations, not reflections of human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knower,' do you truly believe His knowledge is utterly unlike any human knowledge, or does your heart subtly imagine it as a greater version of yours? What step can you take today to purify your understanding of divine attributes?
?
Do you find yourself using the same words for God and creation carelessly, without consciously negating the likeness? How can you cultivate a habit of affirming transcendence in your speech?
?
The necessity (al-darura) of speaking about God means your words, though finite, point to the Real. Does that humble you? In your next dua, will you be mindful that your expressions are mere approximations, requiring your heart's full presence?

Maturidi's Distinction: Essential Names vs. Essential Attributes
Maturidi classifies words like al-Hayy as essential names and their sources as essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's terminological distinction between essential names (al-asma' al-dhatiyya) and essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya), and why this distinction matters in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Essential names are attributes in form; essential attributes are their sources.

Maturidi, a foundational figure in Sunni theology, developed precise terminology to discuss God's attributes. This passage introduces his distinction between words that are morphologically attributes (like al-Hayy, 'the Living') and the underlying qualities they refer to (like life itself). Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusion between the linguistic expression and the theological reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how Maturidi classifies two types of terms.
Pay attention to the examples: al-Hayy, al-‘Alim, al-Qadir, al-Rahman.
2
Identify the two categories
Identify which terms are called 'essential names' and which are called 'essential attributes'. Note the pattern: names are attribute forms, attributes are their source nouns.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction is important for tawhid. How does it help affirm God's attributes without implying multiple eternal entities?
This guards against confusing language with reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفهم من عبارات الماتريدي حول بيانه بالصفات الإلهية بأنه يطلق عبارة «الأسماء الذاتية» لكلمات هي من حيث صيغتها صفات، مثل الحي، والعالم، والقادر، والرحمن؛ وأما المصدر لتلك الكلمات مثل الحياة والعلم والقدرة، فهي كلمات يسميها «الصفات الذاتية». وفي كتابيه اللذين وصلا إلى يومنا الحاضر، نرى الماتريدي فيهما يستعمل عبارات تعبدية تصل في مناسبات عديدة إلى تعبيرات صوفية فيضيف حينئذ إلى الله تعالى عديدا من الأسماء والصفات.
And we understand from al-Maturidi's statements regarding his explanation of the divine attributes that he applies the term 'essential names' (al-asma' al-dhatiyya) to words which, in terms of their form, are attributes, such as al-Hayy (the Living), al-‘Alim (the Knowing), al-Qadir (the Powerful), and al-Rahman (the Merciful). As for the source of those words, such as al-hayah (life), al-‘ilm (knowledge), and al-qudra (power), these are words he calls 'essential attributes' (al-sifat al-dhatiyya). And in his two books that have reached us to the present day, we see al-Maturidi in them using devotional expressions that in many occasions reach mystical expressions; he then adds to God, exalted, many names and attributes.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage explains Maturidi's terminological distinction between 'essential names' (al-asma' al-dhatiyya) and 'essential attributes' (al-sifat al-dhatiyya). The essential names are words that are morphologically attributes, like al-Hayy (the Living), al-‘Alim (the Knowing), al-Qadir (the Powerful), and al-Rahman (the Merciful). Their source nouns—life, knowledge, power—are called 'essential attributes.' This distinction allows Maturidi to affirm the attributes without implying multiplicity in the essence. The passage also notes that Maturidi, in his extant works, uses devotional and sometimes mystical language, adding many names and attributes to God.

Key Terms
الأسماء الذاتية
الصفات الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that calling God 'al-Hayy' is the same as saying He has 'life' as a separate quality.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between the name (al-Hayy) and the attribute (life). The name is a form of address, while the attribute refers to the quality itself, but neither implies multiplicity in God.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi call the source words like 'life' (al-hayah) and 'knowledge' (al-‘ilm)?
A) Essential names
B) Essential attributes
C) Divine names
D) Active attributes

Key Takeaway
Maturidi distinguishes between essential names (attribute-form words) and essential attributes (their source nouns) to precisely discuss God's attributes without implying multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Hayy, do you realize that this name refers to His very essence, not a quality added to Him? How does that deepen your reliance on His self-sufficiency?
?
Maturidi was precise in distinguishing names from attributes. Are you equally careful in how you speak about Allah, or do you use His names thoughtlessly? What step will you take today to be more mindful of His names?
?
The passage mentions that Maturidi's expressions reached mystical devotion. When you recite Allah's names, is your heart engaged as fervently as your tongue? What would need to change in your routine for that to become true?

Maturidi's Threefold Classification of Divine Attributes
Maturidi introduces his threefold classification of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's threefold classification of divine attributes into negative, affirmative, and active.

Core Idea
Maturidi classifies attributes into three types: negative, affirmative, and active.

This lesson introduces Maturidi's classification of divine attributes, which aligns with later Sunni kalam. He categorizes attributes into three types: negative (salbiyya) that negate imperfection, affirmative (thubutiyya) that connect God to creation, and active (fi'liyya) that describe God's relationship with the world. Understanding this classification is key to grasping Maturidi's theological framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi lists three categories of attributes.
2
Identify the three categories
Find where the passage mentions the negative, affirmative, and active attributes. Note the function of each.
Negative attributes negate anthropomorphism; affirmative attributes connect God to people; active attributes relate to creation.
3
Reflect on the classification's purpose
Consider why Maturidi organized attributes this way. How does each category uphold tawhid?
This classification prevents anthropomorphism while affirming God's actions.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasped the role of negative attributes.
The checkpoint is based directly on the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجدير بالذكر أن تصنيف الصفات الذي كان سائدا في مؤلفات كلامية متأخرة لدى المذهب السنى عامة نراه موجودًا عند الماتريدي أيضًا. فبناء على ذلك، نراه يتناول موضوعات عديدة، منها يستدل بأن محدث العالم واحد فينفي بالصفات السلبية كل شيء يوهم التشبيه (خصوصا في خاصيتي العجز والنقص)؛ ومنها خلق العالم ونظامه، وكذلك النداء المنشق الذي يوجّهه الله إلى الناس عن طريق الصفات الثبوتية التي تربطه بالذات الإلهية، وأخيرًا الصفات الفعلية التي تتعلق بالعلاقة بين الله وبين العالم. لقد تعرض الماتريدي في أبحاث مستقلة الصفات التالية: صفة الكلام التي تعتبر إحدى الصفات الثبوتية ولها أهميتها من حيث كونها صفة قديمة أو مخلوقة، وصفة الإرادة التي تعتبر مهمة في البحث عن دور العبد وتأثير الإرادة الإلهية في الأفعال الإرادية وكونها موضع البحث حول مشاكل القضاء والقدر، وصفة الرؤية التي تبحث موضوع رؤية الله ضمن الصفات التنزيهية. ومن الجدير بالذكر أن صفات التنزيه أو الصفات السلبية التي تنظم مبدأ التوحيد لديه في فهمه للألوهية لها أهميتها الخاصة عنده؛ ففي هذا المجال قد بذل جهدًا واسعا في توجيه اتهاماته ضد الفرق الإسلامية والمعتقدات والتيارات الخارجة عن الإسلام على حد سواء
And it is worth noting that the classification of attributes that was prevalent in later kalam works within the Sunni school in general is found also in al-Maturidi. Accordingly, we see him addressing many topics: he argues that the originator of the world is one, and through the negative attributes (al-sifat al-salbiyya) he negates everything that suggests anthropomorphism (especially in the characteristics of incapability and deficiency); and among them are the creation of the world and its order, as well as the call that God directs to people through the affirmative attributes (al-sifat al-thubutiyya) that connect Him to the divine essence; and finally the active attributes (al-sifat al-fi‘liyya) that relate to the relationship between God and the world. Al-Maturidi discussed in independent investigations the following attributes: the attribute of speech, which is one of the affirmative attributes and has importance in terms of being an eternal or created attribute; the attribute of will, which is important in researching the role of the servant and the influence of the divine will in voluntary actions and being the subject of research concerning issues of predestination and decree; and the attribute of vision, which investigates the topic of seeing God within the attributes of transcendence. And it is worth noting that the attributes of transcendence (al-sifat al-tanzihiyya) or the negative attributes (al-sifat al-salbiyya), which organize the principle of tawhid in his understanding of divinity, have their own special importance in his view; in this area he made great effort in directing his accusations against Islamic sects and non-Islamic beliefs and currents alike.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage outlines Maturidi's classification of divine attributes. He follows the later Sunni classification into three types: (1) negative attributes (sifāt salbiyya) that negate imperfection and anthropomorphism, especially in negating incapability and deficiency; (2) affirmative attributes (sifāt thubūtiyya) that connect God to the essence, such as are used in God's call to people; and (3) active attributes (sifāt fi‘liyya) concerning God's relation to the world. Then the passage lists three attributes al-Maturidi independently investigated: speech (kalām), which is discussed as eternal or created; will (irāda), which involves the role of the servant and predestination; and vision (ru’ya), discussed within the framework of transcendence. The passage emphasizes the special importance of the negative/transcendence attributes for Maturidi's principle of tawhid, and notes his effort in refuting various Islamic and non-Islamic groups.

Key Terms
الصفات السلبية
الصفات الثبوتية
الصفات الفعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi only discusses two categories of attributes (negative and affirmative).
Correction: Maturidi explicitly includes a third category: active attributes (fi‘liyya), which concern God's relationship with the world.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of attributes does Maturidi use to negate everything that suggests anthropomorphism?
A) Affirmative attributes
B) Active attributes
C) Negative attributes
D) Essential names

Key Takeaway
Maturidi classifies divine attributes into three categories: negative (salbiyya), affirmative (thubutiyya), and active (fi‘liyya), each serving a distinct theological purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously imagine them like human qualities? How can Maturidi's emphasis on negative attributes help you purify your concept of God in your heart, starting today?
?
The passage says that through affirmative attributes, God directs a call to people. Are you truly listening to that call in your life, or have you become desensitized to it? What practical step can you take this week to respond?
?
Active attributes describe God's ongoing relationship with the world. Do you live as if God is actively involved in your daily affairs, or have you slipped into a deistic mindset? How will you remind yourself of His constant presence and action?

Key Attributes: Speech, Will, Vision, and the Primacy of Negative Attributes
Maturidi discusses three specific attributes and the primacy of negative attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's treatment of the attributes of speech, will, and vision, and the central role of negative attributes in his theology.

Core Idea
Speech, will, and vision are key attributes Maturidi investigates, with negative attributes forming the core of tawhid.

This lesson examines three specific attributes that Maturidi discussed independently: speech (kalām), will (irāda), and vision (ru’ya). Each is placed within his larger framework of affirmative and negative attributes. The lesson also highlights the special importance of negative attributes (sifāt tanzihiyya) in Maturidi's understanding of tawhid and his refutation of opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the three attributes
The passage names three attributes Maturidi investigated: speech, will, and vision. Identify them and note their descriptions.
2
Understand the theological issue for each
For speech: is it eternal or created? For will: how does it relate to human actions and predestination? For vision: can God be seen?
These were debated topics in kalam.
3
Recognize the emphasis on negative attributes
The final part stresses that negative attributes (tanzihiyya) are fundamental for tawhid and were used to refute erroneous groups.
Maturidi directed accusations based on this understanding.
4
Reflect on the overall framework
Consider how these specific attributes fit into the threefold classification from the previous lesson.
Speech and will are affirmative; vision is discussed under transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجدير بالذكر أن تصنيف الصفات الذي كان سائدا في مؤلفات كلامية متأخرة لدى المذهب السنى عامة نراه موجودًا عند الماتريدي أيضًا. فبناء على ذلك، نراه يتناول موضوعات عديدة، منها يستدل بأن محدث العالم واحد فينفي بالصفات السلبية كل شيء يوهم التشبيه (خصوصا في خاصيتي العجز والنقص)؛ ومنها خلق العالم ونظامه، وكذلك النداء المنشق الذي يوجّهه الله إلى الناس عن طريق الصفات الثبوتية التي تربطه بالذات الإلهية، وأخيرًا الصفات الفعلية التي تتعلق بالعلاقة بين الله وبين العالم. لقد تعرض الماتريدي في أبحاث مستقلة الصفات التالية: صفة الكلام التي تعتبر إحدى الصفات الثبوتية ولها أهميتها من حيث كونها صفة قديمة أو مخلوقة، وصفة الإرادة التي تعتبر مهمة في البحث عن دور العبد وتأثير الإرادة الإلهية في الأفعال الإرادية وكونها موضع البحث حول مشاكل القضاء والقدر، وصفة الرؤية التي تبحث موضوع رؤية الله ضمن الصفات التنزيهية. ومن الجدير بالذكر أن صفات التنزيه أو الصفات السلبية التي تنظم مبدأ التوحيد لديه في فهمه للألوهية لها أهميتها الخاصة عنده؛ ففي هذا المجال قد بذل جهدًا واسعا في توجيه اتهاماته ضد الفرق الإسلامية والمعتقدات والتيارات الخارجة عن الإسلام على حد سواء
And it is worth noting that the classification of attributes that was prevalent in later kalam works within the Sunni school in general is found also in al-Maturidi. Accordingly, we see him addressing many topics: he argues that the originator of the world is one, and through the negative attributes (al-sifat al-salbiyya) he negates everything that suggests anthropomorphism (especially in the characteristics of incapability and deficiency); and among them are the creation of the world and its order, as well as the call that God directs to people through the affirmative attributes (al-sifat al-thubutiyya) that connect Him to the divine essence; and finally the active attributes (al-sifat al-fi‘liyya) that relate to the relationship between God and the world. Al-Maturidi discussed in independent investigations the following attributes: the attribute of speech, which is one of the affirmative attributes and has importance in terms of being an eternal or created attribute; the attribute of will, which is important in researching the role of the servant and the influence of the divine will in voluntary actions and being the subject of research concerning issues of predestination and decree; and the attribute of vision, which investigates the topic of seeing God within the attributes of transcendence. And it is worth noting that the attributes of transcendence (al-sifat al-tanzihiyya) or the negative attributes (al-sifat al-salbiyya), which organize the principle of tawhid in his understanding of divinity, have their own special importance in his view; in this area he made great effort in directing his accusations against Islamic sects and non-Islamic beliefs and currents alike.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage outlines Maturidi's classification of divine attributes. He follows the later Sunni classification into three types: (1) negative attributes (sifāt salbiyya) that negate imperfection and anthropomorphism, especially in negating incapability and deficiency; (2) affirmative attributes (sifāt thubūtiyya) that connect God to the essence, such as are used in God's call to people; and (3) active attributes (sifāt fi‘liyya) concerning God's relation to the world. Then the passage lists three attributes al-Maturidi independently investigated: speech (kalām), which is discussed as eternal or created; will (irāda), which involves the role of the servant and predestination; and vision (ru’ya), discussed within the framework of transcendence. The passage emphasizes the special importance of the negative/transcendence attributes for Maturidi's principle of tawhid, and notes his effort in refuting various Islamic and non-Islamic groups.

Key Terms
صفة الكلام
صفة الإرادة
صفة الرؤية
صفات التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The attribute of will
B) The attribute of speech
C) The attribute of vision
D) The attribute of power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's independent discussions of speech, will, and vision highlight key kalam debates, while negative attributes serve as the foundation of tawhid and refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that negative attributes are the foundation of tawhid for Maturidi. Think about your own conception of Allah: do you focus more on what He is (affirmative attributes) or on what He is not? Which one truly shapes your worship and awe?
?
Maturidi used negative attributes to refute other groups. Are there any subtle beliefs or cultural ideas you hold that might limit Allah's transcendence? What would it cost you to uproot them and affirm His absolute uniqueness?
?
The attribute of will touches on predestination. How does your understanding of divine will affect your view of your own choices and mistakes? Do you live with a sense of surrender to Allah's will, or do you resist it when things don't go your way?

The Three Categories of Divine Attributes
Maturidi enumerates three categories of attributes and explains their roles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three categories of divine attributes—negative, positive, and active—and their respective functions in affirming tawḥīd.

Core Idea
Maturidi classifies attributes to safeguard tawḥīd from anthropomorphism and deficiency.

Maturidi’s theological method begins with a classification of divine attributes that balances transcendence and relation. He distinguishes between negative attributes that clear God of imperfections, positive attributes that connect Him to His creation, and active attributes that describe His ongoing interaction with the world. This passage introduces that framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi uses concise expressions.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three categories of attributes: negative (salbiyya), positive (thubūtiyya), and active (fi‘liyya). Find the description of each.
3
Understand the purpose of each category
Ask yourself what function each category serves. The passage tells you: negative attributes negate anthropomorphism, positive attributes connect God to creation, active attributes describe interaction.
This is the key to Maturidi's system.

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive summary of Maturidi's classification of divine attributes. It begins by noting that his classification follows the later Sunni tradition. Then it explains the three categories: (1) negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya) that negate imperfection and anthropomorphism, (2) positive attributes (ṣifāt thubūtiyya) that relate to the divine essence and through which God communicates with people, and (3) active attributes (ṣifāt fi‘liyya) that pertain to God's interaction with the world. ?
The active attributes remind us that God is constantly involved in the world. Do you live as if God is actively engaged in your daily life, or do you act as if He is distant? What one change will you make starting tomorrow to acknowledge His ongoing role in your affairs?

Specific Attributes: Speech, Will, and Vision
Maturidi lists three attributes he investigated independently and their issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's treatment of three key attributes—speech (kalām), will (irāda), and vision (ru’ya)—and the theological issues associated with each.

Core Idea
Maturidi treats speech, will, and vision as central attributes with distinct theological implications.

Beyond the general classification, Maturidi dedicates independent studies to specific attributes that have major doctrinal ramifications. This passage highlights three such attributes—speech, will, and vision—and the debates surrounding them, such as whether speech is eternal or created, the role of divine will in human actions, and the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three attributes mentioned.
2
Identify the three attributes and their issues
For each attribute, note the theological issue it raises: for speech, whether it is eternal or created; for will, its relation to human action and predestination; for vision, its compatibility with God's transcendence.
3
Reflect on why these issues matter
Consider how each issue relates to the broader defense of tawḥīd. For example, the eternity of speech affirms God's perfection, while the vision debate touches on divine transcendence.
This connects to the negative and positive categories from the previous lesson.

Negative Attributes and the Principle of Tawḥīd
Maturidi emphasizes the importance of negative attributes for tawḥīd and his efforts against opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central role that negative attributes play in Maturidi's conception of tawḥīd and how he used them to refute opposing views.

Core Idea
Negative attributes are the foundation of tawḥīd, clearing God of all imperfections.

Maturidi gives special emphasis to the negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya) because they directly uphold the principle of tawḥīd. By negating any imperfection or resemblance to creation, these attributes protect God's uniqueness. The passage notes that Maturidi exerted great effort in using these attributes to refute those who associate deficiency with God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic, then the translation. Pay attention to the language of organization and effort.
2
Identify the key function of negative attributes
The passage says negative attributes 'organize the principle of tawḥīd'. Understand what that means—they are not just a list of negations but a coherent system.
3
Reflect on Maturidi's effort
The passage mentions he exerted extensive effort in directing accusations. Consider why negative attributes would be the basis for refuting opponents.
These attributes guard against anthropomorphism and deficiency.

6 min
Wisdom, Justice, Good and Evil: Definitions and Refutations
Defines key terms and refutes dualist and Mu'tazilite interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper meanings of wisdom, justice, good, and evil in Islamic theology, and why dualist and Mu'tazilite definitions are inadequate.

Core Idea
True wisdom is correctness, not benefit to self or others.

Imam al-Maturidi begins his discussion of predestination by clarifying foundational terms. Before we can talk about divine decree, we must agree on what these words mean. He defines wisdom, justice, good, and evil with precision, then critiques two faulty definitions that reduce wisdom to mere benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the discussion.
Pay attention to the sequence: context → definitions → universal agreement → critique.
2
Identify the four terms and their definitions
List the terms wisdom, justice, good, and evil, and note how Maturidi defines each one. Compare with their opposites.
Wisdom is 'correctness in speech and action', justice is 'placing a thing in its proper place', good is 'what brings a good result'.
3
Notice the universal agreement
Maturidi states that all Muslims and religious adherents agree that Allah must be described with these positive terms and declared free from their opposites.
This shows the definitions are not controversial.
4
Understand the dualist and Mu'tazilite definitions
The dualists define wisdom as 'beneficial to its possessor', the Mu'tazila as 'beneficial to others'. Compare these with the correct definition.
Both make wisdom relative to benefit, which Maturidi rejects.
5
Grasp why these definitions are wrong
Maturidi says both groups tried to posit a cause for divine actions and to find a result fitting benefit and praise. This imposes a limitation on Allah's actions.
True wisdom is intrinsic correctness, not contingent benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن موضوع القدر يعتبر من الناحية موضوع البحث حول عدم تضييق دائرة الاهتمام بالصفات الإلهية وتجهيز الأرضية المناسبة للتكليف الحقوقي والأخلاقي للمكلف، ومن ناحية أخرى يجب الاهتمام به لإيجاد حل لمشكلة الشر. فقد دخل الماتريدي هذا المبحث بالبحث عن المشكلة الأخيرة. ويعنى ذلك أنه يحاول بهذه الطريقة قبل كل شيء أن يوضح بالبيان الشافي لبعض المصطلحات مثل الحكمة التي تعني الإصابة في القول والفعل وضده السفه، والعدل الذي يعني وضع الشيء في موضعه وضده الجور، والخير الذي يعني الشيء الذي تأتي بنتيجة حسنة وضده الشر. ومن المعلوم أن المسلمين جميعا، وحتى جميع المعتنقين للأديان، يعتقدون بلزوم وصف الله تعالى بمعاني تلك المصطلحات وتنزيهه بأضدادها. غير أن هؤلاء الذين يتبنون عقيدة الثنائية (الثنوية) يعرفون الحكمة بأنها الشيء النافع لصاحبه وكذلك هؤلاء المنتسبون للاعتزال يعرفونها بأنها الشيء الذي ينفع الآخر فهما فرقتان استهدفتا وضع علة للأفعال الإلهية، وفي الوقت نفسه حاولتا من عندهم إيجاد نتيجة لائقة للنفع والمدح.
The topic of predestination is considered, on the one hand, a subject of research concerning not narrowing the circle of attention to the divine attributes and preparing the appropriate foundation for the legal and moral obligation of the morally responsible person, and on the other hand, it must be attended to in order to find a solution to the problem of evil. Al-Maturidi entered this investigation by researching the last problem. This means that he tries in this way, before everything else, to clarify with a sufficient explanation some terms such as wisdom (al-hikmah), which means correctness in speech and action, and its opposite is foolishness (al-safah); justice (al-'adl), which means placing a thing in its proper place, and its opposite is injustice (al-jawr); and good (al-khayr), which means that which brings a good result, and its opposite is evil (al-sharr). And it is known that all Muslims, and even all adherents of religions, believe in the necessity of describing God Almighty with the meanings of these terms and declaring Him transcendent above their opposites. However, those who adopt the doctrine of dualism (al-thunawiyyah) define wisdom as 'the thing beneficial to its possessor,' and likewise those affiliated with the Mu'tazila define it as 'the thing beneficial to others'; both sects aimed to posit a cause for divine actions, and at the same time they tried from themselves to find a fitting result for benefit and praise.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines key theological terms and refutes misinterpretations. It begins by situating the discussion of predestination within the broader context of divine attributes and the problem of evil. Al-Maturidi enters this topic by first clarifying terms: hikmah is correctness in speech and action (opposite: safah), 'adl is placing something in its proper place (opposite: jawr), and khayr is what yields a good result (opposite: sharr). All Muslims and religious adherents agree that God must be described by these positive terms and declared free from their opposites. However, the dualists (al-thunawiyyah) define hikmah as 'what benefits its possessor,' and the Mu'tazila define it as 'what benefits others.' Both groups err by attempting to assign an external cause to divine actions and seeking a result that suits benefit and praise, thus anthropomorphizing God's wisdom.

Correction: True wisdom is intrinsic correctness in speech and action, not contingent on benefit. Allah's actions are wise regardless of perceived benefit.

What would it mean for you to accept God’s placements as intrinsically just, even when they don’t match your sense of fairness?
?

Relativity of Good and Evil Except Lying
Al-Maturidi explains the relativity of justice and foolishness except lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Maturidi, moral judgments (good/evil) are generally relative to context, with lying as the sole exception that is always evil.

Core Idea
Moral relativity stops at lying: it is always evil.

This passage from al-Maturidi addresses a key nuance in divine attributes and ethics. While justice and wisdom are good, and injustice and foolishness are evil, these judgments can reverse depending on the situation—except for lying, which is never justified. Let's examine his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the emphasis on context and the exception of lying.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the general rule
Find where al-Maturidi says that something may be foolish or wise depending on person or situation.
This is the relativity principle.
3
Spot the exception
Find the statement about lying being the only thing that does not change.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Understand the implications
The passage then discusses human ignorance of true outcomes and that every harm can yield benefit.
This connects to divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما لا شك فيه أن الجور (الظلم) والسفه قبيحان، وأن العدل والحكمة حسنان في الجملة عند الماتريدي؛ لكن شيئًا واحدا قد يكون سفها في شخص أو في حال، وقد يكون حكمة في شخص أو في حال. ويعني ذلك أن كل شيء يمكن أن يكون في حال وفي ضده، ما عدا الكذب الذي لا يتغير من حال إلى حال على الإطلاق. فيلزم بهذا جهل كل من البشر لمعرفة نتائج حقيقة السفه والحكمة وأحوالهما في شيء ما بالتأمل فيه، مثل أن يعرف جميع الأسباب التي بها تتغير أحوال المحسوسات على الحواس. ولا يوجد ضرر ألبتة إلا وأمكن أن ينتفع به أحد. فهذا الانتفاع يكون إما عن طريق الدلالة إلى الحق، وإما أن يكون الانتفاع هذا عن طريق الموعظة وسيلة، أو ما يحتمل بعض العناصر فيه مثل تذكر النعمة وتحذير النقمة، أو يكون من تذكير الله تعالى الذي يملك قدرة الخلق وسلطة الأمر في العالم، وغير ذلك مما يكثر ذكره من الخصوصيات. فالمفهوم من ذلك كله بأننا كما نفهم بأن الأفعال الإلهية ليست ببعيدة عن الحكمة في معناها المطلق، فكذلك تثبت الحقيقة القائلة بأن الحقائق إذا انفردت على حدة لا يمكن دائما تثبيت الحكمة أو السفه في كل واحدة منها على حدة.
And there is no doubt that injustice (al-jawr) and foolishness (al-safah) are ugly, and that justice (al-'adl) and wisdom (al-hikmah) are good in general according to al-Maturidi; but one and the same thing may be foolishness in one person or situation, and may be wisdom in another person or situation. This means that everything can be in one state and its opposite, except lying (al-kadhib), which does not change from state to state at all. Hence, it follows that every human being is ignorant of knowing the true results of foolishness and wisdom and their states in a thing through reflection, like knowing all the causes by which the states of sensible things change upon the senses. And there is no harm whatsoever except that someone can benefit from it. This benefit is either by way of indicating the truth, or by way of admonition as a means, or by what may contain some elements such as reminding of blessing and warning of punishment, or by the reminder of God Almighty who possesses the power of creation and the authority of command in the world, and other particularities too numerous to mention. So the understanding from all this is that just as we understand that divine actions are not far from wisdom in its absolute sense, so too the truth is established that when realities are considered individually, it is not always possible to definitively assign wisdom or foolishness to each one individually.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage discusses the relativity of moral judgments. According to al-Maturidi, injustice and foolishness are generally ugly, while justice and wisdom are generally good. However, the same action can be foolish in one context and wise in another, with the sole exception of lying, which is always ugly regardless of context. Because of this relativity, humans cannot fully know the true outcomes of foolishness and wisdom through reflection alone. The passage then notes that every harm can potentially yield some benefit, whether by pointing to truth, serving as admonition, reminding of blessings or punishment, or reminding of God's power. The conclusion is that while divine actions are never devoid of absolute wisdom, when individual realities are isolated, it is not always possible to definitively label them as wise or foolish, because their ultimate context is known only to God.

Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that most moral judgments depend on context, except lying which is always evil.

Human beings cannot fully know the outcomes of actions to judge whether they are foolish or wise (al-Maturidi). How does this lesson challenge you to suspend judgment on your current circumstances and rely on God's perfect knowledge?

Divine Wisdom and Context-Dependent Moral Values
Maturidi discusses divine wisdom and the context-dependence of moral qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's position that while Allah's wisdom is absolute and intrinsic, human judgments of good and bad are often context-dependent, with the sole exception of lying being always evil.

Core Idea
Context determines moral value except for lying, which is always ugly.

Maturidi opens by affirming that Allah is Wise by essence, not by external cause. He then addresses a key question: Are acts like injustice and foolishness always ugly? He explains that in general they are, but the same act may be considered foolish in one situation and wise in another—except lying, which never changes. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and the logical progression.
2
Identify the key claims
Find the statements about Allah's intrinsic wisdom, the general rule that justice/wisdom are good and injustice/foolishness are ugly, and the exception of lying.
Lying is the only action that does not change moral status.
3
Reflect on human limitation
Consider Maturidi's argument that humans cannot fully know the consequences of actions to determine wisdom or foolishness absolutely.
Even harm can be beneficial as a lesson.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that divine acts are always wise in absolute sense, but when we examine individual acts in isolation, we cannot always assign wisdom or foolishness to them.
This preserves both divine perfection and contextual moral evaluation.

للنفع والمدح. لكن الله تعالى هو الحكيم من عنده دون أن الحاجة إلى علة خارجية، وهو الغنى عن جميع الحاجات، والعليم المحيط بكل شيئ. فالجهل والحاجة هما اللذان يبعدان المرء في العالم المحسوس عن القول الصواب والمعاملة الصحيحة؛ وهذا لا يمكن أن يكون موضوع البحث له سبحانه. ومما لا شك فيه أن الجور (الظلم) والسفه قبيحان، وأن العدل والحكمة حسنان في الجملة عند الماتريدي؛ لكن شيئًا واحدا قد يكون سفها في شخص أو في حال، وقد يكون حكمة في شخص أو في حال. ويعني ذلك أن كل شيء يمكن أن يكون في حال وفي ضده، ما عدا الكذب الذي لا يتغير من حال إلى حال على الإطلاق. فيلزم بهذا جهل كل من البشر لمعرفة نتائج حقيقة السفه والحكمة وأحوالهما في شيء ما بالتأمل فيه، مثل أن يعرف جميع الأسباب التي بها تتغير أحوال المحسوسات على الحواس. ولا يوجد ضرر ألبتة إلا وأمكن أن ينتفع به أحد. فهذا الانتفاع يكون إما عن طريق الدلالة إلى الحق، وإما أن يكون الانتفاع هذا عن طريق الموعظة وسيلة، أو ما يحتمل بعض العناصر فيه مثل تذكير النعمة وتحذير النقمة، أو يكون من تذكير الله تعالى الذي يملك قدرة الخلق وسلطة الأمر في العالم، وغير ذلك مما يكثر ذكره من الخصوصيات . فالمفهوم من ذلك كله بأننا كما نفهم بأن الأفعال الإلهية ليست ببعيدة عن الحكمة في معناها المطلق، فكذلك تثبت الحقيقة القائلة بأن الحقائق إذا انفردت على حدة لا يمكن دائما تثبيت الحكمة أو السفه في كل واحدة منها على حدة .
For benefit and praise. But Allah, the Exalted, is the Wise from Himself without need for an external cause, and He is the Independent of all needs, and the All-Knowing encompassing everything. Ignorance and need are what distance a person in the sensible world from correct speech and right action; and this cannot be a subject of research for Him, exalted. And there is no doubt that injustice (oppression) and foolishness are ugly, and that justice and wisdom are good in general according to al-Maturidi; but one thing may be foolish in a person or in a situation, and may be wisdom in a person or in a situation. And that means that everything can be in one state and its opposite, except lying which does not change from state to state at all. So it follows from this that all humans are ignorant of knowing the true results of foolishness and wisdom and their states in something by reflecting upon it, such as knowing all the causes by which the states of sensible things change upon the senses. Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's nuanced view on divine wisdom and moral values. He begins by affirming that Allah is Wise inherently, without external cause, and free from need and ignorance that cause human error. He then states that injustice and foolishness are universally ugly, and justice and wisdom are good in general, but a single action may be considered foolish in one context and wise in another, with the sole exception of lying, which is always ugly. Humans cannot fully know the consequences of what is wise or foolish; they must reflect on causes. Even harm can be beneficial as a lesson or reminder. The conclusion is that divine acts are always wise in an absolute sense, but when isolated realities are examined, wisdom or foolishness cannot be universally assigned—context matters.

The True Meaning of Divine Will
Maturidi distinguishes four meanings of irada and identifies the one truly applicable to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four meanings of irada (will) in Maturidi's theology and why only one is truly attributable to Allah.

Core Idea
Not all wills are alike—Allah's will is effective and unimpeded.

Imam al-Maturidi provides a crucial purification of the concept of will (irada) as applied to Allah. In human language, 'will' can mean wish, command, or approval, but none of these fit the Divine. By examining these meanings, Maturidi establishes the true nature of God's will, which is directly linked to His knowledge and encompasses all things.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Many people mistakenly apply human concepts of will to Allah. Maturidi begins by clarifying the term.
The word irada has multiple senses in Arabic.
2
Study the four meanings
List the four meanings Maturidi gives: wish, command/claim, approval/acceptance, and effective execution without constraint.
The first three are disqualified for various reasons.
3
Identify the true meaning
Focus on the fourth meaning: not being under another's control and carrying out the act without obstacle. This is the only one applicable to Allah.
This is the real irada.
4
Connect to knowledge
Maturidi links this will to the attribute of knowledge, showing that God's will pertains to everything without exception.
This refutes the idea that God's will is limited to good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الإرادة فقد أشار الماتريدي إلى أن هذا الاصطلاح يستعمل في أربعة معان. فالأول هو أن يُراد بالإرادة التمنى، فلا يجوز أن يوصف الله به على الإطلاق. والثاني أن يُراد بها الأمر والدعوى، والثالث أن تكون بمعنى الرضا والقبول فهذان المعنيان الأخيران لا يجوز نسبتهما إلى الله خصوصا في موضوعات تتعلق بالذم والرابع أن يراد بها عدم كونه تحت حكم الآخر وضغطه، وإجراء الفعل المراد دون مواجهة أي مانع أو سهو ؛ هذا هو معنى حقيقة الإرادة التي يوصف بها الله سبحانه. فالإرادة المقصودة بهذا المعنى قد ربطها الماتريدي بصفة العلم، فبين أنها بهذا الشكل تتعلق بكل شيء حسن أو قبيح.
As for the will (irada), al-Maturidi indicated that this term is used in four meanings. The first is that by 'will' is meant wish (tamanni), and it is not permissible to describe God by it at all. The second is that by it is meant command and claim; the third is that it is in the sense of approval and acceptance; these two last meanings are not permissible to attribute to God, especially in matters related to blame. And the fourth is that by it is meant not being under the rule or pressure of another, and carrying out the desired act without facing any obstacle or oversight; this is the meaning of the true will by which God, exalted, is described. So al-Maturidi linked the will meant by this meaning to the attribute of knowledge, and he explained that in this form it pertains to everything, good or evil.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage explains Maturidi's classification of the meanings of 'irada' (will). He lists four: (1) wish, not applicable to God; (2) command or claim; (3) approval or acceptance; these two cannot be attributed to God especially in blameworthy contexts; (4) the true meaning: absence of being under another's control and performance of the intended act without hindrance. This last meaning is how God's will is described, and Maturidi connects it to God's knowledge, affirming that it encompasses all things, good and evil.

Key Terms
إرادة
تمنى
رضا
قبول
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's will is like human wish or approval.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies it is the effective will that accomplishes the act without constraint.

Checkpoint

A) Wish (tamanni)
B) Command and claim
C) Approval and acceptance
D) Not being under another's rule and executing the act without obstacle

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that God's will is the unimpeded execution of His desire, encompassing all things, and is rooted in His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's will encompasses everything, good or evil, and is never obstructed, what does that mean for your own regrets? Can you truly say you wish something had happened differently, knowing it was from His effective will? How does this change your acceptance of past events you considered bad?
?
God's will is not like your wishful thinking. How often do you treat your supplications as mere wishes rather than requests to the One whose will actually accomplishes? What would change if you truly believed that every outcome is under His unimpeded will?
?
Maturidi links God's will to His knowledge. If God's will is based on His complete knowledge, what does that imply about your own limited understanding? Are there areas where you still think you know better than what has been decreed?

The Four Meanings of Will (Irada) in Maturidi's Thought
Maturidi classifies four meanings of irada and identifies which applies to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct meanings of the term 'irada' (will) according to Imam Maturidi and which one is properly attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's will is not wish or command, but sovereign execution free from all obstacles.

Maturidi provides a precise classification to prevent anthropomorphism. Before we can discuss divine will, we must distinguish it from human wishful thinking, commanding, or approving. This passage clarifies which meaning truly belongs to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let Maturidi's own classification come through.
The passage is concise, so read carefully.
2
Identify the four meanings
List the four meanings Maturidi gives for 'irada'. Notice how he introduces each one.
They are: tamanni, amr wa da'wa, rida wa qubul, and the fourth which is the true meaning.
3
Understand why three are rejected
For each of the first three meanings, ask yourself why they cannot apply to Allah. The commentary will help.
Especially note that the third meaning (rida wa qubul) is rejected only in blameworthy contexts.
4
Focus on the fourth meaning
The fourth meaning is the only one attributed to Allah. Understand its components: freedom from external control, execution without obstacle or oversight.
Maturidi ties this to the attribute of knowledge (ilm).
5
Reflect on the connection to knowledge
Maturidi says that will in this true sense relates to everything, good or evil. Consider why knowledge is connected to will.
This will be important for later discussions on divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الإرادة فقد أشار الماتريدي إلى أن هذا الاصطلاح يستمعل في أربعة معان. فالأول هو أن يُراد بالإرادة التمنى، فلا يجوز أن يوصف الله به على الإطلاق. والثاني أن يُراد بها الأمر والدعوى، والثالث أن تكون بمعنى الرضا والقبول فهذان المعنيان الأخيران لا يجوز نسبتهما إلى الله خصوصا في موضوعات تتعلق بالذم والرابع أن يراد بها عدم كونه تحت حكم الآخر وضغطه، وإجراء الفعل المراد دون مواجهة أي مانع أو سهو ؛ هذا هو معنى حقيقة الإرادة التي يوصف بها الله سبحانه. فالإرادة المقصودة بهذا المعنى قد ربطها الماتريدي بصفة العلم، فبين أنها بهذا الشكل تتعلق بكل شيء حسن أو قبيح.
As for will (al-irada), Maturidi indicated that this term is used in four meanings. The first is that will is intended as wish (al-tamanni), so it is not permissible to describe Allah with it at all. The second is that it is intended as command and claim (al-amr wa al-da'wa). The third is that it is in the sense of satisfaction and acceptance (al-rida wa al-qubul). These latter two meanings are not permissible to attribute to Allah, especially in matters pertaining to blame. The fourth is that it is intended as not being under the rule and pressure of another, and carrying out the desired action without encountering any obstacle or oversight; this is the meaning of the true will with which Allah, exalted is He, is described. So will intended in this sense Maturidi connected to the attribute of knowledge, explaining that in this form it pertains to everything, good or evil.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the term 'irada' (will) into four distinct meanings, identifying which is appropriate for Allah. The first meaning is wish (tamanni), which is impossible for Allah because it implies need and lack. The second is command and claim (amr wa da'wa), which is not attributed to Allah especially in blameworthy contexts. The third is satisfaction and acceptance (rida wa qubul), similarly not attributed. The fourth meaning is the freedom from external control and the execution of an act without hindrance or oversight. This fourth meaning is the true reality of will that is attributed to Allah. Maturidi then connects this will to the attribute of knowledge (ilm), stating that in this sense it pertains to everything, both good and evil, meaning that Allah's will encompasses all that exists.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التمني
الأمر والدعوى
الرضا والقبول
صفة العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's will is like human wishful thinking, i.e., a desire for something lacking.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the first meaning (tamanni) is impossible for Allah because it implies need. Allah's will is rather the sovereign and unimpeded execution of His actions, free from any external constraint or oversight.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the four meanings of irada is appropriate for Allah?
A) Wish (tamanni)
B) Command and claim (amr wa da'wa)
C) Satisfaction and acceptance (rida wa qubul)
D) Not being under others' control and executing action without obstacle

Key Takeaway
The only meaning of will that truly describes Allah is the power to execute actions without hindrance, and this will encompasses all things through His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you truly believe that nothing happens outside Allah's will? How does this awareness change your reaction to trials? Commit to one specific change in your attitude this week.
?
Reflecting on Maturidi's fourth meaning, where in your life do you still act as though your will is hindered by others or circumstances, rather than submitting to the reality that Allah's will alone prevails? What would it look like to live with that certainty?
?
If Allah's will encompasses both good and evil, how does that affect the way you interpret your own sins or the sins of others? Do you still feel the weight of personal accountability? Write down one area where you need to take responsibility rather than blame destiny.

Divine Attributes

Motion, Rest, Composition, Separation, Permanence, and Annihilation are Accidents
Maturidi proves that motion, rest, composition, separation, permanence, and annihilation are accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that six commonly observed states of bodies are accidents (a'rad) distinct from the body itself.

Core Idea
Changing states prove accidents – a body can alter while remaining itself.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle of kalam: observable changes in bodies indicate the existence of accidents that are distinct from the bodies' substance. He uses the simple examples of a body that moves and rests, or comes together and separates, to show that these states cannot be identical to the body. Then he extends the same reasoning to permanence and annihilation, demonstrating that even these are accidents that inhere in bodies. This lesson covers the core proof that these six attributes are accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Note how Maturidi states the conclusion first (these are other than the body) and then gives evidence.
The proof relies on the principle of contrary states.
2
Identify the proof pattern
Maturidi uses two similar proofs: one for motion/rest/composition/separation, and one for permanence/annihilation. Identify the structure: if X were the body itself, the body could not undergo contrary states. Since it can, X must be an accident.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Consider why Maturidi takes the time to prove these are accidents. In kalam, establishing that things are accidents (a'rad) is crucial for later arguments about the contingency of the world and the existence of God.
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم معلوم أن تكون الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والتفرق غير الجسم، إذ قد يكون الشيء جسما متفرّقا يجتمع ، ومتحركا يسكن; فلو كان لنفسه يكون كذلك لم يكن ليحتمل مُضادّات الأحوال على بقاء الجسم بحاله. وعلى هذا يُخرج الفناء والبقاء; إذ قد يوجد غير باق ولا فان في أوقات، فيلزم أن يكون غيره، وكذلك من أراد بقاء الشيء أو فناءه يقصد إلى غير الوجه الذي يقصد بالآخر، ثبت أنهما غيران يَحْلان. وكثير من المعتزلة يقولون بهذا في الأول، ويأبَوْنَ في البقاء والفناء. مع ما يبطل قولهم، إذ كان مختلفا بنفسه في احتمال ذلك، ولو جاز ذلك وجاز بقاء الأجسام بأنفسها لجاز كونها لا بغير. على أن هذا بالمعتزلة أولى، لأنهم لا يجعلون من الله إلى جملة العالم غير العالم، به كان العالم; إذ الإرادة يجعلونها من العالم والفعل كذلك، وذاته كان موجوداً عندهم ولم يكن العالم، ولم يحدث شيء سوى كون العالم، فاذا كان بنفسه وبه يبقى. وفي ذلك فساد التوحيد ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is known that motion and rest, composition and separation are other than the body, for a body may be separated and then come together, and moving and then still. If it were by itself, it would not admit contrary states while the body remains in its condition. And upon this, annihilation and permanence are derived, for something may exist not permanent and not annihilated at times, so it must be other than [the body]. Likewise, whoever intends the permanence or annihilation of a thing intends a different aspect from the other; so it is established that they are two different [accidents] that inhere. And many of the Mu'tazila affirm this concerning the first [i.e., motion, rest, etc.] but refuse [the same] for permanence and annihilation. There is also what invalidates their statement, because it is different by itself in bearing that. And if that were permissible and the permanence of bodies by themselves were permissible, then it would be permissible for them to be without another. But this is more incumbent on the Mu'tazila, because they do not posit anything from God to the whole world other than the world — through it the world came to be. For they posit that will is from the world, and action likewise, and His essence was existent with them while the world was not, and nothing occurred except the coming-to-be of the world. So if it [the world] is by itself and through it persists, then that leads to the corruption of tawhid, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage argues that motion, rest, composition, and separation are accidents (a'rad) distinct from the body. Proof: a body can be in contrary states (e.g., moving then still) while remaining the same body. If motion were the body itself, it could not change to rest without the body changing. Therefore, these are accidents. Similarly, permanence (baqa') and annihilation (fana') are accidents because something can be in a state of neither permanence nor annihilation at a given moment. Moreover, the intention to preserve or annihilate a thing targets different aspects, so they are distinct accidents. Maturidi then criticizes the Mu'tazila for inconsistently accepting that motion etc. are accidents but denying the same for permanence and annihilation. He argues that if bodies could persist by themselves without accidents, then they could exist without a Sustainer, which is absurd. This is even more problematic for the Mu'tazila because they deny that God's will and action are distinct from the world, effectively making the world self-subsisting, which destroys tawhid.

Key Terms
حركة (motion)
سكون (rest)
اجتماع (composition)
تفرق (separation)
بقاء (permanence)
فناء (annihilation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that motion or rest are intrinsic to the body, like its color or shape.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are accidents because the body can be in opposite states at different times while remaining the same body.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must motion and rest be accidents distinct from the body?
A) Because the Mu'tazila say so.
B) Because a body can be in contrary states while remaining the same.
C) Because motion and rest are invisible.
D) Because bodies cannot move without a mover.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that motion, rest, composition, separation, permanence, and annihilation are all accidents that inhere in bodies, not the bodies themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is constantly in motion or rest, yet you rarely pause to ask who causes these changes moment by moment. If motion is an accident needing a cause, who is causing your very movement right now? Will you acknowledge that cause with gratitude before you take your next step?
?
The fact that you exist from one moment to the next is not from yourself—permanence is an accident given by Allah. Do you live with that awareness, or do you go through your day assuming your own existence is self-evident? What would change if you truly felt your continued existence is a gift renewed every moment?
?
If even the state of 'being separated' or 'being together' is an accident that depends on Allah, then nothing about your physical state is outside His control. Is there an area of your life where you act as if your circumstances are self-made? Are you ready to hand over that illusion of control today?

Critique of Mu'tazila on Accidents and the Implications for Tawhid
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazila for inconsistency and shows their view destroys tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila's inconsistent acceptance of some accidents while rejecting others, and how their position leads to the corruption of tawhid.

Core Idea
Denying that permanence is an accident undermines God's role as Sustainer.

After proving that both the active states (motion, rest, composition, separation) and the passive states (permanence, annihilation) are accidents, Maturidi turns to a powerful critique of the Mu'tazila. He points out that many Mu'tazila accept the first set but deny that permanence and annihilation are accidents. He then shows the dangerous theological consequences: if bodies can persist by themselves without the accident of permanence, they would be independent of God, which destroys the very foundation of tawhid. This lesson unpacks that critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'وكثير من المعتزلة'. Notice how Maturidi first states their inconsistency, then refutes it, then draws the theological conclusion.
2
Identify the inconsistency
The Mu'tazila accept that motion etc. are accidents but deny the same for permanence and annihilation. Maturidi argues this is arbitrary because the same logical proof applies.
3
Follow the theological argument
If bodies persist by themselves, they would be independent of God. Maturidi then links this to the Mu'tazila's own doctrine about will and action, showing that their position leads to a self-subsisting world, which corrupts tawhid.
This is a strong refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And upon this, annihilation and permanence are derived, for something may exist not permanent and not annihilated at times, so it must be other than [the body]. Commentary
This passage argues that motion, rest, composition, and separation are accidents (a'rad) distinct from the body. Proof: a body can be in contrary states (e.g., moving then still) while remaining the same body. If motion were the body itself, it could not change to rest without the body changing. ?

Proof from Revelation that the Creator is One
Maturidi enumerates three proofs for divine oneness and elaborates on the proof from revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's use of revelation (al-sam‘) as a proof for the oneness of the Creator, including the semantic range of the term 'one' (wāḥid).

Core Idea
The word 'one' means both the first number and unique greatness—both point to tawḥīd.

Maturidi begins the section on tawḥīd by outlining three proofs: revelation, reason, and the testimony of creation. In this lesson, we explore the first proof—scriptural evidence—and how the very word 'one' (wāḥid) carries layers of meaning that support the doctrine of God’s absolute oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text is cut off mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the argument.
2
Identify the three proofs
Maturidi lists three indications: revelation, reason, and the testimony of creation. Find where he mentions them and note that he only elaborates on the first in this passage.
3
Focus on the revelation proof
The revelation proof is based on 'consensus of statement despite differences' (اتفاق القول على اختلافهم على الواحد). Ask yourself: how does this consensus support oneness?
Maturidi argues that even polytheists, when they say 'one', ultimately affirm the true God in some sense.
4
Understand the dual meaning of 'wāḥid'
Maturidi explains that 'one' (واحد) is not just a number; it also denotes greatness, dominion, and excellence. This is crucial for tawhid because it shows God's uniqueness is not merely numerical.
He gives the example of saying 'So-and-so is the unique one of the age'.
5
Follow the infinity argument
Maturidi begins an argument about numbers and infinity to show that if 'one' were only numerical, it would lead to absurdity. Although the passage cuts off, try to grasp the direction: if numbers are infinite, then limiting 'one' to number would make the world infinite, which is impossible.
This argument is incomplete in the source but points to a deeper rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة محدث العالم واحد. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: والدلالة على أن محدث العالم واحد لا أكثر السمع، والعقل، وشهادة العالم بالخلقة. أ فأما السمع فهو اتفاق القول على اختلافهم على الواحد، إذ من يقول بالأكثر يقول به على أن الواحد اسم لابتداء العدد، واسم للعظمة والسلطان والرفعة والفضل، كما يقال: فلان واحد الزمان ومنقطع القرين في الرفعة والفضل والجلال. وما جاوز ذلك لا يحتمل غير العدد، والأعداد لانهاية لها من حيث العدد؛ وفي تحقيق ما يعد يخرج عن النهاية بالعدد، فيجب أن يكون العالم غير متناه؛ إذ لو كان من كل منها شيء واحد،
Question: The maker of the world is one. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The indication that the maker of the world is one, not more, is (from) revelation, reason, and the testimony of the world by creation. As for revelation: it is the consensus of statement—despite their differences—upon the One, since whoever says (there are) more says it (only) insofar as 'one' is a name for the beginning of number, and a name for greatness, dominion, eminence, and excellence, as is said: 'So-and-so is the unique one of the age and peerless in eminence, excellence, and majesty.' And what goes beyond that does not admit anything except number, and numbers have no end in terms of number; and in the realization of what is counted, it emerges from infinity by number. So the world must be infinite, since if from each of them there were one thing,
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (مسألة) on the oneness of the creator. Maturidi states that there are three proofs: revelation (السمع), reason (العقل), and the testimony of the world through creation (شهادة العالم بالخلقة). He then elaborates on the proof from revelation. He notes that despite differences among people, there is a consensus (اتفاق القول) on the One, because those who claim multiple gods still ultimately affirm the one true God in some sense. He explains that the word 'واحد' (one) has two meanings: it is the first number, but it also signifies greatness, dominion, eminence, and excellence—as when someone is called 'the unique one of the age'. Beyond that, numbers are infinite. If the term 'one' were taken only numerically, then the world would have to be infinite (because numbers are infinite), but that leads to an absurdity: if from each number there were one thing, the world would be infinite. The text cuts off, but the argument appears to be that 'one' as applied to God does not mean a numerical one that could be followed by two, but rather a unique, incomparable one.

Key Terms
محدث العالم
السمع
اتفاق القول
واحد
ابتداء العدد
العظمة والسلطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'one' (واحد) only means the number one, so when applied to God it merely indicates that He is the first in a sequence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'واحد' also signifies greatness, dominion, and unique excellence, so tawhid means God is incomparable, not just numerically first.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the word 'واحد' (one) mean when applied to God?
A) Only the first number.
B) Greatness, dominion, eminence, and excellence.
C) Both the first number and greatness.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof from revelation shows that the term 'one' for God implies both numerical oneness and unique greatness, undermining any claim of multiple creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Ahad' in your prayers, do you truly ponder that 'Ahad' means more than just 'one'—it means He is uniquely incomparable? Are there areas of your life where you act as if there are other 'gods' besides Him, even if you say He is one? What is one specific change you will make today to reflect that He is truly 'Unique One of the age'?
?
Māturīdī says the word 'one' also means greatness, dominion, eminence, and excellence. Does your belief in God's oneness translate into awe and reverence in your daily actions? How? Will you remind yourself of His uniqueness before your next important decision?
?
The proof from revelation shows that even polytheists cannot escape using the word 'one' for God. Have you ever realized that the very language you use to describe God testifies to His oneness? How does that affect your trust in His revelation? Pledge to learn one more proof from the Qur'an about tawḥīd this week.
