6 min
Affirming Names and Attributes without Anthropomorphism
Maturidi defends affirming divine names and attributes while negating all similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that affirming divine names and attributes using human language does not imply any resemblance between God and creation.

Core Idea
Using human words for God does not imply resemblance; we negate likeness by adding 'not like the knowers'.

Maturidi addresses a central concern in theology: how can we speak about God using human language without falling into anthropomorphism? In this passage, he explains that the names are necessary for knowledge, but we always negate any similarity. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style of Maturidi's argumentation.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where Maturidi states that there is no resemblance (tashabuh) in affirming names and attributes. Understand why he says this is so.
3
Examine the method: knowing the unseen through the witnessed
Maturidi argues that we must use names from the witnessed world because there is no other way to know the unseen (gha'ib). Notice how he justifies this epistemological principle.
4
Understand the role of imagination (wahm)
Maturidi distinguishes between verbal resemblance and imagined resemblance. He explains that the similarity people imagine comes from comparing essences, not from the words themselves.
This is a key distinction to avoid confusion.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final point: if there were no names, there would be no imagined resemblance. Our use of names does not obligate resemblance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : وليس في إثبات الأسماء وتحقيق الصفات تشابه لنفي حقائق ما في الخلق عنه كالهستية والثبات ولكن الأسماء لما لم يُحتمل التعريف ولا تحقيق الذات بحق الربوبية إلا بذلك، إذ لا وجه لمعرفة غائب إلا بدلالة الشاهد، ثم إذا أريد الوصف بالعلو والجلال. فذلك طريق المعرفة في الشاهد وإمكانُ القول، إذ لا يحتمل وسعنا العرفان بالتسمية بغير الذي شاهدنا ، ولا الإشارة إلى ما لا نأخذ من الحس وحق العيان. لو احتمل وسعنا ذلك لقلنا ذلك، لكنا أردنا به ما يُسقط الشبه من قولنا : «عالم لا كالعلماء»، وهذا النوع في كل ما نسميه به ونصفه والله الموفق. مع ما كان التشابه الذي تُقدّره أوهامنا ليس عن قول اللسان تُقدّره، بل بما كنا نعرف الشبه بين الذاتين والفعلين، فإلى ذلك يرجع وهمنا عند التسمية؛ وذلك يُحققه لو لم يكن لهما اسم عُرِفا به ووصف وصفا به. فإذا كان الله سبحانه، فيما اعتقدنا وحدانيته، اعتقدناه غير شبيه بالمعروفين في تسمية الآحاد لم يلزمنا التسمية بما تعرَّفنا ما لولا الاسم لم يجب التشبيه بذلك الاسم . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: There is no resemblance (tashabuh) in affirming the names and verifying the attributes because of the negation of the realities of what is in creation from Him, like [the attributes of] 'istiyya (being?) and thubāt (stability). But rather, the names are [used] because it is not possible to [attain] definition nor the verification of the essence by the truth of lordship except through that, since there is no way to know the unseen except through the indication of the witnessed (shāhid). Then when one intends to describe Him with exaltation and majesty, that is the way of knowledge in the witnessed and the possibility of speech, since our capacity does not allow knowledge through naming with other than what we have witnessed, nor through pointing to what we do not take from sense and the truth of ocular vision. If that were possible within our capacity, we would say it, but we intend by it what negates the likeness from our saying 'Knowing, not like the knowers', and this type [applies] in everything we name Him with and describe Him. And God is the granter of success. Moreover, the resemblance that our imaginations (awhām) conceive is not conceived from the utterance of the tongue, but rather from what we know of resemblance between two essences and two acts; our imagination returns to that when naming. And that is verified by [the fact that] if they did not have a name by which they are known and a description by which they are described, then if God, exalted, in what we believe of His oneness, we believe Him not similar to the known ones in the naming of individuals, we are not obligated by the naming [that we have learned] because without the name, similarity would not be necessitated by that name. And there is no power save with God.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a defense of affirming names and attributes for God without falling into anthropomorphism (tashbih). Maturidi explains that the names and attributes we use are taken from the witnessed world, but we negate any likeness by adding qualifiers like 'not like the knowers'. The similarity that people imagine comes from the imagination (wahm) comparing essences and acts, not from the words themselves. Since we do not know God's essence directly, we must use the language of the witnessed while negating likeness.

Key Terms
إثبات الأسماء
تحقيق الصفات
تشابه
الشاهد
الغائب
وهم
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that using human words for God implies that God is similar to humans.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the words are used with a negation of likeness, as in 'Knowing, not like the knowers', and that imagined resemblance comes from comparing essences, not from the words themselves.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why do we use names derived from the witnessed world for God?
A) Because there is no other way to know the unseen.
B) Because God is exactly like the witnessed.
C) Because the names are arbitrary.
D) Because we have direct knowledge of God's essence.

Key Takeaway
Affirming divine names and attributes does not entail anthropomorphism because the names are used while negating any likeness between God and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allah knows all', do you ever unconsciously visualize a kind of 'knowing' similar to human knowledge? What specific words can you add in your heart right now to negate that likeness?
?
This lesson reveals that our imagination naturally compares God to creation. Can you identify one attribute of God (like mercy or power) where your mind secretly pictures a created version? Will you commit today to actively say '...not like the merciful' when thinking of His mercy?
?
Maturidi says resemblance comes from our habit of comparing essences and acts. What mental habit in your own life needs to be broken so that your thoughts of God become purer? Write a concrete step you will take this week to train your mind toward tanzih.

Allah's Eternal Attributes: Knowing, Powerful, Acting, Generous
Maturidi affirms Allah's eternal attributes and gives a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Maturidi affirms that Allah has always been knowing, powerful, acting, and generous, and how he uses an analogy from the created world to support this.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternal so that everything comes to be at its appointed time.

In this passage from Kitab al-Tawhid, Imam al-Maturidi lays out a foundational creedal statement: Allah's essential attributes of knowledge, power, action, and generosity are eternal. He does not merely state them; he gives a rational purpose and supports it with an analogy from human experience. This lesson will guide you through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the full creedal statement. Notice the five attributes: knowing, powerful, acting, generous, and the condition 'in ways valid in the intellect and with which governance stands.'
The phrase 'لم يزل' indicates eternal pre-existence.
2
Understand the purpose of eternity
Maturidi says these attributes are eternal 'so that by His act everything comes to be at its time.' Reflect on why pre-existent attributes are necessary for creation.
This removes the notion that Allah could be independent of creation ('غناء عن التكوين').
3
Analyze the analogy from the witnessed world
Maturidi argues from human knowledge and will: we know and will things before they exist, and they come to be through that. Similarly, divine attributes must pre-exist. Consider how this analogy strengthens the claim.
The analogy is from the shahid (witnessed) to the ghayb (unseen).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
{ قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ونحن نقول بأنه عز وجل لم يزل عالما قادرا فاعلا جَوَادًا على الوجوه التي تصح في العقل ويقوم معها التدبير . إنه لم يزل كذلك ليكون بفعله كل شيء يكون في وقت كونه بوجه يصح عنه رفع الوصف بالغناء عن التكوين والامتناع عن وقوع القدرة عليه، والغناء بنفسه في الوجود عن الباري. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وذلك معلوم في الشاهد في العلم والإرادة بأشياء ليست بكائنة لتكون إلا بهما، فمثله عندنا القدرة والإرادة والجود وما ذُكِر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And we say that He, Almighty and Majestic, has never ceased to be knowing, powerful, acting, and generous in the ways that are valid in the intellect and with which governance stands. He has never ceased to be such, so that by His act everything that comes to be at its time comes to be in a way that removes the description of being independent of creation and the prevention of power from occurring upon it, and independence of the Creator in existence. And there is no power except with Allah. And that is known in the witnessed world: knowledge and will regarding things that are not yet existent, so that they come to be only through them. So similarly according to us are power, will, generosity, and what has been mentioned. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a creedal statement by Imam al-Maturidi about Allah's eternal attributes. He affirms that Allah has always been (lam yazal) knowing, powerful, acting, and generous in rationally valid ways that are necessary for governance (tadbir). These attributes are eternal so that by His act every thing comes to be at its appointed time. This removes the notion that He is independent of creation (ghina' 'an al-takwin) or that power can be withheld. Maturidi supports this by analogy from the witnessed world (shahid): knowledge and will exist regarding future things that are not yet actual, and those things come to be only through that knowledge and will. Similarly, for us, power, will, and generosity are attributes that enable action.

Key Terms
لم يزل
عالم
قادر
فاعل
جواد
تكوين
تدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's attributes are separate entities from His essence.
Correction: Maturidi sees them as eternal and identical to His essence; they are not separate but are essential attributes that have always been.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the purpose of Allah's eternal attributes?
A) To show His independence from creation.
B) To enable everything to come to be at its time.
C) To be known by humans.
D) To create the world in one moment.

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes of knowledge, power, action, and generosity are eternal and necessary for His creation, as evidenced by analogy from human knowledge and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's knowledge is eternal and includes every action you will ever take, does that make you more cautious or more careless about your sins? How should it change your attitude toward repentance today?
?
The passage says Allah's eternal attributes exist 'so that everything comes to be at its time.' Where are you struggling to trust Allah's timing in your life? Will you surrender that area to Him now, or continue demanding your own schedule?
?
Maturidi uses the analogy of human will and knowledge: you know and will an action before doing it. Does your own experience of planning and acting prove that your will is real and effective? Then how much more certain should you be of Allah's will? Do you live with that certainty, or do you act as if His will might fail?

Divine Attributes
beginner

God's Eternal Attributes: Knowing, Powerful, Acting, Generous
Maturidi asserts God's eternal attributes and explains their necessity for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi rationally defends the eternity of God's attributes (knowing, powerful, acting, generous) and the necessity of these attributes for creation.

Core Idea
God's eternal attributes are the rational basis for creation.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a core tenet of Maturidi theology: the Creator's attributes are eternal. Maturidi not only lists these attributes but also offers a rational argument, drawing an analogy from the human experience of knowledge and will. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding God's relationship to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لم يزل' (has not ceased), emphasizing eternity.
2
Identify the four attributes and their role
The passage lists four attributes: knowing, powerful, acting, generous. Note how Maturidi ties them to 'governance' (التدبير) and the act of bringing things into existence (التكوين).
3
Understand the analogy from the witnessed world
Maturidi draws an analogy: just as human knowledge and will are directed at non-existent things to bring them into existence, so too God's power, will, and generosity are eternal and necessary for creation.
This is a key Maturidi argument: using the witnessed as evidence for the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ونحن نقول بأنه عز وجل لم يزل عالما قادرا فاعلا جَوَادًا على الوجوه التي تصح في العقل ويقوم معها التدبير . إنه لم يزل كذلك ليكون بفعله كل شيء يكون في وقت كونه بوجه يصح عنه رفع الوصف بالغناء عن التكوين والامتناع عن وقوع القدرة عليه، والغناء بنفسه في الوجود عن الباري. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وذلك معلوم في الشاهد في العلم والإرادة بأشياء ليست بكائنة لتكون إلا بهما، فمثله عندنا القدرة والإرادة والجود وما ذُكِر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And we say that He, mighty and majestic, has not ceased to be knowing, powerful, acting, generous, in the ways that are sound in the intellect and by which governance is established. He has not ceased to be thus so that everything that comes to be in its time comes to be by His act, in a manner by which the description of independence from bringing into existence is removed, and prevention of power over it is prevented, and independence in existence from the Creator is prevented. And there is no power except by Allah. And that is known in the witnessed realm regarding knowledge and will for things that are not yet existent to become existent only by them, so similarly with us are power, will, generosity, and what was mentioned. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a positive assertion of God's eternal attributes. Maturidi states that God has always been (لم يزل) knowing, powerful, acting, and generous in ways that are rational and support governance (التدبير). He is eternally such that everything that occurs at its proper time occurs by His act, and this description removes any notion that God is independent of the act of bringing into existence (التكوين) or that power cannot apply to it. Then he draws an analogy from the witnessed world: we know that knowledge and will exist in us for things that are not yet existent so that they become existent through them. Similarly, for God, power, will, generosity, and the like are eternal attributes that are the basis for creation. This is a typical Maturidi argument using the witnessed as evidence for the unseen.

Key Terms
عالم
قادر
فاعل
جواد
التدبير
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's attributes are accidental or temporal, created in time.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that God's attributes are eternal (لم يزل) and not subject to temporal change. He uses the analogy from the witnessed world to argue that attributes like knowledge and will are directed toward non-existent things, implying their priority and eternality.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to support the eternity of God's attributes?
A) The analogy of a natural cause
B) The analogy of knowledge and will in the witnessed world for things that are not yet existent
C) The analogy of light and darkness
D) The analogy of an old man's age

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that God's attributes are eternal and rational, using the analogy of witnessed knowledge and will to explain their necessity for creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you see God's eternal knowledge, power, and generosity behind every detail? Or do you ever live as if the universe runs on its own? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that God's attributes are the very reason everything exists right now?
?
Maturidi says God's attributes are rational and sound. Have you ever felt that God's commands seem arbitrary or unfair? How does knowing that His attributes are the rational basis for creation challenge that feeling? What step can you take today to submit more fully to His wisdom?
?
If God's generosity is eternal and necessary for creation, then every breath you take is a gift from His sheer bounty—not because you deserve it. Does that reality humble you, or do you take it for granted? What will you do today to respond to His generosity with gratitude?

How Can a Wise God Do Foolish Acts? Maturidi's Answer
Maturidi responds to the dualist objection by defining wisdom as correct placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of wisdom as 'hitting the mark' and how he refutes the dualist objection that a wise God cannot perform seemingly foolish acts.

Core Idea
Wisdom is putting everything in its proper place, even when human minds cannot see it.

The dualists challenged monotheists: how can a wise God commit foolish acts? Maturidi responds by redefining wisdom itself. He argues that true wisdom is not about avoiding what humans perceive as foolish, but about placing everything exactly where it belongs. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows how it answers the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi first denies that foolishness can come from one wise by essence.
The dualist's question is presented in direct speech.
2
Identify the definition of wisdom
Look for the phrase 'وما الحكمة إلا الإصابة' and see how Maturidi expands it into three components: placing everything, giving each portion, not depriving rights.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Understand the root of the objection
Maturidi explains that the dualists' rejection is due to ignorance of the limits of wisdom and the true portions of things.
This clarifies why the objection arises.
4
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect on how this definition of wisdom relates to God's attribute of being Al-Hakim (the Wise). How does it differ from human concepts of wisdom?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائلهم : كيف زعمتم أنه يجوز أن يكون من الحكيم يجيئ فعل السفه ؟ قلنا : هذا لا يجيئ ممن هو حكيم بذاته، إنما يجيئ ممن يجهل كما قلتم في النور من الجهل بعمل الظلمة ونحو ذلك . فأما الله سبحانه يتعالى عن ذلك . لكن قد يجوز أن يكون فَعَل حكمة لا يبلغها عقل البشر، وإلا فهو يَجِلَّ عن ذلك. وما الحكمة إلا الإصابة : أن يُوضع كلُّ شيء موضعه، ويُعْطَى كلَّ ذي حظ حظه، ولا يبخس بأحد حقه. وإنما أبى من يظن بالله أو لا يعلم بما يضيف إليه الموحدون ذلك، لجهلهم بحدود الحكمة ومبلغ الحظوظ، وإيجابهم الحقوق لمن ليست لهم. وسنذكره إن شاء الله في موضع هو أملك به من هذا.
Then if their questioner says: How do you claim that it is possible that an act of foolishness comes from the Wise? We say: This does not come from one who is wise by His essence; it only comes from one who is ignorant, as you said about light from ignorance of the work of darkness and the like. As for Allah, the Exalted, He is far above that. But it is possible that He did an act of wisdom that the intellect of humans does not reach; otherwise, He is too exalted for that. And wisdom is nothing but hitting the mark: that everything be placed in its proper place, and every possessor of a portion be given his portion, and no one be deprived of his right. And the only one who refuses (to accept) is he who thinks about Allah or does not know what the monotheists attribute to Him, due to their ignorance of the limits of wisdom and the extent of portions, and their imposing rights upon those who do not have them. And we will mention it, if Allah wills, in a place more fitting for it than this.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The dualist asks: How can you (monotheists) claim that a wise God can perform foolish acts? Maturidi answers: An act of foolishness does not come from one who is wise by essence; it only comes from ignorance. However, God is transcendent above that. But it is possible that God did an act of wisdom that human intellects cannot grasp; otherwise, God is too exalted to do foolishness. Then Maturidi defines wisdom as 'الإصابة' (hitting the mark): putting everything in its proper place, giving each its due, and not depriving anyone of their right. Thus, what seems foolish to humans may be perfect wisdom from God. The refusal of the dualists to accept this stems from their ignorance of the boundaries of wisdom and the proper portions of things, and from their asserting rights for those who do not have them (e.g., claiming darkness has a right to exist separately). Maturidi defers a fuller discussion to a more appropriate place.

Key Terms
الحكيم (the Wise)
فعل السفه (act of foolishness)
الحكمة (wisdom)
الإصابة (hitting the mark)
عقل البشر (human intellect)
الحظوظ (portions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can do foolish acts or that what appears foolish to humans indicates a flaw in God.
Correction: Maturidi explains that true wisdom is placing everything correctly; what seems foolish may be an act of wisdom beyond human grasp, and God is far above actual foolishness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the definition of wisdom?
A) Knowledge of all things.
B) Hitting the mark: placing everything in its proper place and giving each its due.
C) The ability to do anything.
D) Avoiding any act that seems foolish.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defines wisdom as the perfect placement of all things, which may appear foolish to humans but is ultimately wise and just.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that a trial or hardship in your life was a 'foolish mistake' from God? How does Maturidi's definition of wisdom as 'placing everything in its proper place' challenge that belief? What would it take for you to surrender that area to His unseen wisdom today?
?
Which 'right' are you demanding from God that He has not promised you? Reflect on the phrase 'imposing rights upon those who do not have them' – is there a situation where you are acting like the dualists, insisting on your own expectations? What would it look like to release that demand?
?
You claim to trust that God is Wise, but do you truly accept that you may never understand His placement of events in your life? Could you thank Him for something He has done that seems foolish to you, simply because He is wise by essence? If not, what is holding you back?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Two Answers to the Objection from Common Terms
Maturidi refutes the objection that since 'fāʿil' and 'ʿālim' are used for Allah despite being used for bodies, 'jism' should also be used.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why terms like 'agent' and 'knower' can be applied to Allah while 'body' cannot, through the two answers given by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Terms that do not imply createdness may be applied to Allah; those that do, cannot.

When opponents argue that since we use 'agent' and 'knower' for both creation and Allah, we should also use 'body', they commit a category error. Imam Maturidi provides two clear responses that show why this analogy fails. Let us examine them directly from his text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation. Notice that Maturidi presents two distinct answers, each introduced by 'one of them' and 'the second'.
The first answer focuses on revelation, the second on the meaning of the terms.
2
Understand the First Answer
The first answer says that if we did not understand a term's meaning, we could still use it if revelation does. But revelation never used 'body' for Allah, so it is not permitted.
This answer prioritizes scriptural authority over rational analogy when meaning is unclear.
3
Understand the Second Answer
The second answer says that the meanings of 'agent' and 'knower' are understood from creation but do not imply temporality. Therefore they can be applied to Allah while negating resemblance. 'Body' implies limits and parts, which are signs of creation.
This is the more fundamental answer, based on the meaning of the terms themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عورضنا باسم الفاعل أو العالم ونحو ذلك؛ قيل: له جوابان; أحدهما أنا لو لم نعقل معنى هذا لكان يجوز التسمية به بما ثبت في السمع، ولم يثبت في الأول، لذلك اختلفا. والثاني أن معنى الفاعل والعالم كان معقولا في الشاهد، وليس ذلك من أدلة الحدث، ولا مما في المعروف من معناه دليله، وقد احتمل وصف الله به; لذلك لزم القول به على نفي الشبه عنه. وبالله التوفيق.
If we are objected to by the name 'agent' (al-fāʿil) or 'knower' (al-ʿālim) and the like, it is said: it has two answers: one of them is that if we did not understand the meaning of this, it would be permissible to name by it according to what is established in hearing (al-samʿ), and it is not established in the first [i.e., in the case of 'body'], therefore they differ. The second is that the meaning of 'agent' and 'knower' is understood in the witnessed world, and that is not among the proofs of temporality, nor does its meaning in the known give a proof of it, and describing Allah by it is possible; therefore asserting it is necessary while negating resemblance to creation. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues: since terms like 'fāʿil' (agent) and 'ʿālim' (knower) are used for bodies in the witnessed world but also applied to Allah, why not also apply 'jism' (body) to Allah? Maturidi gives two answers. First, if we did not understand the meaning of these terms, we would still use them if revelation established them, but revelation did not establish 'jism' for Allah. Second, the meanings of 'fāʿil' and 'ʿālim' are intelligible from the witnessed world and do not imply temporality; they can be applied to Allah while negating any resemblance to created agents and knowers. In contrast, 'jism' inherently implies parts and limits, which are signs of createdness.

Key Terms
فاعل (fāʿil)
عالم (ʿālim)
معقول (maʿqūl)
شاهد (shāhid)
حدث (ḥadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All terms used for creation are equally applicable to Allah.
Correction: Only those terms whose meanings do not imply createdness (temporality, parts, limits) can be applied to Allah, and even then while negating resemblance.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason Maturidi gives for why 'fāʿil' and 'ʿālim' are applicable to Allah but 'jism' is not?
A) Because 'fāʿil' and 'ʿālim' are not used for bodies in the witnessed world
B) Because their meanings are understood from the witnessed world and do not imply temporality
C) Because 'jism' is not an attribute
D) Because revelation only uses 'fāʿil' and 'ʿālim'

Key Takeaway
Terms applied to Allah must not imply createdness; 'agent' and 'knower' meet this condition, but 'body' does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing', do you truly feel that His knowledge is beyond your comprehension, or do you subconsciously limit it to your own understanding of knowledge? How can you train your heart to purify that belief?
?
You often use the name 'Creator' for Allah, but have you ever caught yourself picturing a 'bigger version of human creation'? What does Maturidi's distinction between terms that imply createdness and those that do not teach you about the danger of such imaginations?
?
Maturidi's second answer shows that a term can be used if its meaning does not entail createdness. Reflect: In your daily life, when you talk about Allah's attributes, do you ever drift into affirming meanings that actually imply similarity to creation? What specific step will you take today to guard your tongue and heart from that?

Refuting the Claim That Agency Implies Corporeality
Maturidi refutes the claim that being agent or powerful entails being a body, and explains the meaning of 'jism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that Allah being an agent implies He is a body, and why the term 'jism' cannot be attributed to Allah without implying composition and other physical attributes.

Core Idea
Being called 'agent' does not make Allah a body; corporeality is not required for action.

Maturidi here responds to an objection from those who argue that terms like 'agent' and 'powerful' in the witnessed world always describe bodies, so Allah must be a body. He shows that this conclusion does not follow, because the terms are applied based on meaning, not corporeality, and because 'jism' itself implies parts and composition which are impossible for Allah. This passage is crucial for understanding the principle of tanzīh (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the opening objection: 'Why do you not say that by what He is named agent He is a body...' Then read Maturidi's immediate answer: 'He is not named by that because he is a body...'
Notice that the objector's argument is based on analogy with the witnessed world.
2
Analyze Maturidi's first two arguments
Maturidi gives two quick refutations: (1) Not every body is called agent/powerful/knower, so the link is not necessary. (2) The terms are justified by revelation and consideration, not by corporeality.
This shows that the objector's premise does not hold universally.
3
Understand the third argument against the objection
Maturidi argues that if this reasoning were allowed, opponents could use it to ascribe other anthropomorphic terms like 'jasad' or 'shakhṣ'. This shows the argument is not valid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: accepting the logic leads to unacceptable conclusions.
4
Examine the meaning of 'jism'
Maturidi explains that in the witnessed world, 'jism' applies to things that admit division and are composed of parts (length, width, composition). It does not apply to accidents, actions, etc.
Thus 'jism' implies physical composition, which cannot be attributed to Allah.
5
Reflect on the final consequence
If we attribute 'jism' to Allah, it would lead to attributing length, color, taste, etc., which are impossible. Similarly, accepting the objection would invalidate the belief in an eternal existent in itself.
Maturidi concludes that the claim that being an agent implies being a body is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا قلت بأنه بما سُمّي به فاعلا كان جسما، وكذلك القادر والعالم؛ إذ لا أحد في الشاهد سُمّي به إلا وهو جسم؟ قيل: لا سُمّي بذلك في الشاهد لأنه جسم، لوجودنا أجسامًا لا تسمّى به؛ فلذلك لم يلزم به القول. على أنا بينا الوجوه التي أحقت التسمية بما سُمّي به: من السمع والعبرة، ولسنا نجد ذلك في الذي عارض به. ولو جاز ذا ليجوز للآخر أيضا أن يقابلنا بمثله في الجسد والشخص ونحو ذلك. مع ما كان اسم الجسم غير واقع في الشاهد على ما لا يحتمل التجزئة والتبعيض من نحو العَرَض والفعل والحركة والسكون، ثبت أنه اسم ذي أجزاء كالطول والعرض واسم المؤلَّفَ. ولو لم يبطل القول بالمؤلّف في حق الله لما يدل ظاهره على فعل به؛ إذ لو بطل هذا الحكم ليبطل القول بموجود بذاته في الأزل. ولو كان كذلك ليجوز القول فيه تعالى بطول وجسد ولون وطعم ونحو ذلك؛ لما ليس في الظاهر إلا ذلك، فإذ لم يجز - لما في الحقيقة إيجابه وإن لم يكن في اللفظ دليله - فمثله في الجسم. والله الموفق.
If it is said: Why do you not say that by what He is named 'agent' He is a body, and likewise 'powerful' and 'knower', since no one in the witnessed world is named by that except he is a body? It is said: He is not named by that in the witnessed world because he is a body, because we find bodies that are not named by that; therefore asserting it is not necessary. Moreover, we have shown the aspects that justify the naming by what He is named: from hearing and consideration, and we do not find that in what he objects with. And if this were permissible, it would also be permissible for another to oppose us with something similar like 'jasad' and 'shakhṣ' and the like. Along with the fact that the name 'body' is not found in the witnessed world for what does not admit division and partitioning of the sort of accident, action, motion, and stillness; it is established that it is a name of something with parts like length and width and the name 'composite' (al-mu'allaf). And if the assertion of 'composite' in the case of Allah were not invalidated, its outward meaning would not indicate an action upon Him; for if this ruling were invalidated, then the assertion of an eternal existent in itself would be invalidated. And if it were so, then it would be permissible to assert of Him, exalted, length, body, color, taste, and the like, since the outward [meaning] is nothing but that. So since it is not permissible—because in reality it necessitates it even if the word does not contain an explicit proof—so likewise for 'body'. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objector claims that since Allah is called 'fāʿil' (agent), 'qādir' (powerful), and 'ʿālim' (knower), and in the witnessed world only bodies are called these, then Allah must be a body. Maturidi responds: First, not every body is called 'fāʿil' etc.; these terms are not applied because of corporeality but due to specific meanings. Second, these terms are justified by revelation and analogy, unlike 'jism'. Third, if this argument were allowed, it could be used to insist on other anthropomorphic terms. Then Maturidi elaborates on the meaning of 'jism': in the witnessed world, 'jism' applies to what admits division and is composed of parts (length, width, composition). To apply 'jism' to Allah would imply composition, which leads to further physical properties like length, color, etc., which are impossible for the eternal. Therefore, the argument that being an agent entails being a body is invalid.

قادر (qādir)
عالم (ʿālim)

مؤلَّف (mu'allaf)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a term like 'agent' is commonly applied to bodies, it cannot be applied to Allah without implying corporeality.
Correction: Maturidi shows that terms are applied based on meaning and justification (revelation/reason), not because of the physical properties of their typical referents. Additionally, 'jism' itself entails parts and composition, which cannot apply to Allah.

B) It implies division into parts and composition
C) It is synonymous with 'shay'
D) It only applies to living beings

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that applying terms like 'agent' to Allah does not imply corporeality, because such terms are defined by their meanings and justification, not by the physical properties of their typical referents, and because 'jism' itself entails composition incompatible with divinity.

?
Maturidi argues that applying terms like 'body' to Allah would lead to absurdities like length and color. Have you ever considered that your mental image of Allah, even if vague, might contain traces of form or shape? What concrete steps can you take to purify your tawhid?
?
The objector in the passage insists that agency must imply being a body because that's all we see. Do you ever judge Allah's actions by human standards? What does this lesson teach you about trusting divine revelation over sensory experience?

Why Not 'Body Not Like Bodies'? Maturidi's Refutation
Maturidi answers an objection about calling God a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects calling God a 'body not like bodies' even though He is called 'a thing not like things'.

Core Idea
Affirming God as 'thing' does not entail corporeality or require calling Him 'body'.

In this passage, Maturidi responds to a hypothetical objector who questions why the same logic that leads to saying 'a thing not like things' does not also lead to saying 'a body not like bodies'. The answer reveals a subtle point about theological language: not every term that can be negated can be affirmed, and the basis for one description may not apply to another. This lesson unpacks Maturidi's layered reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines carefully. An objector asks: 'Since you say God is a thing not like things, why don't you say He is a body not like bodies?' Notice the attempt to draw an analogy between 'thing' and 'body'.
The objector assumes that if 'thing' is permissible, then 'body' should also be permissible.
2
Examine Maturidi's first reply
Maturidi argues that the cause (sabab) that compelled them to call God a 'thing' does not exist for calling Him a 'body'. He points out that most things—such as accidents and attributes—are not bodies, so calling God a 'thing' does not imply corporeality.
This is a key point: the general term 'thing' has a broader application.
3
Analyze the deeper argument
Maturidi then addresses the phrase 'not like things'. He says it is a negation (nafy), not an affirmation of quiddity (māhiyyah). Thus, it does not provide a basis for demanding 'body not like bodies'. He also gives an analogy: why not say 'human not like people'?.
The objector misunderstands the function of negation.
4
See the self-contradiction
Finally, Maturidi shows that the objection is self-defeating: if you say 'body', then 'thing not like things' would become 'thing not like some things' (since body is a division of things), which invalidates the statement 'body not like bodies'.
The logic of the objector collapses under its own weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[إطلاق لفظ «الشيء» على الله ]
فإن قيل : إذ قلتم : شيء لا كالأشياء» ، لمَ لا قلتم: «جسم لا كالأجسام»؟ قيل له : لأن السبب الذي ألزَمَنا القول بالشيء لم يوجد في الجسم، لذلك لم نقل. وبعد، فإنه لا يخلو فيما يريد إلزامنا [ من القول بالجسمية ] من أن يُلزمنا بقولنا بالشيء فوجدنا أكثر الأشياء - وهي الأعراض والصفات من غير لزوم القول فيها بالجسمية - يمنع ذلك. وإن كان يريد [إلزامنا] بقولنا : « لا كالأشياء فليس هو حرف الإثبات ليدل على مائية المثبت، فلا لهذا السؤال. وهو كمن يقول : إذ جاز أن يكون شيئًا لا كالأشياء لمَ لا جاز وجه أن يكون إنسانًا لا كالناس؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فجواب مثله أن يقال: لأنه ليس بجسم فيقال: كالأجسام ، وليس هذا النوع بمعارضة إنما هو مُحاكمة؛ ونحن لا نملك إيجاد [الصفات] للإله حتى نقابل بمثل هذا فيقال لنا: إذ جعلتم ذالِمَ لا جعلتم ذا، بل يتعالى عن الجعل على جهة ، بل يوصف بما هو عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
ثم المعارضة عند التحصيل يتناقض ؛ لأنه قال : إذ قلتم : شيء لا كالأشياء» لم لا قلتم : جسم لا كالأجسام ؟ فإذا قلنا : جسم»، يصير قولنا : «شيء لا كالأشياء» [هو] «شيء لا كبعض الأشياء» ؛ إذ الجسم أحد قسمي الأشياء، وفي ذلك بطلان القول بجسم لا كالأجسام. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If it is said: When you say 'a thing not like things', why do you not say 'a body not like bodies'? It is said to him: Because the cause that compelled us to say 'thing' does not exist in body, therefore we do not say it. Moreover, it is not free from what he intends to compel us [to say about corporeality] from compelling us by our saying 'thing' – for we find most things – which are accidents and attributes without the necessity of saying corporeality – prevent that. And if he intends [to compel us] by our saying 'not like things', it is not an affirmative particle to indicate the quiddity of what is affirmed, so this question has no basis. It is like one who says: When it is permissible for Him to be a thing not like things, why is it not permissible for Him to be a human not like people? The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: The answer to such a person is that it is said to him: Because He is not a body, so that it could be said 'like bodies'. And this type is not an objection; it is a judgment. We do not have the power to create [attributes] for God such that we could be confronted with something like this and be told: Since you made Him possessing faculty, why did you not make Him possessing? Rather, He is exalted above being made in any manner, but is described by what He is. There is no power except with God. Then the opposition upon investigation is contradictory: because he said: When you say 'a thing not like things', why do you not say 'a body not like bodies'? For if we say 'body', our saying 'a thing not like things' becomes 'a thing not like some things', since the body is one of the two divisions of things, and in that is the invalidation of the statement 'a body not like bodies'. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector asks: Since you say that God is a 'thing not like things', why don't you also say He is a 'body not like bodies'? Maturidi's first answer is that the reason for calling God a 'thing' does not apply to 'body'. Then he elaborates: most things are accidents and attributes, which are not bodies, so calling God a 'thing' does not entail corporeality. Furthermore, the phrase 'not like things' is a negation, not an affirmation of quiddity, so it does not support the objection. He then gives an analogy: it is like asking why God is not called a human not like people. The Shaykh (Maturidi) adds that the real issue is that God is not a body, so the analogy fails. This is not a proper objection but a prejudgment. Finally, he shows that the objection is self-contradictory because if you said 'body', then 'thing not like things' would become 'thing not like some things', which defeats the purpose. The passage ends by affirming that God is described by what He actually is, not by analogy.

جسم
تشبيه
مائية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that calling God 'thing' implies corporeality or that 'body not like bodies' is a permissible description.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'thing' is a broader category that does not entail body, and the objection is based on a false analogy and misunderstanding of negation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not necessary to say 'body not like bodies' when you say 'thing not like things'?

B) Because the cause for saying 'thing' does not exist for 'body'.
C) Because 'body' is not a thing.
D) Because the Qur'an forbids it.

Key Takeaway
God is described by what He is, not by analogy with created things, and the term 'thing' does not imply corporeality.

The Meaning of 'Thing Not Like Things'
Maturidi explains the meaning of 'thing not like things' as negating the quiddity of things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the expression 'thing not like things' negates the quiddity (what-ness) of both entities and attributes, affirming God's incomparability.

Core Idea
God is a 'thing' but unlike all other things in essence and attributes.

Maturidi now refines the meaning of the phrase 'a thing not like things'. He explains that this negation removes the quiddity of all created things, which fall into two categories. Understanding this removal is key to avoiding both anthropomorphism and divestment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice Maturidi's concise style and how he defines 'not like things' as negation of quiddity.
2
Identify the two categories
Maturidi says things are two types: entity (ʿayn) which is body, and attribute (ṣifa) which is accident. Locate these in the text.
These are the only two categories of created things.
3
Understand the analogy with tashbīh and taʿṭīl
Maturidi compares the removal of 'body' from entities to the removal of likeness from affirmation and negation. Understand how both removals protect correct tawhid.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask: Why is it important to negate both body and accident from God? How does this prevent anthropomorphism and excessive abstraction?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم معنى قولنا : شيء لا كالأشياء» هو إسقاط مائية الأشياء . وهي نوعان: عين وهو جسم وصفة وهي العرض ، فيجب به إسقاط مائية الأعيان وهي الجسم، والصفات وهي الأعراض، فإذا أزَلْنا ذلك المعنى الذي هو جسم من الأعيان أبطلنا الاسم الذي هو لذلك المعنى، كما إذا أزلنا / معنى التشبيه من الإثبات ونفي التعطيل أبطلنا القول به . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the meaning of our saying 'a thing not like things' is the negation of the 'what-ness' of things. And they are two types: an entity (ʿayn) which is a body (jism), and an attribute (ṣifa) which is an accident (ʿaraḍ). So it necessarily implies the negation of the 'what-ness' of entities, which is body, and of attributes, which are accidents. So when we remove that meaning which is body from entities, we nullify the name that is for that meaning, just as when we remove the meaning of likeness (tashbīh) from affirmation and negation of taʿṭīl, we nullify the saying of it. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of the expression 'a thing not like things'. Maturidi states that it means the removal (isqāṭ) of the 'what-ness' (māhiyya) of things. He then explains that things are of two types: (1) an entity (ʿayn), which is a body (jism), and (2) an attribute (ṣifa), which is an accident (ʿaraḍ). Therefore, when we say 'not like things', we are negating both the quiddity of bodies and the quiddity of accidents. If we were to allow 'body' as a description, that would contradict the negation. He draws an analogy: just as we negate tashbīh (likening God to creation) from both affirmation of attributes and negation of taʿṭīl (divesting God of attributes), we similarly nullify the term 'body'. The passage ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

مائية
عين
جسم
صفة
عرض
تشبيه
تعطيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'thing' only refers to physical objects, but Maturidi includes both entities and attributes.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that 'thing' covers both bodies (entities) and accidents (attributes). Therefore, 'not like things' negates quiddity in both realms.

Checkpoint

A) Substance and accident
B) Entity (ʿayn) and attribute (ṣifa)
C) Body and spirit
D) Creator and creation

Key Takeaway
Saying 'God is a thing not like things' negates the quiddity of both bodies and accidents, affirming that God is utterly unique in essence and attributes.

Maturidi uses an analogy about removing likeness. Reflect: have you ever prayed or called upon Allah with a mental image of Him? What can you do to ensure your worship is free from any form of anthropomorphism?

The First Formulation: Agreement in Names Does Not Imply Likeness
Maturidi presents the first formulation for using 'thing' for God and argues that shared names do not imply likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of Maturidi's two formulations for using the term 'thing' for God, and why agreement in names does not necessitate likeness in essence.

Core Idea
Shared names don't imply shared reality.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a potential confusion: if we call God 'thing,' does that make Him similar to other things? He introduces two formulations, beginning with a key distinction: names can be common while meanings differ. This lesson unpacks his first formulation with examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the phrase 'الموافقة في الأسماء لا توجب التشابه'.
This is the core principle.
2
Examine the example of 'unique one'
Maturidi gives the example of saying 'so-and-so is the unique one of his age and the unique one of his people.' Notice that two persons can share the name 'unique one' while denying any likeness in the intended respect.
This illustrates how names can be shared without implying similarity.
3
Consider the example of kufr and Islam
Maturidi shows that 'kufr' and 'Islam' are both called 'name' but have opposite meanings. This further supports that shared names do not necessitate likeness.
The same principle applies to movements, actions, and the term 'thing' for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا في القول بالشيء عبارتان : ١ ـ إحداهما أن يُجعل الشيء اسما، والموافقة في الأسماء لا توجب التشابه، لما قد يُستعمل في موضع نفي الموافقة في المعنى، نحو أن يُقال: «فلان واحد عصره وواحد قومه على نفي أن يكون له فيهم نظير أو شبيه من الوجه الذي أريد ، وإن كانوا جميعا في تسمية الواحد شركاء؛ ولو كانت الموافقة في الاسم توجب التشابه لا يحتمل استعماله في موضع إرادة نفي الموافقة. وكذلك نجد قول «كفر) و «إسلام على تحقيق الـ «اسم» لكل واحد منهما، والموافقةُ من حيث القول، ولكن المعنى متناقض ؛ وكذا ذلك في الحركات والأفعال ونحو ذلك .
And we have regarding the saying of 'thing' two formulations: One of them is that 'thing' is made a name, and agreement in names does not necessitate likeness, because it is sometimes used in a place of denying agreement in meaning, such as when it is said: 'So-and-so is the unique one of his age and the unique one of his people' in denial that he has any equal or likeness among them in the respect intended, even if they all share in the naming of 'one'. And if agreement in name necessitated likeness, it would not be permissible to use it in a place where denial of agreement is intended. Similarly, we find the saying of 'disbelief' (kufr) and 'Islam' as a true name for each of them, and agreement in wording, but the meaning is contradictory. The same applies to movements, actions, and the like.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage classifies two formulations (ʿibāratān) for using the word 'thing' for God, but only the first is elaborated. The first formulation treats 'thing' as a mere name (ism). Maturidi argues that sharing the same name does not imply similarity in reality. For example, two people can both be called 'unique one' (wāḥid) but in different respects, meaning each has no equal in his own context. If agreement in name implied likeness, such usage would be impossible. He gives another example: 'kufr' and 'Islam' are both called 'name' yet they are opposites. The same applies to movements and actions. Thus, calling God 'thing' does not make Him like other things.

Key Terms
شيء
اسم
موافقة
تشابه
واحد
كفر
إسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the same word for God and creation implies they share some attribute or likeness.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that agreement in names does not entail agreement in meaning or reality. Examples like 'unique one' and 'kufr/Islam' show that opposites can share a name.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi give to show that agreement in name does not imply likeness?
A) Calling both God and humans 'merciful'
B) Calling two different people 'unique one' in different senses
C) Using the word 'thing' for God and creation
D) The word 'body' applied to different entities

Key Takeaway
Using 'thing' for God is permissible because shared names do not imply shared attributes; the first formulation establishes this principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that names and meanings can diverge. Examine your heart: where do you need to make your reality match your claim?
?
Consider someone who disagrees with you on religious practice. Do you assume that because you both share the name 'Muslim', your faith is essentially the same? Maturidi's argument challenges you: shared names do not necessitate likeness. How does this free you from judging others based on labels alone, and what does it demand of you in terms of humility and tolerance?
?
The example of 'kufr' and 'Islam' shows that the same word 'name' can apply to opposites. Your own faith is not static—it can strengthen or weaken. Today, are you truly living up to the name 'Islam' (submission)? Or are there areas of your life where you are submitting to your desires rather than to Allah? Resolve to make your name and your reality one.

Two Proofs for Affirming 'Thing' for God
Maturidi presents scriptural and rational proofs for calling God 'thing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational proofs for affirming the term 'thing' (shay') for Allah, and why it is necessary to use this term.

Core Idea
Calling God 'thing' affirms His existence without implying similarity to creation.

Imam Maturidi presents two distinct lines of evidence—one from the Qur'an and one from reason—to demonstrate that the term 'thing' (shay') rightfully applies to Allah. This lesson examines each proof in detail, clarifying the theological necessity of this affirmation and addressing common misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: two proofs introduced as 'وجهان' (two aspects).
2
Examine the scriptural proof (samʿ)
Focus on how Maturidi uses verse 42:11 ('There is nothing like Him') to argue that the negation of likeness presupposes that God is a 'thing'. Also note the second verse (6:19).
The argument: if God were not a 'thing', the negation would be meaningless.
3
Examine the rational proof (ʿaql)
Understand Maturidi's claim that 'thingness' is simply the name of affirmation (ithbāt) in common usage, and its opposite ('no thing') is negation. Since God exists, He must be affirmed as a 'thing'.
This argument is cut short in the text; think about how it would be completed.
4
Reflect on the necessity of both proofs
Consider why Maturidi uses both scripture and reason. Each reinforces the other: revelation provides direct authority, while reason shows the logical necessity.
This dual approach is characteristic of Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل إثبات القول بـ «الشيء» وجهان: أ - أحدهما السمع من قوله: ليس كمثله شيء [الشورى ، ١١/٤٢]، ولو لم يكن هو شيئًا لم ينف عنه شيئية الأشياء باسم الشيئية؛ إذ الشيء في التحقيق خلاف ما لا يحتمل القول بالشيء . وكذلك قوله : قل أي شيء أكبر شهادة قل الله شهيد ﴾ [الأنعام ، ١٩/٦]، فلو لم يكن يقع عليه اسم الشيء لكان لا يحتمل تضمنه ذلك القول حتى يُنسب إليه . بــ وأما العقل فهو أن الشيئية اسم الإثبات لا غيرُ في العُرْف، إذ القول بـ «لا شيء» نفي
And the proof for affirming the saying of 'thing' has two aspects: A) One is scripture (samʿ) from His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11). And if He were not a thing, He would not have negated from Himself the thingness of things by the name of thingness; for 'thing' in reality is the opposite of what does not admit the saying of 'thing'. Likewise His saying: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness between me and you' (Qur'an 6:19). For if the name 'thing' did not apply to Him, He would not be capable of containing that statement such that it is attributed to Him. B) As for reason (ʿaql), it is that 'thingness' (shayʾiyya) is the name of affirmation (ithbāt), nothing else, in common usage, for the saying 'not a thing' is negation.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the permissibility (or necessity) of calling God 'thing'. The first proof is scriptural: the Qur'an itself uses the word 'thing' for God in two verses. In 42:11, God negates likeness by saying 'there is nothing like Him' – this negation presupposes that God is a 'thing', otherwise the negation would be meaningless. Similarly in 6:19, God asks 'what thing is greatest in testimony?' and answers 'Allah is a witness', implying that 'thing' applies to Him. The second proof is rational: in common usage, 'thingness' simply means affirmation of existence, and its opposite ('no thing') is negation. Since God exists, He must be called a 'thing'. However, the rational proof is incomplete here, but the argument is that 'thing' is the basic expression for existence.

إثبات

عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that calling God 'thing' is anthropomorphic, implying He is like created objects.
Correction: The proofs show that 'thing' here is a necessary term for affirming existence, not implying similarity or limitation. The Qur'an itself uses the term while affirming that nothing is like Him.

Checkpoint

B) 'There is nothing like Him'
C) 'Allah is the Creator of all things'
D) 'And to Allah belongs the dominion of the heavens and the earth'

Key Takeaway
Affirming 'thing' for Allah is established by both revelation and reason, confirming that He exists and is not like any created thing.

The Meaning of Quiddity and God's Unique Attributes
Maturidi defines quiddity and applies it to God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that created beings have a definable quiddity (māhiyyah), whereas God's attributes like knowledge and power cannot be defined by a quiddity; rather, they are understood by negation of their opposites, avoiding anthropomorphism and separation from essence.

Core Idea
God's attributes have no definable 'whatness'—they are known by negation of deficiency.

Imam Maturidi begins by explaining the concept of quiddity (māhiyyah) as applied to created things. He then shows that for God, we cannot define His quiddity; we only describe Him by what He is not. This distinction is crucial to avoid comparing God to creation (tashbih) and to affirm that His attributes are not separate from His essence (ghayriyyah).

Guided Path
1
Understand quiddity in creation
Read the definitions of body and man given by Maturidi. Notice that quiddity is expressed by a property (khāsiyyah) that defines the essence.
Body is defined by having three dimensions; man by living, rational, mortal.
2
Apply the concept to God
See how Maturidi says that for God's attributes 'Knowing' and 'Powerful', we do not define a quiddity. Instead, we only mention the removal of concealment and that things come to Him.
This avoids restricting God to a definition.
3
Recognize the warnings against tashbih and ghayriyyah
Understand that if we attempted to define God's quiddity, we would fall into anthropomorphism. Also, if we treat knowledge and power as separate from God's essence, we would fall into the error of otherness.
The phrase 'not like other than Him' affirms attributes are by His essence.
4
Conclude with the proper affirmation
Maturidi concludes that God is knowing and powerful by His essence, not by another. This is the correct tawhidic understanding.
This sets the foundation for all discussions of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مائيته ' ، نحو أن يُقال: «ما الجسم ؟» ، فتقول : «ما له أبعاد ثلاثة»؛ و «ما الإنسان؟» فتذكر حده المعروف في الشاهد من الحي الناطق الميت أي المحتمل لذلك. وكذلك كل جوهر له حد يُذكر باسم الخاصية له. وعلى ذلك «عالم» و «قادر» لا يُذكر خاصيته بحرف يحد ذاته أو يُعلم مائيته، إنما يُذكر ارتفاع الخفاء عنه وتأتي الأشياء له، ولا يُذكر مائية ذاته، فجائز القولُ بذلك [ في حق الله ]؛ وليس في ذلك حرف التشبيه في مائية الذات؛ فخُشي أن يفهم غيريّة العِلم والقدرة ، كما هما في الشاهد، فقيل : لا كغيره ممن ذكر، ليُعلم أنه بذاته عالم قادر لا بغيره وبالله التوفيق.
Its quiddity, such as is said: 'What is the body?' So you say: 'That which has three dimensions'; and 'What is man?' So you mention its known definition in the witnessed realm as the living, rational, mortal—i.e., susceptible to that. Likewise every substance has a definition mentioned by the name of its property. And regarding 'Knowing' and 'Powerful', their property is not mentioned by a letter that defines His essence or makes known His quiddity; rather, the removal of concealment from Him and that things come to Him is mentioned, and the quiddity of His essence is not mentioned. So it is permissible to say that concerning God; and there is in that no letter of anthropomorphism in the quiddity of the essence. So it was feared that otherness of knowledge and power might be understood, as they are in the witnessed realm, so it was said: not like other than Him who was mentioned, so that it be known that He is knowing and powerful by His essence, not by another. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines quiddity (māhiyyah) and applies it to created things like body and man, where essence can be defined. For God, attributes like 'Alim (Knowing) and Qadir (Powerful) do not have a quiddity that can be defined; rather, they indicate negation of ignorance and inability. This avoids tashbih (anthropomorphism). The passage warns against understanding knowledge and power as separate from God's essence (ghayriyyah), so it is said that He is knowing and powerful by His essence, not by another.

Key Terms
مائية
خاصية
تشبيه
غيرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine attributes have a quiddity (māhiyyah) like created attributes, or that they are separate from God's essence.
Correction: God's attributes are not defined by a quiddity; they are understood by negation of their opposites (ignorance, inability). They are not 'other' than His essence: He is knowing and powerful by His essence.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between defining the quiddity of created beings and defining the quiddity of God?
A) They are the same.
B) Created beings' quiddity can be known, but God's quiddity is not defined.
C) God's quiddity is defined by negation.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
God's quiddity is unknowable; He is 'Alim and Qadir by His essence, not through separate attributes or by comparison to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divine Names from Revelation and Reason
Maturidi affirms the dual source of divine names and refutes tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine names and attributes are established by both revelation and reason, and that using the same names for God and creatures does not imply similarity (tashbih).

Core Idea
Divine names are known through revelation and reason, without implying similarity between God and creation.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a fundamental question in Islamic theology: How do we know the names and attributes of Allah? Maturidi argues that both the revealed texts (sam‘) and rational reflection (‘aql) confirm the names. He also responds to the concern that using the same names for God and creatures might lead to anthropomorphism, clarifying that similarity is only in the name, not in the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully, noting the distinction between 'sam‘' (revelation) and '‘aql' (reason).
2
Identify the two sources
The passage states that naming God is true from both sam‘ and ‘aql. Mark where each is mentioned.
3
Understand the refutation of tashbih
The text addresses those who think naming God implies similarity. Notice how Maturidi argues that similarity is only in the name, not the essence.
4
See the rational proof
Finally, the passage gives a rational argument: the diversity of creation indicates that God's action is by choice, not nature, supporting the names from reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الوصف الله بأنه قادر عالم حي كريم جواد، والتسمية بها حق من السمع والعقل جميعا . فالسمع ما جاء به القرآن وسائر كتب الله ، وسَمَّى بالذي ذكرتُ الرسل والخلائق كل منهم. إلا أن قوما وجهوا تلك الأسماء إلى غيره ظناً منهم أن في إثبات الاسم تشابها بينه وبين كل مسمّى ولو كان به ذلك لكان بنفي التعطيل ذلك، وبنفيه أيضا تشابه بينه وبين ما لا يدخل تحت اسم، وهو ما ليس بموجود . ولكن قد بينا بعد التشابه لموافقة الاسم. فهو مسمّى بما سَمَّی به نفسه موصوف بما وصف به نفسه. والعقل يوجب ذلك، لأن الله سبحانه إذ ثبت عنه مُختَلِفُ الخلق بجوهره وصفاته دلّ على أن فعله ليس بفعل الطباع بل هو فعل الاختيار.
Then describing Allah as powerful, knowing, living, generous, bountiful, and naming by them is true from both revelation and reason together. As for revelation, it is what the Quran and all the books of Allah brought, and He named the Messengers and all creatures with what I mentioned. However, some people directed those names to other than Him, thinking that in affirming the name there is similarity between Him and every named thing. If it were so, then by negating it there would be similar negation of attributes, and by negating it also there would be similarity between Him and what does not fall under a name, which is non-existent. But we have already explained that similarity is due to agreement of the name. So He is named with what He named Himself, described with what He described Himself. And reason necessitates that, because Allah, when it is established that He has created different creations in their essence and attributes, indicates that His action is not the action of nature but rather the action of choice.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage establishes that the divine names and attributes are known through both revelation (sam') and reason ('aql). It refutes the idea that naming God causes similarity (tashbih) to created things. Instead, the names are proper to God, and reason confirms that His diverse creation proves His action is by choice, not nature.

Correction: Maturidi clarifies that similarity is only in the name, not in the essence. Negating the names would lead to worse problems like denying attributes or implying similarity with non-existence.

Key Takeaway

Divine Attributes: Established by Revelation and Reason
Maturidi states the dual source of divine attributes and rebuts the charge of anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the names and attributes of God are established by both revelation and reason, and that affirming names does not imply similarity with created beings.

Core Idea
Revelation and reason together establish God's names, without implying similarity.

Imam Maturidi opens this passage by affirming that describing God with attributes like Powerful, Knowing, and Living is true from both revelation and reason. He then addresses a common doubt: does naming God make Him resemble His creation? Through a clever refutation, he shows that denying names would be even worse, and concludes that reason itself demands that God's actions be voluntary, not mechanical.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi starts with a clear statement and then addresses an objection. Pay attention to the key terms like sam' (revelation) and 'aql (reason).
2
Identify the two sources
The passage says the attributes are right from both revelation and reason. Locate the part that explains what revelation provides and what reason provides. Revelation gives the names from the Qur'an and earlier books; reason shows that God's diverse creation implies He acts by choice.
Notice that revelation provides the specific names, while reason provides the argument that God's act is voluntary.
3
Understand the objection and refutation
Some people think that if we affirm names like 'Powerful' for God, it makes Him similar to powerful creatures. Maturidi refutes this by saying that if that were true, then denying names would make God similar to non-existent things. This shows that the fear of similarity is unfounded.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument: the opposite leads to an absurdity.
4
Reflect on the rational proof
Finally, Maturidi argues that the sheer diversity of creation in essence and attributes proves that God's act is not like natural processes (which are uniform) but is an act of free choice. This rational proof confirms what revelation already states.
This connects the names to God's voluntary action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ في صفات الله تعالى ] قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الوصف لله بأنه قادر عالم حي كريم جواد، والتسمية بها حق من السمع والعقل جميعا. فالسمع ما جاء به القرآن وسائر كتب الله، وسَمَّى بالذي ذكرتُ الرسل والخلائق كل منهم. إلا أن قوما وجهوا تلك الأسماء إلى غيره ظناً منهم أن في إثبات الاسم تشابها بينه وبين كل مسمّى ولو كان به ذلك لكان بنفي التعطيل ذلك، وبنفيه أيضا تشابه بينه وبين ما لا يدخل تحت اسم، وهو ما ليس بموجود. ولكن قد بينا بعد التشابه لموافقة الاسم. فهو مسمّى بما سَمَّى به نفسه موصوف بما وصف به نفسه. والعقل يوجب ذلك، لأن الله سبحانه إذ ثبت عنه مختلف الخلق بجوهره وصفاته دل على أن فعله ليس بفعل الطباع بل هو فعل الاختيار.
A question concerning the attributes of God, may He be exalted. Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the description of God as Powerful (Qadir), Knowing ('Alim), Living (Hayy), Generous (Karim), Bountiful (Jawad), and the naming with these is a truth from both revelation (sam') and reason ('aql) together. Revelation is what the Qur'an and all of God's books came with, and He named the messengers and all creatures with what I mentioned. Except that some people directed those names to other than Him, thinking that in affirming the name there is a similarity (tashabuh) between Him and every named thing (musamma). But if that were so, then by negating the name there would be a similarity between Him and what does not fall under a name, which is non-existent. Rather, we have already explained the similarity in terms of agreement of the name. So He is named by what He named Himself, described by what He described Himself. And reason necessitates that, because when it is established that God, the Most High, has created diverse creation in essence and attributes, this indicates that His act is not an act of natures (tabi'a) but an act of choice (ikhtiyar).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. It begins by stating that describing God with attributes like Qadir, 'Alim, etc., and naming Him by them, is established by both revelation and reason. Revelation provides the names from the Qur'an and earlier scriptures; God Himself named the prophets and all creatures with these names. The author then refutes a common misconception: some people think that affirming names for God implies similarity (tashbih) between Him and created beings. He counters by arguing that if that were the case, then denying names would imply similarity between God and non-existence, which is absurd. Therefore, God is named and described only as He has named and described Himself. Finally, reason itself requires this: since God creates diverse beings with distinct essences and attributes, His action cannot be a blind natural process (tab') but must be an act of free choice (ikhtiyar). This is the first step in proving that God acts voluntarily.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming names like 'Powerful' for God implies He is similar to powerful creatures.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if affirming a name implied similarity, then denying the name would imply similarity to non-existence, which is worse. Thus the objection is self-defeating.

Checkpoint

B) Only revelation

Key Takeaway
The names and attributes of God are established by both revelation and reason, and affirming them does not imply similarity with creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divine Attributes

Choice Implies Power and Will
Maturidi deduces power and will from choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational inference that if God acts by choice, He must possess both power (qudra) and will (irada).

Core Idea
Choice without power is impotent; power without choice is blind.

Having established that God acts by choice (ikhtiyar), Maturidi now draws a necessary conclusion: such an agent must have power (qudra) to execute that choice and will (irada) to direct it. This short but dense passage uses a contrast between orderly and disordered action to prove the point, relying on the methodological principle that the visible world (shahid) provides a basis for understanding the unseen (gha'ib).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage aloud, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'fa' (then) and 'li-kawnihi' (because He is).
Notice how the argument unfolds in a single conditional chain.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage moves from premise (choice established) to conclusion (power and will established) via a contrast: a powerless agent produces disorder. Trace this reasoning step by step.
This is a classic kalam inference (istidlal).
3
Reflect on the shahid-gha'ib analogy
Maturidi uses the observable world (shahid) as a model for understanding the unseen (gha'ib). Think about why this analogy is valid in kalam and how it supports the argument.
This method is central to Maturidi's epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا ثبت الاختيار ثبتت له القدرة على الخلق والإرادة، لكونه على ما هو عليه؛ لا قدرة له يخرج الذي يكون منه مضطربا فاسدا، ولا يملك الشيء وضده. فثبت أن ما كان منه بقدرة كان واختيار؛ وذلك أمارات الفعل الحقيقية في الشاهد الذي هو أصل للعلم بالغائب. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And when choice is established, there is established for Him power (qudra) over creation and will (irada), because He is as He is; one who has no power brings forth what is disordered and corrupt from him, and he does not possess a thing and its opposite. So it is established that what comes from Him is by power and choice; and that is the mark of true action in the witnessed (shahid) which is the basis for knowledge of the unseen (gha'ib). And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a logical inference: once it is proven that God acts by choice (ikhtiyar), it necessarily follows that He has power (qudra) and will (irada). The author contrasts a voluntary agent with a compelled one: a compelled agent produces disordered results and cannot do the opposite of what it does. A voluntary agent, by contrast, has the capacity to create or not create, to bring into existence or annihilate. This ability to do a thing and its opposite is the sign of true action in the observable world (shahid), which serves as a basis for understanding the unseen (gha'ib). The proof is that a being without power would produce disordered actions, but God's creation is orderly and purposeful.

Key Terms
الاختيار
القدرة
الإرادة
الفعل الحقيقي
الشاهد
الغائب
الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what must be established once choice (ikhtiyar) is proven?
A) That God is generous
B) That God has power and will
C) That God is eternal
D) That God is one

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that God's power and will are logically necessary corollaries of His voluntary action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God has absolute power and will over everything. But when you face a setback, do you truly trust that His choice is better than yours? Where in your life do you resist His decree? Can you name one area where you need to surrender your will to His today?
?
The lesson teaches that a being without power produces disorder. Look at your own actions—where do you see disorder, procrastination, or lack of follow-through? What does that reveal about your reliance on your own strength rather than on Allah's power? What one step will you take to align your effort with tawakkul?
?
If God's power and will are inseparable, what does that mean for your prayers? Do you ask as if He is both able and willing to respond? Or do you doubt His will or power in certain du'as? Write down one du'a you've been hesitant to make because you doubted one of these attributes, and commit to making it with full conviction tonight.
