Proof from the Perfect Sequence of Creation
Maturidi's first two proofs from the order and sustenance of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the flawless continuity and order of creation itself serves as proof that God possesses knowledge of all things.

Core Idea
A perfectly ordered sequence cannot arise from ignorance; it must be based on knowledge.

Maturidi begins his argument for divine knowledge by pointing to the most obvious evidence: the observable order of the universe. He invites us to reflect on the continuous, uninterrupted sequence of events and the way each created thing is perfectly suited to its purpose. Before we examine the source, consider how even a small example of order—like a watch—requires a knowledgeable maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on continuity of action
Read the Arabic and translation for the first part of the passage, where Maturidi states that the continuity and perfection of action is evidence that the action was based on knowledge.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice that Maturidi presents an observation (the world is ordered) and draws a conclusion (therefore God knows what He does). This is a simple a fortiori argument.
The phrase 'محكما متقنا' emphasizes flawless perfection.
3
Reflect on the second proof
Read the second part about the creation of substances and their sustenance. How does this add a new dimension?
Here he argues that not only are things ordered, but each thing's nature and needs are known and provided for.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ما ذكرنا من تواصل الفعل - أعني الواقع به بالفعل - وتتابعه محكما متقنا هو الدليل على أنه كان فعله على العلم به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إنه إذ خرج كل الجواهر التي لا يمتحن في مصالح الممتحنين، وخلق كل شيء أريد به البقاء مع خلق ما به بقاؤه، علم أنه كان بمن يعلم كيفية كل شيء وحاجته وما به القوام والمعاش. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إن الله سبحانه خلق الخلق خلقاً دلّ على حدثه، وعلى أن له محدثًا، وعلى وحدانية محدثه؛ فلولا أن علم بالخلق - يعلم أنه إذا خَلَق على ما خَلَق كان فيه دليل العلم به وبخلقه - لا يُحتمل أن يخرج على ذلك خلقه. وبالله التوفيق. وعلى ذلك مجيئ الرسل بالأمر الذي لو اتبعوا وعملوا بما جاءوا به ما احتمل الخلاف ولا التفرق ولا الفساد، فلولا علمه أمكنه متفرقة.
And based on what we mentioned of the continuity of action - I mean that which actually occurs - and its sequence, firmly fixed and perfect, is the evidence that His action was based on knowledge of it. And there is no power except with Allah. And also, when He brought forth all the substances (jawahir) that are not tested in the interests of those put to trial, and created everything intended for permanence along with creating what sustains it, it is known that He is one who knows the nature (kayfiyya) of everything, its need, and that by which subsistence and livelihood exist. And there is no power except with Allah. And also, Allah, the Most High, created creation in a way that indicates its temporality (huduth), and that it has a creator (muhdith), and the oneness (wahdaniyya) of its Creator; were it not that He knows about creation - that He knows that when He creates as He creates, there is in it evidence of knowledge of Him and of His creation - it would not be possible that His creation would come out like that. And with Allah is success. And likewise, the coming of the messengers with the command which, if they had followed and acted upon what they brought, would not have admitted disagreement, division, or corruption; if not for His knowledge, it could have been diverse [fragmented?].
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that God has knowledge ('ilm). The first proof: the continuous, flawless sequence of events in creation is so perfectly ordered that it must be based on knowledge. The second: God created every substance (jawhar) and all things with their sustenance precisely tailored to their needs, which requires knowledge of each thing's nature and requirements. The third: the entire created order points to its own temporality (huduth), the existence of a creator, and His oneness; only a creator who knows exactly what He is doing could produce such a creation that inherently bears those proofs. The passage then adds a proof from the sending of messengers: the teachings they brought are so coherent and unified that, if followed, they would prevent disagreement and corruption. This coherence reflects divine knowledge, as otherwise the messages would have been contradictory.

Key Terms
تواصل الفعل
محكما متقنا
الجواهر
المصالح
القوام
المعاش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the order in creation is due to chance or nature without a knowing agent.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such flawless continuity and precise provision cannot be attributed to blind forces; only knowledge can produce such a system.

Checkpoint

A) That God is powerful
B) That God's action was based on knowledge
C) That the world is eternal

Proof from Creation's Inherent Signs and Messengers
Maturidi's third proof from creation's intrinsic signs and messenger proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the very structure of creation points to a knowing creator, and how the unified message of prophets confirms this knowledge.

Core Idea
Creation itself testifies to its Maker's knowledge, and prophets bring a unified message that cannot come from ignorance.

This same knowledge is reflected in the consistent, unified message brought by all prophets. Read the passage to see how these two lines of evidence converge.

Proofs from Creation: Divine Action Based on Knowledge
Maturidi presents two proofs from creation that God's action is based on knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses two arguments from creation—the flawless sequence of divine acts and the precise creation of substances with their sustenance—to prove that God's action is based on knowledge.

Core Idea
The order and provision in creation prove God acts with knowledge.

Maturidi now presents concrete proofs from the observable world. He argues that the perfect sequence of divine actions and the way everything is created with exactly what it needs cannot be products of chance. These observations lead to the conclusion that God's action is based on knowledge ('ilm). Let's examine each proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice that Maturidi presents two distinct arguments: one about the continuity of action, another about the creation of substances and their needs.
2
Understand the first proof: continuity/perfection
Maturidi says the solid and perfect sequence of divine action (tawāṣul al-fiʿl) is evidence that God acts with knowledge. Why? Because a consistent, flawless pattern implies an intelligent agent.
This is like seeing a perfectly timed machine—it shows the maker's knowledge.
3
Understand the second proof: creation of substances and provision
Maturidi points out that substances not tested in human trials (perhaps fundamental elements) are created, and everything intended to last is provided with what sustains it. This precise tailoring shows the Creator knows each thing's modality and needs.
Think of how living things have food and habitat—this isn't random.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ما ذكرنا من تواصل الفعل - أعني الواقع به بالفعل - وتتابعه محكما متقنا هو الدليل على أنه كان فعله على العلم به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إنه إذ خرج كل الجواهر التي لا يمتحن في مصالح الممتحنين، وخلق كل شيء أريد به البقاء مع خلق ما به بقاؤه، علم أنه كان بمن يعلم كيفية كل شيء وحاجته وما به القوام والمعاش. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And upon what we mentioned of the continuity of action—I mean the action that actually occurs—and its sequence being solid and perfect, it is the evidence that His action was based on knowledge of it. And there is no power except by Allah. And also, when He brought forth all the substances that are not tested in the interests of those who are tested, and created every thing intended for permanence along with creating what sustains it, it is known that He is One who knows the modality of every thing, its need, and what constitutes its support and livelihood. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs that God's action is based on knowledge. Maturidi first refers back to the previous discussion about the continuity and sequence of divine action in the world, which he describes as solid (muḥkaman) and perfect (mutqanan). Such flawless and coherent order can only come from an agent who acts with knowledge ('ilm) of what He is doing. The second proof is drawn from the creation of substances (jawāhir) that are not subject to testing in the interests of those who are tested—perhaps referring to celestial bodies or fundamental elements that serve as a stable backdrop for human life. He then points out that everything intended for permanence is created along with that which sustains it, such as food and means of livelihood. This precise provision shows that the Creator knows the modality (kayfiyya) of each thing, its specific needs, and what supports its existence. Thus, both the perfection of action and the suitability of creation for its purpose are evidence that God acts with knowledge.

تواصل الفعل (tawāṣul al-fiʿl)
علم ('ilm)
جواهر (jawāhir)
قوام (qiwām)
معاش (maʿāsh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order in creation could arise from nature or chance without knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the perfect sequence and tailored provision require knowledge, not chance.

Checkpoint
What does the continuity and perfection of divine action indicate according to Maturidi?
A) That God acts by necessity
B) That God's action is based on knowledge
C) That the world is eternal
D) That God is not involved in creation

Key Takeaway
The flawless order of actions and the precise provision for creation demonstrate that God acts with knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage says God created everything intended for permanence along with what sustains it. Think of one blessing you rely on daily (like food, water, or shelter). Have you ever thanked the One who knew exactly what you needed before you existed? Will you today, with full awareness that His knowledge is the reason it exists?
?
If God's flawless creation proves He acts with knowledge, then your failures and mistakes are not from His lack of knowledge but from your own choices. How does this truth challenge the way you respond to your sins—do you blame circumstance or genuinely repent to the All-Knowing?

Refuting Deniers of Takwin and Attributors of Creation to Natures
Maturidi contrasts two erroneous views on creation and argues that denying takwin is worse than naturalism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rhetorical refutation of two erroneous views about creation and why denying takwin leads to an even weaker position than naturalism.

Core Idea
Denying takwin makes creation causeless, worse than attributing it to natures.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with two opposing camps: those who deny an eternal attribute of origination (takwin) and those who attribute creation to natural causes like natures and nutrients. He employs a surprising rhetorical comparison to expose the weakness of the first group. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the gist of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the two views refuted
Look for the two groups: those who deny takwin and those who attribute creation to natures/nutrients. Notice how Maturidi describes each.
3
Understand the comparative argument
Maturidi says the naturalist view is 'more right' (aḥaqq) than the first. Ask why he would say that. The commentary will explain the reasoning.
This is a rhetorical move, not an endorsement of naturalism.
4
Reflect on the role of takwin
Consider how this argument supports the necessity of the divine attribute of takwin. Think about what happens if creation has no divine origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك، قول من قال: «كان الله ولا خلق، ثم كان الخلق بلا تكوين، هو غير الخلق كقول من ذكر - بلا غير المضاف إليه - العالم. والله الموفق. على أن قول من نسب الخلق إلى الطبائع والأغذية أحق - إذ في ذلك إثبات أمر كان به غيرها - من قول من يجعل الخلق الله بعد أن لم يكن بلا شيء من الله سوى كون الخلق، فيكون تحقيق، وليس من هؤلاء تحقيق. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Likewise, the statement of one who says: 'There was Allah and no creation, then creation came into being without origination (takwin),' is like the statement of one who mentioned—without adding it to its creator—the world. And Allah is the grantor of success. Moreover, the statement of one who attributes creation to natures and nutrients is more right—since in that there is affirmation of something by which others come to be—than the statement of one who makes creation by Allah after it was not, without anything from Allah other than the existence of creation, so that would be an affirmation, and these people have no affirmation. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage refutes two erroneous views about creation. The first is the claim of those who say that God existed without creation, and then creation came into being without any attribute of origination (takwin) from God—i.e., creation occurs without an eternal attribute of 'making' (takwin) being involved. Maturidi compares this to the statement of someone who mentions the world without attributing it to its Creator, which is essentially a denial of God's role. The second view is that of those who attribute creation to natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) and nutrients (aghdhiya), i.e., naturalistic causes. Maturidi surprisingly says that this view is more right (aḥaqq) than the view of those who affirm that God creates but without any attribute from Him other than the sheer existence of creation. Why? Because the naturalist at least affirms some cause (an amr by which things come to be), whereas the latter group affirms no real cause from God—they only say 'creation exists' without linking it to a divine attribute. Maturidi then adds that the naturalists have some 'affirmation' (taḥqīq) while these others have none. This is a rhetorical point to show the inconsistency of those who deny the attribute of takwin, as they end up with a position even weaker than naturalism.

Key Terms
تكوين (takwin)
طبائع (ṭabāʾiʿ)
أغذية (aghdhiya)
تحقيق (taḥqīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is endorsing naturalism or saying it is true.
Correction: Maturidi is using a rhetorical comparison to show that denying takwin is worse; he is not accepting naturalism as correct.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the statement of those who attribute creation to natures is 'more right' than that of those who affirm creation without takwin?
A) Because naturalism is true
B) Because the naturalists at least affirm a cause, while the latter affirm no divine attribute
C) Because the naturalists believe in God
D) Because creation cannot be from God

Key Takeaway
Denying the attribute of takwin results in a view of creation that is even weaker than naturalism, as it provides no causal link from God to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world around you, do you truly see every moment of existence as a direct act of God's origination, or do you sometimes fall into thinking that things simply 'happen'? How does this lesson challenge you to see creation differently?
?
Maturidi says that denying takwin is worse than attributing creation to nature. How does this make you reconsider the importance of affirming all of God's attributes, even those that seem mysterious or beyond your understanding?
?
If you were to explain to someone why creation must come from an attribute of God rather than just occurring without a cause, what would you say? What does your heart truly believe about God's role in the existence of every creature, and does that belief shape how you live?

Arguments from the Witnessed Realm Refuting Creation as God's Essential Act
Maturidi presents two analogical arguments from the witnessed realm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments from the witnessed realm (shāhid) that demonstrate creation is not identical to God's essence: the analogy of power requiring action, and the argument from evil.

Core Idea
Power without action is impossible; attributing evil to God is blasphemy.

One of the key debates in kalām is whether creation is an essential attribute of God or a distinct act. Maturidi uses two powerful analogies from the witnessed world (shāhid) to argue that creation cannot be God's essence. The first draws on everyday experience of power and speech, the second on evil and wrongdoing. Both lead to the conclusion that God's act of creation is distinct from His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once without analysis. Let Maturidi's words sink in.
2
Identify the two arguments
Notice the first argument about power and speech, and the second argument about evil and moral qualities. See how Maturidi uses the witnessed realm (shahid) as evidence for the unseen (gha'ib).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that both arguments lead to the same conclusion: creation is not identical to God's essence. The second argument also shows that attributing evil to God would be blasphemy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لا يوجد في الشاهد قادر غير ممنوع لا فعل له، وقادر على الكلام لا كلام له؛ والشاهد هو دليل الغائب، فلزم ذلك فيه. وبالله التوفيق. ما قد يوصف الخلق بالفساد والشر والقبح والسوء؛ فلو كان لذاته فعل الله لكان مع ذلك كله موصوفا مسمى فيقال: مفسد شرير قبيح الفعل سيئ العمل. فإذا كان الوصف بهذا والتسمية كفرا، ثبت أن الذي سمي به ووصف هو غير هذا. وبالله النجاة. وعلى ذلك الولاد والطاعة والمعصية والكسب لو كان في الحقيقة له لسمي به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And likewise, there is not found in the witnessed realm a powerful being who is not prevented having no action, nor one powerful over speech having no speech; and the witnessed realm is the evidence for the unseen, so that applies to Him. And with Allah is success. What may describe creation with corruption, evil, ugliness, and badness; so if it were by His essence the act of Allah, He would with all that be described and named, and it would be said: corruptor, evil, ugly of act, bad of work. So when describing with this and naming is blasphemy, it is established that what is named and described by this is other than Him. And with Allah is deliverance. And upon that, birth, obedience, disobedience, and acquisition—if they were in reality His, He would be named by them. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments based on analogy with the witnessed realm (shāhid) and on the problem of evil. The first argument: In the witnessed world, we never find a powerful being who is not prevented (from acting) yet has no action, nor someone powerful over speech yet has no speech. Since the witnessed world is the evidence for the unseen (ghā'ib), the same must apply to God: He is powerful and must have an action (creation) and speech (the Quran). This refutes those who deny that God has an attribute of takwin or that He speaks. The second argument: Creation contains evil (sharr), corruption (fasād), ugliness (qubḥ), and badness (sū'). If creation were God's essential act (li-dhātihi fiʿl Allāh) in the sense that it is identical to His essence, then He would be described with these negative qualities and would be called 'corruptor', 'evil', etc. But describing and naming God with such attributes is blasphemy (kufr). Therefore, it is established that what is described by these evil qualities is not God Himself but rather a distinct act. Maturidi then extends the argument: things like birth (walad), obedience (ṭāʿa), disobedience (maʿṣiya), and acquisition (kasb) are attributed to humans; if they were in reality God's acts, He would be named by them. Thus, creation is not identical to God's essence but is a distinct act through an attribute.

Key Terms
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā'ib)
فساد (fasād)
شر (sharr)
قبح (qubḥ)
كفر (kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi is denying that God creates evil or that God causes evil. However, the point is that if creation were God's essence, evil would be directly attributable to His essence, which is impossible. Creation is distinct from God's essence, so evil in creation does not tarnish God's essence.
Correction: Maturidi is not saying God causes evil; he is arguing that if creation were God's essence, evil would be attributable to Him, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that describing God with corruption, evil, ugliness, and badness is blasphemy?
A) That God does not create evil
B) That creation is not identical to God's essence
C) That evil does not exist
D) That God is not powerful

Key Takeaway
Creation is not God's essential act, because power without action is impossible in the witnessed world, and because evil in creation cannot be attributed to God without blasphemy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or evil in your life, do you ever find yourself blaming Allah or feeling that He is unjust? How does this lesson's argument that evil cannot be attributed to Allah expose that tendency in your heart? What will you do to replace that blame with trust in His perfect attributes?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's power is never idle and that He is constantly acting, does your daily life reflect that trust? Where are you passively waiting for Allah to act while neglecting your own responsibility to act in obedience? Name one area today where you will actively rely on His power instead of your own.
?
Have you ever described Allah with a negative attribute indirectly—such as complaining that He is 'unfair' or 'unjust' when something bad happens? This lesson teaches that such descriptions are kufr. Will you repent from such thoughts and commit to guarding your tongue and heart against any blame of Allah? What concrete steps will you take to remind yourself of His perfection?

God Must Be Essentially an Agent
Maturidi presents a logical argument for God's essential agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's inability to act in pre-eternity was due to the absence of creation, not a deficiency in His essence, establishing that He is essentially an agent.

Core Idea
God's agency is essential, not contingent on creation.

This lesson presents a concise logical argument from Imam Maturidi. He asks: if God's action was not permissible in pre-eternity, was that due to His essence or something else? The answer leads to a key conclusion about divine agency. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style of Maturidi's reasoning.
2
Identify the disjunction
Find the two possibilities Maturidi presents: either the impermissibility of action is essential to God or due to another cause.
This is the core of the logical argument.
3
Understand the consequence of essential non-action
If it were essential, God would be non-acting forever, which contradicts His known agency. Maturidi rejects this branch.
This shows the argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See how the conclusion follows
Since it is not for His essence that He is non-acting, He is essentially an agent. Agency is part of His eternal attributes.
Connect this to the attribute of takwin (origination).
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: God's power is not dependent on creation; He is always capable of acting when He wills.
This distinguishes Islamic theology from certain philosophical views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى إذ لم يجز منه الفعل في الأصل ثم جاز؛ فإما أن يكون لا يجوز لنفسه فيجب أن يكون كذلك أبدا، أو لغيره وهو الذي عنه السؤال. وإذ ثبت أنه لا لنفسه يجوز غير فاعل، فهو لنفسه فاعل. والله الموفق.
And also, when it is not permissible from Allah the Exalted that action occur originally, then it becomes permissible; so either it is not permissible for His essence, and then it must be so forever, or it is for another, and that is what is asked about. And when it is established that it is not for His essence that He is non-acting, then He is essentially an agent. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This is a concise logical argument about divine agency. Maturidi begins with a premise: God's action (fiʿl) was not permissible in the beginning (i.e., in pre-eternity, before creation), then it became permissible (when creation occurred). He then poses a disjunction: either this impermissibility (of acting) is essential to God—meaning it is impossible for Him to act by virtue of His essence—in which case He would never be able to act, and therefore would be forever non-acting; or the impermissibility is due to something else (i.e., the absence of a cause or the non-existence of creation). The latter is the case under discussion. Since it is established that it is not due to His essence that He is non-acting (because that would imply eternal inability), it follows that He is essentially an agent (faʿʿāl). That is, agency is part of His essential attribute; He is not dependent on external causes to become capable of action. This argument supports the view that God has an eternal attribute of takwin (origination) by which He creates when He wills.

Key Terms
فعل (fiʿl)
ذات (dhāt)
فاعل (fāʿil)
تجويز (tajwīz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's pre-eternal inaction was due to a lack of power or deficiency.
Correction: Maturidi shows it was due to the absence of creation, not a deficiency in essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if God's inability to act in pre-eternity were essential to Him, what would follow?
A) He would be able to act when He wills
B) He would be forever non-acting
C) He would change His essence
D) He would need an external cause

Key Takeaway
God's agency is essential to His essence, not contingent on creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since God's agency is essential and not caused by anything, how does this truth challenge your moments of impatience when you feel God is not acting in your life? Will you today consciously affirm that His silence is not a lack of power but a display of His essential wisdom?
?
If God's inability to act before creation was not a deficiency but due to the absence of creation, what does that tell you about your own struggles with God's timing? Are you willing to accept that His non-action in a situation is not a sign of weakness but a part of His eternal plan? Write down one area where you need to trust His essential agency this week.
?
This argument proves that God is essentially an agent—He doesn't become able only when He wills. How does this knowledge refute the fear that God might become unable or change? What would it mean for you to place your total reliance on an essence that never changes?

Refutation of the Prayer Analogy for Creation
Maturidi refutes the prayer analogy for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the analogy between divine creation and human prayer as a delusion.

Core Idea
Analogy does not imply identity: prayer is not parallel to creation.

Some theologians argued that creation is God's act in reality, using an analogy with human prayer. Maturidi responds that this analogy is a delusion, because the name 'prayer' applies to human acts in a way that does not transfer to divine acts. In this passage, we see his reasoning and the importance of precise naming.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the opponent's claim and Maturidi's response.
The opponent uses analogy to argue that creation is God's act in the same sense as prayer is the servant's act.
2
Identify the analogy and its flaw
The opponent compares creation to prayer. Maturidi says this is a delusion (wahm). Why? Because the name 'prayer' is specific to the servant's act; it does not prove that 'creation' follows the same pattern.
Maturidi emphasizes that naming (tasmīya) has rules.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi states that the analogy does not indicate that creation is in reality His act. He also references an earlier proof that such an equation is impossible.
The refutation hinges on the proper application of names, not on a denial that God creates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض من يزعم أن الخلق هو فعل الله في الحقيقة: إنه كالصلاة وهي فعل العبد في الحقيقة. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وذلك وهم؛ إذ ذلك اسم لفعله في الحقيقة. ثم لم يدل على أن الخلق هو في الحقيقة فعله ليسلم له. على أنا قد بينا من حق التسمية به ما يبين إحالة ذلك. فإن قيل: إذ وصف الله بالتكوين في الأزل لم لا كان المكون؟ قيل: لما كون لتكون الأشياء على ما تكون، وذلك نحو القول بالقدرة على الأشياء.
And said some of those who claim that creation is the act of Allah in reality: 'It is like prayer, and it is the act of the servant in reality.' Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: That is a delusion; because that is a name for His act in reality. Then it does not indicate that creation is in reality His act, to be conceded to him. Moreover, we have shown from the right of naming by it what shows the impossibility of that. So if it is said: 'Since Allah is described with takwin (origination) in pre-eternity, why is the originated (mukawwan) not eternal?' The answer: He originated (kawwana) so that things come to be as they are, and that is like the statement about power over things.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: a refutation of an analogy and an answer to an objection. First, some opponents argue that creation (khalq) is God's act in reality (ḥaqīqa) by analogy with prayer (ṣalāt), which is the act of the servant in reality. They imply that just as prayer is truly the servant's act, so creation is truly God's act. Maturidi (Abu Mansur) responds that this is a delusion (wahm). He says that 'prayer' is a name (ism) for the servant's act in reality, but that does not prove that 'creation' is a name for God's act in reality in the same way, because the analogy is not parallel. He adds that he has already shown from the correct usage of naming that such an equation is impossible (iḥāla). The second part addresses an objection: if God is described with the eternal attribute of takwin (origination), then why is the originated (al-mukawwan) not also eternal? The answer is that takwin is an eternal attribute by which things come to be (takuunu) when God wills. It is like the attribute of power (qudra) over things: power is eternal, but the objects of power come into existence at their appointed times. Thus, takwin is eternal while the mukawwan is temporal. This preserves God's eternal agency without making creation co-eternal.

Key Terms
صلاة (ṣalāt)
وهم (wahm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If prayer is called an act of the servant in reality, then creation can be called an act of God in reality by analogy.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the analogy is a delusion because the name 'act' applies to God and humans in fundamentally different ways; the nature of divine agency is not comparable to human agency.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi call the prayer analogy a delusion (wahm)?
A) Because prayer is not a real act
B) Because the name 'prayer' is for the servant's act, but this does not prove that 'creation' is God's act in the same sense
C) Because creation is not an act at all
D) Because the opponents are disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Analogy between divine and human acts fails because naming has its own rules that distinguish between categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because two things share a name, they must be identical in nature? In what area of your life have you made such an assumption? How might this lesson correct that habit?
?
When you hear an argument that uses an analogy, are you quick to accept it if it sounds plausible? What standard should you apply before accepting an analogy about Allah?
?
Maturidi calls the opponent's argument a 'delusion'. Is there any belief you hold that might be founded on a delusion similar to this—assuming similarity where none exists? Are you willing to examine it today?

Answering the Objection about Takwin and the Eternity of Origination
Maturidi answers the objection about takwin and the eternity of the created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's defense of takwin as an eternal attribute whose effect is temporal.

Core Idea
Takwin is eternal like power, but its effect is created in time.

An objection arises: if God is described with the eternal attribute of takwin (origination), then why is the originated (mukawwan) not also eternal? Maturidi answers by drawing an analogy with power (qudra): the attribute is eternal, but its objects are temporal. This preserves God's eternal agency without compromising creation's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage for the second part. The objection is: since takwin is eternal, why isn't the mukawwan eternal? The answer: takwin is like power over things.
The objection assumes that an eternal cause must have an eternal effect.
2
Understand the analogy with power
Maturidi compares takwin to qudra (power). Power is an eternal attribute of God, but the objects of power (maqdurāt) come into existence at their appointed times.
Likewise, takwin is eternal, but what it originates (mukawwan) appears in time.
3
See how this resolves the issue
This shows that an eternal attribute can have temporal effects, so there is no need for the mukawwan to be eternal. Allah's eternal takwin is sufficient to bring about creation in time.
This preserves both God's eternal agency and the createdness of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Moreover, we have shown from the right of naming by it what shows the impossibility of that. So if it is said: 'Since Allah is described with takwin (origination) in pre-eternity, why is the originated (mukawwan) not eternal?' Commentary
This passage contains two parts: a refutation of an analogy and an answer to an objection. First, some opponents argue that creation (khalq) is God's act in reality (ḥaqīqa) by analogy with prayer (ṣalāt), which is the act of the servant in reality. They imply that just as prayer is truly the servant's act, so creation is truly God's act. Maturidi (Abu Mansur) responds that this is a delusion (wahm). He says that 'prayer' is a name (ism) for the servant's act in reality, but that does not prove that 'creation' is a name for God's act in reality in the same way, because the analogy is not parallel. قدرة (qudra)

Takwin is an eternal attribute of Allah that brings about creation in time, just as power brings about its objects when He wills.

?

Proof from the Witnessed World That God Is Eternally Acting
Maturidi argues from the witnessed world that a capable being must have an act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God must have an eternal act and speech, based on analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
In the witnessed world, capability implies action; so too for God.

This short passage from Imam Maturidi presents a crucial proof that God is eternally acting. Using the principle that the witnessed world (al-shahid) reveals the unseen (al-gha'ib), Maturidi argues that since a capable being in this world must have an act, God, the ultimate Capable, must also have an eternal act and speech. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the complete Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage begins with 'وكذلك' (and likewise), connecting to a previous argument.
2
Identify the two observations
Maturidi makes two empirical claims about the witnessed world: a capable being not prevented has an act, and a being capable of speech has speech. Find these in the text.
The phrase 'غير ممنوع' (not prevented) is key to the first observation.
3
Understand the analogy and conclusion
The linking principle is 'والشاهد هو دليل الغائب' (the witnessed is the evidence for the unseen). Apply the observations to God, who is the unseen Capable, and conclude that He must have an act and speech.
The phrase 'فلزم ذلك فيه' means 'that is necessary for Him'—the conclusion is a logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لا يوجد في الشاهد قادر غير ممنوع لا فعل له وقادر على الكلام لا كلام له؛ والشاهد هو دليل الغائب، فلزم ذلك فيه . وبالله التوفيق.
And likewise, there does not exist in the witnessed world a capable being who is not prevented (from acting) without having an act, nor a being capable of speech without having speech; and the witnessed world is the evidence for the unseen, so that is necessary for Him (God). And with God is success.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen. Maturidi states two empirical observations: first, there is no capable being in the witnessed world that is not restricted (or prevented) yet has no act; second, there is no being capable of speech who has no speech. Since the witnessed world (al-shahid) is the evidence for the unseen (al-gha'ib), it follows that the same must apply to God: God, being capable, must have an act (creation) and speech (the Quran). The passage ends with a common formula invoking God's success.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
قادر
فعل
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's capability does not require an act; He could be capable without acting.
Correction: Maturidi argues that capability without act would imply incapacity, based on the analogy from the witnessed world: no capable being is found without an act unless prevented. Since God is not prevented, He must have an act.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the witnessed world demonstrate about a capable being?
A) A capable being may have no act if it is not prevented
B) A capable being must have an act if it is not prevented
C) A capable being always has an act regardless of prevention
D) A capable being's act is not necessary

Key Takeaway
God, being capable, must have an eternal act (creation) and speech (the Quran), as evidenced by the analogy from the witnessed world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is eternally acting—creating, sustaining, speaking—are you living as though He is personally involved in your every moment? When did you last pause to recognize His ongoing act in your life? What one change would make you more aware of His constant presence?
?
Maturidi argues from the witnessed world to the unseen. Look at your own capabilities: are you acting upon what you are capable of, or are you withholding action out of laziness or fear? How does your failure to act reflect on your trust in God's own active nature?
?
God's capability necessitates speech—the Quran. Do you treat the Quran as the eternal, living speech of God speaking to you right now, or just as a historical text? What would it mean for your next recitation to hear it as God's direct address to you?

5 min
Evil in Creation Proves It Is Not God's Essential Act
Maturidi gives a reductio argument against creation being God's essential act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's reductio argument that the presence of evil in creation shows creation cannot be an essential act of God.

Core Idea
If creation were God's essential act, evil would be God's attribute, but that is disbelief.

Maturidi tackles a profound theological question: Is creation a necessary outflow of God's essence? He observes that creation contains evil, ugliness, and corruption. If creation were directly from God's essence, then these negative qualities would be attributable to God Himself. But describing God as evil or corrupt is blasphemy. Therefore, creation must be distinct from God's essential act.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the key terms: الفساد, الشر, القبح, السوء. Then read the English translation.
The passage is a single logical argument.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Notice the 'if...then' structure: 'if that were by His essence the act of God, then He would be described by all of that.' This is the core of the reductio.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow how Maturidi moves from the conditional to the impossibility: describing God with evil is disbelief (كفر), so the premise (creation as essential act) must be false.
The argument is valid: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that Maturidi does not deny God creates evil indirectly; he denies that evil is directly from God's essence. This preserves God's perfection.
This aligns with the common misunderstanding handled in the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما قد يوصف الخلق بالفساد والشر والقبح والسوء؛ فلو كان لذاته فعل الله لكان بذلك كله موصوفًا مسمى فيقال: مفسد شرير قبيح الفعل سيّئ العمل. فإذا كان الوصف بهذا والتسمية كفرا، ثبت أن الذي سُمّي به ووصف هو غير هذا.
What may be described of creation in terms of corruption, evil, ugliness, and wickedness; if that were by His essence the act of God, then He would be described by all of that, and it would be said: corrupt, evil, ugly in action, wicked in deed. And since the description and naming by this is disbelief, it is established that the one named and described is other than this.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument. Maturidi notes that creation contains things described as corrupt, evil, ugly, and wicked. If creation were God's essential act (i.e., directly from His essence without intermediary), then God Himself would be described by these negative attributes. But describing God as 'corrupt', 'evil', etc., is disbelief. Therefore, creation cannot be God's essential act; the one who is described and named by these attributes (i.e., creation) is other than God. So the act of creation must be distinct from God's essence.

Key Terms
الفساد (corruption)
الشر (evil)
القبح (ugliness)
السوء (wickedness)
فعل الله (act of God)
وصف (description)
تسمية (naming)
كفر (disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's argument implies God does not create evil at all.
Correction: Maturidi only denies that evil is directly from God's essence; God may still create evil indirectly through secondary causes. The point is that evil is not an attribute of God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would follow if creation were God's essential act?
A) Creation would be perfect
B) God would be described as corrupt, evil, ugly, and wicked
C) Creation would not contain evil
D) God would be separate from creation

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the presence of evil in creation to prove that creation is not an essential act of God, because attributing evil to God would be disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness suffering or evil in the world, do you ever find yourself questioning God's goodness or even feeling anger toward Him? How does this passage's argument—that calling God 'evil' would be disbelief—confront that feeling directly? What would it look like for you to truly separate the evil in creation from God's essence in your heart?
?
This lesson shows that even the thought of attaching corruption to God is kufr. Have you ever, even subtly, blamed God for personal misfortunes as if He were the source of evil? If you were to consistently remind yourself that evil is only in creation, not in the Creator, how might your response to hardship change? Commit to one specific way you will practice this distinction this week.
?
The Maturidi argues that describing God with evil is logically impossible because it leads to disbelief. But what about your own speech? Do you ever casually attribute negative events to God (e.g., 'God sent this trial to punish me') in a way that could blur the line between His justice and the evil in creation? How can you speak about God's actions in a manner consistent with this lesson's teaching?

The Eternity of Takwin and the Temporality of Its Effect
Maturidi answers an objection about takwin and the temporality of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attribute of takwin (bringing into existence) is eternal, yet the things He brings into existence occur at their appointed times, without implying that creation is eternal.

Core Idea
Takwin is eternal; its effect is temporal — like power, will, and knowledge.

Maturidi here addresses a common objection: if God's attribute of bringing into existence (takwin) is eternal, why aren't the things He brings into existence also eternal? The answer draws a crucial distinction between an eternal attribute and its temporal effect. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how it preserves both God's eternal perfection and the temporal nature of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first sentence of the passage: 'If it is said: Since God is described with takwin in pre-eternity, why is the thing formed not also pre-eternal?' Understand the challenge.
2
Identify the key analogy
Notice that Maturidi answers by comparing takwin to power, will, and knowledge. These are eternal attributes, yet their objects occur in time.
This analogy is central to the argument.
3
Understand the distinction
The occurrence of events is according to what occurs, not according to knowledge or power. The attribute does not change when its effect appears later.
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this answer protects God from being subject to time or change while affirming that creation is genuinely temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذ وصف الله بالتكوين في الأزل لِمَ لا كانَ المكون؟ قيل : لما كوّن لتكون الأشياء على ما تكون، وذلك نحو القول بالقدرة على الأشياء والإرادة لها والعلم بها ليكون كل شيء في وقته. والحدث على الذي يكون لا على العلم به؛ وإن كان الذي يكون يكون من بعد في حد الكائن من غير تغير العلم به والقدرة عليه.
If it is said: Since God is described with takwin (bringing into existence) in pre-eternity, why is the thing formed (al-mukawwan) not [also] pre-eternal? It is said: He formed only so that things come to be as they are, and that is like [saying] He has power over things, will for them, and knowledge of them so that everything occurs in its time. And the occurrence [of things] is according to what occurs, not according to knowledge of it; even though what occurs occurs later within the limit of the thing formed, without change in the knowledge of it or power over it.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector asks: If God is described with takwin (the attribute of bringing into existence) in pre-eternity, why are the things formed not also pre-eternal? The answer is that the takwin is for the purpose of things coming to be at their appointed times, similar to how God has eternal power, will, and knowledge, but the objects of these attributes occur at their specific times. The occurrence of events is determined by the events themselves, not by the eternal knowledge. Although the event occurs later, the knowledge and power remain unchanged. Thus, takwin is eternal but its effect is temporal.

Key Terms
تكوين (bringing into existence)
مكون (thing formed)
أزل (pre-eternity)
قدرة (power)
إرادة (will)
علم (knowledge)
وقت (time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an attribute is eternal, its effect must also be eternal.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the effect depends on the intended time, not on the attribute itself. The attribute remains unchanged while its effect occurs at the appointed time.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's answer, why are things formed not eternal despite God's eternal takwin?
A) Because takwin is not eternal
B) Because God's power is limited
C) Because the takwin is for the purpose of things coming to be at their specific times
D) Because the things formed are not real

Key Takeaway
Takwin is an eternal attribute of God, but its effect (the things formed) occurs in time, just as God's eternal power, will, and knowledge relate to temporal objects without change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power, will, and knowledge are eternal yet everything that happens to you unfolds in time, how does that reshape your trust in a sovereign plan that was never improvised?

Maturidi says 'the occurrence of events is according to what occurs, not according to knowledge of it.' Your choices are not mere shadows of foreknowledge. How does that truth confront any excuse you've made to avoid taking full responsibility for your actions?
?
You are a 'mukawwan'—brought into existence by an eternal takwin. What does it mean to you that your life is not an accident but the deliberate effect of an eternal act? How does that demand a different kind of gratitude and purpose from you today?

Eternal Attributes, Temporal Objects
Maturidi states the principle that God's attributes are eternal but their objects are temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that divine attributes are eternal while their objects are temporal, and why specifying the times of objects is necessary to avoid confusion.

Core Idea
God's attributes are eternal; their objects are temporal — never confuse the two.

Imam Maturidi establishes a foundational principle for discussing divine attributes. When we say God knows, wills, or acts, we affirm that these attributes belong to Him eternally. However, the things He knows, wills, or acts upon exist only when He brings them into being. To avoid mistakenly attributing pre-eternality to created things, Maturidi insists on specifying the times of these objects. This short passage thus protects both God's transcendence and the contingency of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for Maturidi's concise style. Then read the literal translation.
Notice the contrast between 'أزل' (pre-eternity) and 'وقت' (time).
2
Identify the two parts of the principle
The principle has two halves: (1) attributes are described in pre-eternity; (2) objects are mentioned with their times. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the doctrinal purpose
Ask: Why did Maturidi need to state this principle? What wrong belief is he guarding against? The commentary explains: to avoid imagining objects as pre-eternal.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this principle protects divine transcendence. God's attributes are not dependent on creation; creation is dependent on God's will and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أن الله تعالى إذا أطلق الوصف له وصف بما يوصف من الفعل والعلم ونحوه يلزم الوصف به في الأزل. وإذا ذكر معه الذي هو تحت وصفه به من المعلوم والمقدور عليه والمراد والمكوّن يُذكر فيه أوقات تلك الأشياء لئلا يتوهم قدم تلك الأشياء.
And the principle is that when God Most High is described absolutely, He is described by what He is described with of action, knowledge, and the like; the description [of Him] by it is necessary in pre-eternity. And when that which is under His description is mentioned with Him, such as the known, the empowered, the willed, and the formed, their times are mentioned [along with it] so that the pre-eternality of those things is not imagined.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage states a doctrinal principle. The principle has two parts. First, when God is described with any attribute like action or knowledge, that attribute is attributed to Him in pre-eternity (azal). Second, when the objects of those attributes (like the known, the willed, the formed) are mentioned alongside, their times must be specified to avoid the false impression that those objects are also pre-eternal. This safeguards divine transcendence and avoids associating temporality with God.

Key Terms
أصل (principle)
وصف (description)
أزل (pre-eternity)
معلوم (known)
مقدور عليه (empowered/controlled)
مراد (willed)
مكون (formed)
وقت (time)
قدم (pre-eternality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that mentioning times for God's acts implies change in God.
Correction: Maturidi explains that mentioning times only clarifies the temporal nature of the objects, not any change in God.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is temporal
B) So that the pre-eternality of those objects is not imagined
C) Because the attributes change
D) Because the objects are eternal

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are eternal, but their objects exist only in time; mixing them leads to theological error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Are there things in your life—your status, wealth, or relationships—that you treat as if they have eternal significance? How does this principle remind you that only God and His attributes are eternal, and everything else is passing? What can you let go of today?

Takwin Without an Object: Does It Imply Incapacity?
Maturidi answers the objection that takwin without a thing formed implies incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the objection that takwin (bringing into existence) without a thing formed would indicate incapacity.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute does not require an eternal object.

This lesson presents an objection against the eternal attribute of takwin (bringing into existence) and Maturidi's careful answer. The objector argues that if takwin exists without a thing formed, that would indicate incapacity. Maturidi explains that this is only problematic if the takwin was intended for a specific time and failed, but not if it is for a future time when the object will appear. He extends this reasoning to other attributes like hearing, sight, generosity, and bounty.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then Maturidi's response.
The objection uses the phrase 'فإن قيل' and the answer begins with 'قيل'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Maturidi makes a distinction between two scenarios: a takwin intended for a specific time that fails (which would imply incapacity) and a takwin that will produce its object at the appropriate future time (which does not).
The phrase 'لوقت' (for a time) is crucial.
3
Apply the same logic to other attributes
Notice how Maturidi extends the same principle to hearing, sight, generosity, and bounty: these are eternal attributes even though their objects are temporal.
This reinforces the idea that divine attributes are eternal and independent of their objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : في التكوين ولا مُكوَّنَ إثبات العجز. قيل: إنما يكون ذلك لو كان التكوين ليكون لوقت فلم يكن؛ وكذلك في الإرادة والعلم به إذا لم يكن فهو جهل واضطرار. فأما ليكون للوقت الذي يكون فيه فلا على ما بينا من العلم. وعلى ذلك السمع والبصر والكرم والجود، إنه موصوف بها في الأزل وإن كان ما يسمع ويبصر وما ذكر حادثاً.
If it is said: In [the case of] takwin without a thing formed, there is an establishment of incapacity. It is said: That would only be so if the takwin was intended to be for a time and it did not occur; likewise with will and knowledge of it, if it does not occur, it is ignorance and compulsion. But [if it is] to occur at the time when it will occur, then not [is there incapacity], according to what we have explained of knowledge. And similarly for hearing, sight, generosity, and bounty: He is described by them in pre-eternity even though what is heard and seen and what is mentioned are temporal.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objector claims that having takwin (the attribute of bringing into existence) without an existing thing formed would indicate incapacity. Maturidi's answer distinguishes between two scenarios: if the takwin was intended to produce a thing at a specific time and it failed, that would be incapacity. But if the takwin is for a future time when the thing will indeed occur, there is no incapacity. This is consistent with his earlier explanation about knowledge and will. He extends the same principle to God's attributes of hearing, sight, generosity, and bounty: these are eternal attributes, even though their objects (heard, seen, etc.) are temporal. The key is that the attribute is real and eternal, but its effect manifests in time.

Key Terms
تكوين (bringing into existence)
مكون (thing formed)
عجز (incapacity)

إرادة (will)

بصر (sight)
كرم (generosity)
جود (bounty)
حدث (temporal occurrence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an eternal attribute must have an eternal object, otherwise the attribute is imperfect.
Correction: Maturidi shows that an attribute can be eternal while its object is temporal; the attribute is perfect because it is appropriate to its object at the time of occurrence.

Checkpoint

B) When the takwin was intended for a past time and the thing did not occur

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are eternal; their effects manifest at appropriate times without implying deficiency in the Divine essence.

The Principle of Incapacity: A Doer Confined to the Actual
Maturidi states the principle linking incapacity to being confined by actual occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that a doer whose act does not go beyond the actual occurrence is described as incapable, and how this principle underscores God's unlimited power.

Core Idea
A doer tied to what actually happens is incapable; God's power transcends the actual.

Imam Maturidi concludes his discussion of divine attributes with a concise yet profound principle: if a being's action never extends beyond what actually occurs, that being is described as incapable. This principle serves to highlight the absolute nature of divine omnipotence, which is not constrained by actuality. Let's examine this principle closely and its implications for understanding God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Maturidi.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the key terms: 'asl (principle), waqi' (actual occurrence), fi'l (act), wasf (description), 'ajz (incapacity). Understand each.
These terms are foundational.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Why does limitation to the actual indicate incapacity? What does it mean to be capable?
Think about the relationship between act and ability.
4
Connect to divine omnipotence
How does this principle affirm that God's power is not limited to what occurs? Apply the principle to God and creation.
Consider the difference between the Creator and creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أن الذي لا يعدو الواقع بفعله وقت الوصف له بالفعل فله وصف عجز
And the principle is that the one whose act does not go beyond the actual occurrence at the time of description of him with the act, then for him is the description of incapacity.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This final passage states a general principle. It says that if a doer's act does not exceed the actual event—meaning he cannot do more than what actually happens, or his ability is limited to what occurs—then that doer is described as incapable. The implication is that God, being perfectly capable, must have the ability to do more than what actually occurs, or to bring about acts beyond the actual. This again reinforces divine omnipotence over creation.

Key Terms
أصل (principle)
واقع (actual occurrence)
فعل (act)
وصف (description)
عجز (incapacity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because God only does what He does, His power is limited to what actually occurs, implying He is subject to the same restriction as creatures.
Correction: Maturidi's principle actually affirms the opposite: God's power is not confined to actuality; it is unlimited. The principle defines incapacity as being restricted to the actual, so God, who can do as He wills, is not incapable.

Checkpoint

B) He is described as incapable
C) He is wise
D) He is powerful

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle teaches that true power includes the ability to go beyond what actually happens; only the incapable are limited to the actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Divine Attributes in Pre-Eternity and Their Temporal Objects
Maturidi states the principle of pre-eternal divine attributes and the need to mention objects' times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists on mentioning the times of the objects of divine attributes to avoid the misconception that those objects are pre-eternal.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are eternal; their objects are temporal and must be tied to time.

Maturidi lays down a crucial principle: when we describe Allah with attributes like knowledge and power, those attributes are pre-eternal. However, the things known, willed, or created by these attributes are not eternal. To avoid confusion, their times must be specified. This passage explains the reasoning and provides proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the flow: a principle, then two proofs. Pay attention to the key terms like الأزل and الوقت.
The passage uses a compact style; read slowly.
2
Identify the core principle
Look for the sentence beginning with 'الأصل أن الله تعالى...' This states the principle: divine attributes are described in pre-eternity, but their objects require mention of time.
The principle distinguishes between the attribute and its object.
3
Analyze the two proofs
The passage gives two proofs: first, from the description itself (already implies pre-eternity); second, from the negative consequences of omitting time (implies pre-eternity, ignorance, or incapacity). Trace each proof in the text.
The second proof illustrates with the example of 'bringer into being'.
4
Reflect on the protected misconception
Maturidi explicitly warns against imagining that the objects of attributes are pre-eternal. Understand why this is a serious error and how specifying time guards against it.
This misconception could lead to associating temporal things with eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإرادة لها والعلم بها ليكون كل شيء في وقته. والحدث على الذي يكون لا على العلم به؛ وإن كان الذي يكون يكون من بعد في حد الكائن، من غير تغيّر العلم به والقدرة عليه. والأصل أن الله تعالى إذا أطلق الوصف له [و] وصف بما يُوصف من الفعل والعلم ونحوه يلزم الوصف به في الأزل. وإذا ذكر معه الذي هو تحت وصفه به من المعلوم والمقدور عليه والمراد والمكوّن يُذكر فيه أوقات تلك الأشياء لئلا يتوهم قدم تلك الأشياء. ولا قوة إلا بالله. دليل الأول ما سبق له [من] الوصف. ودليل الثاني أنه إذا لم يذكر وقت المفعول به يومئ قدم المفعول أو الجهل به في غير وقته، وكذلك [يومئ] العجز لأنه إذا قيل: «هو مُكَوِّنٌ يومئ أنه كون ليكون في هذه الساعة؛ وكذلك العلم به والقدرة عليه والإرادة. للساعة ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Will and knowledge are for it so that everything occurs in its time. The occurrence is upon what occurs, not upon knowledge of it; even if what occurs occurs later within the limit of the thing coming to be, without change in knowledge of it and power over it. The principle is that when Allah is described absolutely, He is described with what He is described of action and knowledge and the like, the description of Him in pre-eternity is necessary. And when that which is under His description is mentioned with Him — of the known, the empowered, the willed, and the brought into being — the times of those things are mentioned in it, lest the pre-eternity of those things be imagined. There is no power except with Allah. The proof of the first is what preceded of the description. The proof of the second is that if the time of the effected thing is not mentioned, it implies the pre-eternity of the effected thing or ignorance of it at other than its time, and similarly it implies incapacity, because if it is said: 'He is a bringer into being,' it implies that He brings into being to be at this hour; and similarly knowledge, power, and will are for the hour. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the principle that divine attributes like will and knowledge are described in pre-eternity (al-azal), but their objects (the known, the willed, the brought into being) are temporal and must be mentioned with their specific times to avoid implying that they are eternal. Maturidi first states that will and knowledge ensure everything occurs in its time; the event depends on the occurrence itself, not on prior knowledge changing. Then he lays down the principle: when Allah is described with attributes like action and knowledge, the description is necessarily in pre-eternity. However, when the objects of these attributes are mentioned alongside, their times must be specified to prevent the misconception that those objects are eternal. He provides two proofs. The first proof: the description itself (e.g., 'He is knowing') already implies pre-eternity. The second proof: omitting the time of the effected thing either implies that the effected thing is pre-eternal (if attached to the attribute without time) or implies ignorance or incapacity—for example, saying 'He is a bringer into being' without a time might suggest He brings into being at the present moment, which is incorrect if the object is future. Thus, the time must be stated. The phrase 'There is no power except with Allah' ends each segment as a pious closing.

Key Terms
الأزل (al-azal)
الصفة (al-sifa)
المعلوم (al-ma‘lum)
المقدور (al-maqdur)
المراد (al-murad)
المكوّن (al-mukawwan)
الوقت (al-waqt)
القدم (al-qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because Allah's attributes are pre-eternal, the objects of those attributes (e.g., what He knows or wills) are also pre-eternal.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the attributes are pre-eternal but their objects are temporal; the times must be mentioned to avoid this confusion.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason for mentioning the times of the objects of divine attributes when describing Allah?
A) To show that Allah is subject to time
B) To avoid imagining that those objects are pre-eternal
C) To indicate that the attributes are temporal
D) To assert that Allah's knowledge changes over time

Key Takeaway
The pre-eternity of Allah's attributes does not imply the pre-eternity of their temporal objects; the times of those objects must be mentioned to avoid such a misconception.

Maturidi's Analysis of 'Resurrection at the Hour'
Maturidi analyzes two interpretations of 'at the hour'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument against interpreting the phrase 'at the hour' as either a time for the resurrection or a time for Allah's act of bringing it into being.

Core Idea
Divine acts cannot be temporally located without implying contingency.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid examines a theological nuance: when someone says 'Allah does the resurrection at the hour,' what exactly does that mean? Maturidi unpacks two possible interpretations and shows why both are problematic. The analysis reveals a key principle: attributing a time to the divine act itself would compromise divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud, then the translation. Notice the bracketed words indicating uncertain OCR. Let the original wording settle in your mind before analyzing.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Maturidi presents two possible meanings of 'at the hour.' Find them in the text: one concerning the resurrection event itself, the other concerning Allah's act of bringing it into being (takwin).
3
Understand why each is rejected
The first interpretation is 'impossible' because the hour is not a container for the resurrection; the second is 'corrupt' because it assigns a time to the act of creation, implying temporality (hadath). Ask yourself what makes the second argument more subtle.
This connects to the key principle of divine transcendence.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this passage illustrates a core Maturidi doctrine: that divine attributes like takwin are eternal, not subject to time. The act of bringing into being is not itself temporal, only its effect is.
This guards against anthropomorphic conceptions of Allah's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولفعل القيامة والفناء معنى آخر، [هو] أن السائل عنها [ب] «أنه يفعل الساعة»، لا يخلو من أن يريد جعل هذه الساعة وقتا للقيامة، أو لتكوين الله القيامة فالأول محال، لما ليست كذلك ]. والثاني فاسد لما فيه جعل الوقت للتكوين، وذلك أمارة الحدث.
And for the act of resurrection and annihilation there is another meaning, [that] the questioner about it [saying] 'He does it at the hour' cannot be free from intending to make this hour a time for the resurrection, or for Allah's bringing the resurrection into being. The first is impossible, because [it] is not like that. And the second is corrupt because it involves making a time for bringing into being, and that is a sign of temporality.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the phrase 'He does the resurrection at the hour' (i.e., on the Day of Judgment). Maturidi considers two possible interpretations: (1) that the hour itself is made the time of the resurrection event, or (2) that the hour is the time for Allah's act of bringing the resurrection into being (takwin). The first interpretation is impossible because the hour is not that kind of time—it is itself the resurrection time, not a container. The second interpretation is corrupt because it assigns a time to the divine act of bringing into being (al-takwin), which would imply that the act itself is temporal (hadith) and therefore contingent. Thus both interpretations are rejected. The passage highlights the distinction between the divine act and its object: the act of creation is eternal, but its temporal manifestation occurs at its appointed time.

Key Terms
القيامة (al-qiyama)
الفناء (al-fana')
الساعة (al-sa'a)
التكوين (al-takwin)
الحدث (al-hadath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'Allah creates the resurrection at the hour' means the act of creation itself occurs at that hour.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that such an interpretation would imply a temporal act, which is incompatible with divine eternity. The act of bringing into being (takwin) is an eternal attribute; only its effect occurs in time.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the interpretation that 'the hour' is the time for Allah's bringing the resurrection into being?
A) Because the resurrection is eternal
B) Because it would make the act of bringing into being (takwin) temporal, which is a sign of temporality
C) Because the hour does not exist
D) Because the questioner is mistaken

Key Takeaway
Attributing a time to the divine act of creation (takwin) would compromise Allah's transcendence and imply contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the Day of Judgment, do you unconsciously imagine it as a future event that will happen 'at a time'? How does Maturidi's argument expose your assumption that Allah's act of creation is bound by time like yours are? Will you now consciously train your mind to say 'Allah creates the Hour' without attaching it to a temporal moment—and what practical change in your daily taqwa might follow from this correction?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's takwin is eternal and not limited by time, why do you still find yourself rushing your prayers or delaying repentance as if His commands belong only to certain hours? How can you live today—not tomorrow—with the urgent awareness that His creative act is always present, and your response to Him must be immediate?
?
Maturidi says making time for the divine act is 'a sign of temporality'—a sign of createdness. When you imagine Allah's power, do you secretly measure it by human standards of 'fast' or 'slow'? Will you commit to purifying your concept of Allah's actions from any trace of similarity to created things, starting with your next moment of worship?
