Defending Eternal Attributes: Takwin Without Object
Maturidi answers the objection that takwin without an object implies incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that describing Allah as 'bringer into being' without an existing object suggests incapacity, by distinguishing between eternal attributes and their temporal objects.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are eternal; their objects are temporal.

Maturidi addresses a common objection: if Allah is described as 'bringer into being' without an existing object, does that imply incapacity? He explains that this would only be problematic if the bringing was intended for a specific time and failed. Instead, attributes like takwin, knowledge, and will are eternal, whereas their objects occur at their proper times. This distinction preserves divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection (qīla) followed by the answer (qīla).
2
Identify the key condition
Maturidi says the objection would hold only if takwin was intended for a specific time and failed. Why does he set this condition?
This condition is crucial for understanding the defense.
3
Explore the analogies
He compares takwin to knowledge, will, hearing, sight, generosity, and munificence. Note that all are eternal attributes, while their objects are temporal.
The analogy reinforces the principle.
4
Apply the principle
Explain in your own words why the objection fails according to Maturidi. Use the analogies to support your answer.
This step helps internalize the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: في التكوين ولا مُكوَّنَ إثبات العجز. قيل: إنما يكون ذلك لو كان التكوين ليكون لوقت فلم يكن؛ وكذلك في الإرادة والعلم به إذا لم يكن [فهو] جهل واضطرار. فأما ليكون للوقت الذي يكون فيه فلا على ما بينا من العلم. وعلى ذلك السمع والبصر والكرم والجود، إنه موصوف [بها] في الأزل وإن كان ما يسمع ويبصر وما ذُكر حادثاً. وعلى ذلك جرى الحدوث. ولا بد من ذكر الوقت للمسموع عند ذكر الأمرين، فمثله الأول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If it is said: In takwin without an object there is [an implication of] incapacity. It is said: That would only be if the bringing into being was intended to be at a time and it did not occur; and similarly in will and knowledge, if they are without an object, [that would be] ignorance and constraint. As for it being for the time in which it occurs, it is not so, based on what we explained of knowledge. And upon that are hearing, sight, generosity, and munificence: He is described with them in pre-eternity even though what is heard, seen, and mentioned is temporal. And upon that temporality proceeds. And it is necessary to mention the time for the heard when mentioning the two matters, so its example is the first. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection: if Allah is described as 'bringer into being' (mukawwin) without a presently existing object (mukawwan), that suggests incapacity—because normally creating without an actual creation would be a defect. The answer: such an implication would hold only if the act of bringing into being was intended to occur at a specific time but failed to produce the object. For Allah, however, the act of takwin is for the time when the object will actually exist; the act itself is eternal and does not depend on the temporal presence of the object. Maturidi likens this to divine knowledge and will: if they had no object, that would be ignorance and compulsion, but they do have objects in their proper times. Similarly, Allah is described as hearing and seeing in pre-eternity, even though the objects (what is heard and seen) are temporal. The general rule is that when two matters (the attribute and its object) are mentioned, the time of the object must be stated. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
التكوين (al-takwin)
المكوّن (al-mukawwan)
العجز (al-‘ajz)
السمع (al-sam‘)
البصر (al-basar)
الكرم (al-karam)
الجود (al-jud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A divine attribute without an object at the moment of description is impossible or indicates incapacity.
Correction: Maturidi explains that attributes are eternal and their objects are temporal; the absence of an object at the time of speech does not indicate incapacity, because the attribute is for the time when the object will exist.

Checkpoint
Under what condition would takwin without an object imply incapacity, according to Maturidi?
A) If the takwin is eternal
B) If the takwin was intended to produce an object at a specific time but failed
C) If the object is too great
D) If Allah does not will the object

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends divine attributes by showing they are eternal while their objects are temporal, so the absence of an object at any given moment does not indicate defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
O you who doubt whether Allah is truly Generous when you see no generosity in your life, will you now believe that His attribute of generosity is eternal even before the object of that generosity arrives? What if the object is you, and it has not yet come? Are you willing to wait?
?
If Allah is described as Hearing and Seeing in pre-eternity even before anything was heard or seen, then your prayers and actions are already known to Him. How does this truth change the way you speak and act today, knowing that His attributes of hearing and seeing are not dependent on the existence of what He hears and sees?
?
The objector claimed that an attribute without its object implies incapacity. But Maturidi showed that the attribute is for the time the object will exist. Which area of your life are you tempted to think that Allah's power is somehow 'empty' or 'useless' because you don't see its effect yet? Will you now rely on His eternal attributes even when the object is not yet visible?

The Criterion of Power: Exceeding the Actual
Maturidi defines power and incapacity by the criterion of exceeding the actual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that power is defined by the ability to exceed the actual occurrence, while incapacity is its absence.

Core Idea
Power is not just doing what happens; it is the ability to do otherwise.

Maturidi begins with a foundational distinction: what separates divine power from human limitation? He offers a crisp criterion: if your act cannot go beyond what actually happens, you are incapable. If it can exceed and produce alternatives, you are powerful. This lesson unpacks that principle and its implications for understanding Allah's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: first a general principle, then examples, then application.
Pay attention to the terms 'exceeding the actual' and 'incapacity'.
2
Identify the two descriptions
Maturidi distinguishes between one who does not exceed the actual (incapacity) and one who exceeds it (power). Find the exact phrasing.
3
Reflect on the analogy of opposites
He gives the example of someone who can do a thing and its opposite. Why does this show completeness? Think about what it means to be able to choose between alternatives.
This analogy illustrates the perfection of Allah's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أن الذي لا يعدو الواقع بفعله وقت الوصف له بالفعل [فله] وصف عجز. والذي يعدوه ويقع عنده [فله] وصف قدرة؛ كمن يكون منه فعل الشيء وضده المتمكن منه، إنه أتم من جهة فعله وأجد. وكذلك من لا يعدو فعله حيّزه هو دون من يقع في كل حيز. كذلك وصف الله بالذي ذكرت، إذ هو وصف التمام. مع ما لا يقع فعل العبد لغير وقته لأنه عن شغله بالفعل يكون وبالآلات والله سبحانه بنفسه يفعل؛ وذلك كما علم سبحانه بذاته وقدر بذاته، وكل من سواه [يقدر ويفعل] بغير الذي لولا ذلك ما قام به فعل، والله هو ينشئ من لا شيء؛ لذلك بطل التقدير بالذي قالوا. وعلى مثل ما ذكرت أمر القدرة والإرادة وجميع ما بينا. ودليل آخر أنه يوجد من العبد الفعل المتولد. يقع بعد العبد الفعل المتولّد. يقع بعد الفراغ بأوقات، كالرمي والجنايات، يستحق اسم القاتل والجاني والمصيب بعد انقضاء حقيقة فعله. فمثله مستقيم من الله وإن كان لا يوصف فعله بالطباع والتولد؛ لِمَا أن خروج أحد الوجهين في الشاهد لم يمنع من تحقيق الفعل، فمثله في الغائب وإن لم يكن من ذلك الوجه. على ما بينا من ثبات شيء ليس بجسم، على جواز القول في الله بالشيء وإن لم يكن عَرَضاً، وكل شيء في الشاهد غير جسم فهو عَرَض بحق الوجود لا أن ذلك اسمه؛ فمثله الأول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the principle is that one who does not exceed the actual by his act at the time of describing him with the act has a description of incapacity. And one who exceeds it and it occurs at his behest has a description of power; like one from whom occurs the thing and its opposite, being capable of it, he is more complete and more proficient in his act. And likewise, one whose act does not exceed his own sphere is inferior to one who occurs in every sphere. Likewise the description of Allah with what I mentioned, for it is the description of perfection. Additionally, the act of the servant does not occur at other than its time because he acts by exertion and instruments, while Allah acts by Himself; and that is as He knows by His essence and is powerful by His essence, and everyone other than Him is powerful and acts by something other than which if not for that, no act would subsist in him, and Allah creates from nothing; therefore the estimation according to what they said is invalid. And upon the like of what I mentioned is the matter of power, will, and all that we have explained. And another proof is that there occurs from the servant the generated act. The generated act occurs after the servant. It occurs after completion at times, like shooting and crimes, he deserves the name of killer, criminal, and marksman after the expiration of the reality of his act. So its analogy is sound from Allah even if His act is not described by nature and generation; because the emergence of one of the two aspects in the witnessed does not prevent the realization of the act, so its analogy is in the unseen even if not from that aspect. Upon what we explained of the affirmation of a thing that is not a body, upon the permissibility of saying 'thing' concerning Allah even if it is not an accident, and every thing in the witnessed that is not a body is an accident by right of existence, not that that is its name; so its analogy is the first. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof regarding divine power (qudra) versus incapacity (‘ajz). Maturidi states a principle: one whose act does not exceed the actual occurrence (i.e., he can only produce exactly what happens and nothing beyond) is described as incapable; one whose act exceeds the actual and occurs at his behest (i.e., he can also produce the opposite or more) is described as powerful. He uses an analogy: a person who can produce a thing and its opposite is more complete in his action. Similarly, one whose act is confined to a single sphere is less than one whose acts occur in every sphere. For Allah, this description is one of perfection: He can create and not create, bring into being and annihilate, etc. Maturidi then contrasts human and divine action: human acts are bound by time, exertion, and instruments; Allah acts by His essence without instruments. He creates from nothing, so the objections raised against His power are invalid. He then provides another proof from generated acts (al-fi‘l al-mutawallid): in human experience, acts like shooting or killing generate effects after the act is completed; the agent is called killer even after the causal chain ends. This shows that a delay between act and effect does not negate the attribution of the act. This applies a fortiori to Allah. Finally, Maturidi draws a parallel to the term 'thing' (shay’): in the witnessed world, everything non-corporeal is called an accident (by virtue of existence, not by name), so calling Allah a 'thing' is permissible without implying accident. Thus the analogy holds.

Key Terms
القدرة
العجز
التمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is simply the ability to do what one does.
Correction: True power is the capacity to exceed the actual occurrence, including the ability to do the opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the description of power from the description of incapacity?
A) Power requires doing more than one act; incapacity requires one act.
B) Power is when the act exceeds the actual; incapacity is when the act does not exceed the actual.
C) Power is when the act is immediate; incapacity is when it is delayed.
D) Power is when the act is done without tools; incapacity is when tools are used.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defines power as the capacity to exceed the actual occurrence and produce alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in something, do you unconsciously attribute it to your own skill or effort, forgetting that your very ability to act is created by Allah? How would acknowledging your utter dependence change the way you approach your next task?
?
Maturidi says the one who can produce both a thing and its opposite is more complete. In your daily life, do you exercise your power to choose obedience over disobedience, or do you let your desires dictate your actions? Are you truly 'complete' in your submission?
?
The passage contrasts human action (limited by time and instruments) with divine action (unlimited and essential). Where in your life do you act as if you possess unlimited power, and how can you remind yourself of your true incapacity relative to Allah?

The Proof from Generated Acts
Maturidi argues that generated acts in humans prove the possibility of divine action across time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses generated acts (al-fi‘l al-mutawallid) in human experience to argue for the attribution of action to Allah even when the effect is not immediate.

Core Idea
A delay between act and effect does not disprove authorship.

Maturidi presents a second proof: in human actions like shooting, the effect (death) occurs after the act, yet we still call the person a killer. This shows that an act can be attributed to an agent even if the outcome is temporally separated. This lesson examines that argument and its application to divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the generated act example
Read the passage about the generated act (al-fi‘l al-mutawallid). Notice the example of shooting and crimes.
The generated act occurs after the direct act.
2
Understand the analogy to Allah
Maturidi argues that if in the witnessed world a delay does not negate agency, then similarly in the unseen world (Allah's acts) a delay does not negate His power.
3
Reflect on the refutation of objections
Consider how this argument refutes the objection that if Allah's creation is eternal and the world is temporal, then He cannot be the creator of the temporal world.
The key is that the generated act in humans is a precedent for attributing delayed effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you experienced a delay between your intention and its outcome, and how did that affect your faith in the effectiveness of your actions? Do you now see that this delay does not diminish your responsibility or agency, just as it does not diminish Allah's? What can you learn from this about patience and trust in divine decree?
?
Consider a sin you committed in the past whose consequences are still unfolding. In what ways might you be tempted to disown responsibility because of the time gap? How does Maturidi’s argument that the agent is still called the killer even after the act refute that temptation? Will you today take ownership of your past actions and seek forgiveness wholeheartedly?
?
The analogy of generated acts applies to Allah’s creation despite temporal gaps. If Allah can be the creator over time, what does that imply about His control over the long-term outcomes in your life? Specifically, what area of your future are you most anxious about, and how can you entrust it to the One whose power is not limited by time?

Can Allah Be Called a 'Thing'?
Maturidi justifies applying the term 'thing' to Allah by analogy with accidents in the witnessed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that it is permissible to call Allah a 'thing' (shay') without implying that He is an accident.

Core Idea
Calling Allah a 'thing' is valid because all non-bodies in the witnessed are called accidents, but only by existence.

In the previous discussions, Maturidi established an analogy: in the witnessed world, anything that is not a body is called an accident by virtue of existing. This opens the door to calling Allah a 'thing' without attributing accidenthood to Him. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about 'thing' and accident
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key point: in the witnessed, everything non-body is an accident by right of existence, not by name.
2
Understand the analogy
Maturidi argues that just as we call a non-body an 'accident' in the witnessed based on existence, we can call Allah a 'thing' without making Him an accident.
The analogy is parallel to the earlier one about power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أن الذي لا يعدو الواقع بفعله وقت الوصف له بالفعل [فله] وصف عجز. والذي يعدوه ويقع عنده [فله] وصف قدرة؛ كمن يكون منه فعل الشيء وضده المتمكن منه، إنه أتم من جهة فعله وأجد. وكذلك من لا يعدو فعله حيّزه هو دون من يقع في كل حيز. كذلك وصف الله بالذي ذكرت، إذ هو وصف التمام. مع ما لا يقع فعل العبد لغير وقته لأنه عن شغله بالفعل يكون وبالآلات والله سبحانه بنفسه يفعل؛ وذلك كما علم سبحانه بذاته وقدر بذاته، وكل من سواه [يقدر ويفعل] بغير الذي لولا ذلك ما قام به فعل، والله هو ينشئ من لا شيء؛ لذلك بطل التقدير بالذي قالوا. وعلى مثل ما ذكرت أمر القدرة والإرادة وجميع ما بينا. ودليل آخر أنه يوجد من العبد الفعل المتولد. يقع بعد العبد الفعل المتولّد. يقع بعد الفراغ بأوقات، كالرمي والجنايات، يستحق اسم القاتل والجاني والمصيب بعد انقضاء حقيقة فعله. فمثله مستقيم من الله وإن كان لا يوصف فعله بالطباع والتولد؛ لِمَا أن خروج أحد الوجهين في الشاهد لم يمنع من تحقيق الفعل، فمثله في الغائب وإن لم يكن من ذلك الوجه. على ما بينا من ثبات شيء ليس بجسم، على جواز القول في الله بالشيء وإن لم يكن عَرَضاً، وكل شيء في الشاهد غير جسم فهو عَرَض بحق الوجود لا أن ذلك اسمه؛ فمثله الأول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the principle is that one who does not exceed the actual by his act at the time of describing him with the act has a description of incapacity. And one who exceeds it and it occurs at his behest has a description of power; like one from whom occurs the thing and its opposite, being capable of it, he is more complete and more proficient in his act. And likewise, one whose act does not exceed his own sphere is inferior to one who occurs in every sphere. Likewise the description of Allah with what I mentioned, for it is the description of perfection. Additionally, the act of the servant does not occur at other than its time because he acts by exertion and instruments, while Allah acts by Himself; and that is as He knows by His essence and is powerful by His essence, and everyone other than Him is powerful and acts by something other than which if not for that, no act would subsist in him, and Allah creates from nothing; therefore the estimation according to what they said is invalid. And upon the like of what I mentioned is the matter of power, will, and all that we have explained. And another proof is that there occurs from the servant the generated act. The generated act occurs after the servant. It occurs after completion at times, like shooting and crimes, he deserves the name of killer, criminal, and marksman after the expiration of the reality of his act. So its analogy is sound from Allah even if His act is not described by nature and generation; because the emergence of one of the two aspects in the witnessed does not prevent the realization of the act, so its analogy is in the unseen even if not from that aspect. Upon what we explained of the affirmation of a thing that is not a body, upon the permissibility of saying 'thing' concerning Allah even if it is not an accident, and every thing in the witnessed that is not a body is an accident by right of existence, not that that is its name; so its analogy is the first. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof regarding divine power (qudra) versus incapacity (‘ajz). Maturidi states a principle: one whose act does not exceed the actual occurrence (i.e., he can only produce exactly what happens and nothing beyond) is described as incapable; one whose act exceeds the actual and occurs at his behest (i.e., he can also produce the opposite or more) is described as powerful. He uses an analogy: a person who can produce a thing and its opposite is more complete in his action. Similarly, one whose act is confined to a single sphere is less than one whose acts occur in every sphere. For Allah, this description is one of perfection: He can create and not create, bring into being and annihilate, etc. Maturidi then contrasts human and divine action: human acts are bound by time, exertion, and instruments; Allah acts by His essence without instruments. He creates from nothing, so the objections raised against His power are invalid. He then provides another proof from generated acts (al-fi‘l al-mutawallid): in human experience, acts like shooting or killing generate effects after the act is completed; the agent is called killer even after the causal chain ends. This shows that a delay between act and effect does not negate the attribution of the act. This applies a fortiori to Allah. Finally, Maturidi draws a parallel to the term 'thing' (shay’): in the witnessed world, everything non-corporeal is called an accident (by virtue of existence, not by name), so calling Allah a 'thing' is permissible without implying accident. Thus the analogy holds.

Key Terms
الشيء
العَرَض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling Allah a 'thing' implies He is an accident, which is impermissible.
Correction: Maturidi explains that in the witnessed, all non-bodies are called accidents only by virtue of existence, not by name. So calling Allah a 'thing' is permissible without attributing accidenthood.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it permissible to call Allah a 'thing'?
A) Because Allah is an accident.
B) Because everything non-body in the witnessed is an accident by right of existence, not by name.
C) Because there is no term other than 'thing'.
D) Because the Qur'an uses it.

Key Takeaway
Calling Allah a 'thing' is permissible by analogy with accidents in the witnessed, based on existence not name.

Two Arguments: Incapacity and the Command Analogy
Maturidi presents two additional arguments: one from incapacity and one from the analogy of temporal commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two arguments that defend eternal divine attributes against objections of temporal creation.

Core Idea
Eternal divine attributes do not imply temporal creation, just as commands apply at their time.

Maturidi continues to defend the eternity of divine attributes, here presenting two further arguments. First, he argues that the opponents' view implies incapacity in Allah, because a temporal effect would require temporal causes. Second, he uses the example of commands and prohibitions: just as a command applies at the time of revelation without being pre-existent, so too Allah's attribute of takwin is eternal while the created thing is temporal. This lesson unpacks both arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from one argument to the next.
The passage is incomplete at the end; this is a known textual issue.
2
Identify the first argument: incapacity
Find where Maturidi says 'what they said is a sign of incapacity' and understand the reasoning: if an effect is not simultaneous with the cause, the cause is limited.
3
Identify the second argument: the command analogy
Look for the discussion of commands and prohibitions. Notice how a command applies at the time of revelation even though the command itself is not newly created. This is analogous to takwin.
Relate this to the overarching debate about eternal attributes.
4
Reflect on the combined force
Consider how both arguments together support the view that Allah's attributes are eternal, even if their effects are temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الذي قالوا أمارة العجز، إذ لا يقدر العبد على ما لا يتحقق مفعوله معه، كما لا يقدر عليه دون استعمال نفسه بالتحريك والتسكين. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإنه لا أحد أبى القول بأنه مأمور منهى في وقته من غير مجيئ أمرٍ في هذا الوقت، وكذلك الوعد والوعيد، فيصير بالمنزل على رسول الله له هو للحال مأموراً منهياً. ما ينكر أن يكون هو للحال كائناً بالتكوين في الأزل. وكذلك الله سبحانه يوصف بكل كائن أنه
And also, what they said is a sign of incapacity, because the servant is not capable of that whose object is not realized with him, just as he is not capable of it without employing himself by moving and stilling. There is no power except with Allah. And after that, there is no one who denies the statement that he is commanded and prohibited at his time without an command coming at this time, and similarly promise and threat, so he becomes, by the revelation to the Messenger of Allah, for him now commanded and prohibited. What denies that he is now existing by the bringing into being in pre-eternity. And likewise Allah is described with every existent that it is...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage contains two additional arguments. First, the opponents' claim (presumably that divine creation is temporal or that Allah's attributes are not eternal) is a sign of incapacity, because a human being cannot achieve an act whose effect is not realized simultaneously; he must move and still himself. This implies that if Allah's creation were dependent on time as the effect, it would be a limitation. Second, Maturidi uses the example of commands and prohibitions: nobody denies that a person is commanded and prohibited at the time of receiving the revelation, even though the command did not exist before. The revelation makes him commanded at that moment. Similarly, it is not objectionable to say that Allah is 'existing by the bringing into being in pre-eternity' (i.e., the divine act of creation is eternal, and the created thing exists at its time). The text ends mid-sentence, but the analogy is clear: just as a command is applied at the time of revelation without being pre-existent, so too Allah's attribute of takwin is eternal and the creature exists at its appointed time. The passage concludes with 'Allah is described with every existent that it is...' likely continuing that every created thing is described as brought into being at its proper time.

Key Terms
العجز (al-‘ajz)
المأمور (al-ma'mur)
المنهي (al-munha)
الوعد (al-wa'd)
الوعيد (al-wa'id)
المنزل (al-munazzal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah's command and creation are eternal, then the commanded act must be eternal too.
Correction: Maturidi shows that commands are applied at the time of revelation, analogously to creation: the command is eternal in divine knowledge but applies temporally.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to support the idea that Allah's creation (takwin) can be eternal while the creature is temporal?
A) The example of a human being who builds a house
B) The example of commands and prohibitions being applied at the time of revelation
C) The example of shooting an arrow
D) The example of the sun giving light

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends eternal divine attributes by arguing that the opponents' view leads to absurdity (incapacity) and by drawing an analogy from temporal commands to eternal takwin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live each day as though Allah's commands (أمر) apply to you right now, or do you postpone obedience as if the command is not yet in force? Commit to one specific command you have been delaying and act on it today.
?
If you claim that Allah's creation (تكوين) is eternal in His knowledge yet manifests in time, do you accept His timing in your own life—even when His plan unfolds slower or differently than you wish? What is one area you need to surrender to His timeline rather than your own?
?
The argument from incapacity warns against attributing human limitations to Allah. Search your heart: do you sometimes think of Allah as bound by your understanding of justice, mercy, or timing? Repent of any mental image that confines the Infinite, and ask Him to reveal His true majesty.

The Perfection of Divine Power
Maturidi contrasts human and divine power to prove God's perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi establishes the perfection of God's power by contrasting it with human power, which is limited by sphere and instruments.

Core Idea
God's power is perfect and unlimited, unlike human power.

In this opening part of his argument, Imam al-Maturidi sets the stage by highlighting the contrast between human and divine power. He argues that God's attribute of creation (takwin) is perfect because it is not limited like human acts. The passage uses the example of a person who can act and its opposite to illustrate completeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of Maturidi's concise style. Notice how he contrasts human and divine power.
2
Identify the contrast
Look for the comparison between the one who can do a thing and its opposite (perfection) and the one whose act does not exceed his sphere (inferiority). Understand that God is described by the perfect side.
3
Understand the proof of perfection
Maturidi argues that God's act is perfect because it is not dependent on instruments or time, unlike human acts. This supports the reality of takwin as an attribute of perfection.

His act — and that which goes beyond it and occurs with him — has the description of power; like one from whom the act of a thing and its opposite that he is capable of occurs, he is more perfect in terms of his act and more complete. And with God is success. Indeed, the meaning of takwin (divine bringing into existence), even if human understanding cannot reach it, can be expressed by the simplest statement that can bear it: by the saying "Be." Everything is upon what He knows that it will be, and it comes to be by it, making everything be upon what its being is at the time of its being without repetition. And into it enters all command and prohibition, promise and threat, and it becomes information about what exists and what will be, upon the differing states of existents at their times and places forever; but the capacity of creation is not capable of grasping the takwin that does not occupy nor tire. And there is no power except with God. And this is a topic that, if one pursued it fully, would distract from reaching the end to the intended goal, and we hope that what we have indicated is sufficient for the one of intellect and understanding.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument from Imam al-Maturidi establishing the reality of the divine attribute of creation (takwin). It begins by contrasting the perfection of God's power with human power: human acts are confined to a specific sphere and require instruments, whereas God's act is not so limited. The author then presents a second proof from generated acts (al-fi'l al-mutawallad). In human experience, an act like throwing an arrow is attributed to the agent even after the immediate act; the agent is called killer even though the death occurs later. Similarly, God's creation is real even if it is not temporally immediate. Maturidi then argues against those who deny this attribute by noting that their position implies insufficiency: a servant cannot produce an effect without his action being realized. He further draws an analogy with command and prohibition: a command given in the past applies now, so likewise takwin (divine bringing into existence) is eternal yet applies to temporal things. Finally, he explains the meaning of takwin: it is encompassed by the command 'Be' (kun), and it brings everything into existence at its proper time without repetition. The passage concludes that this is a deep topic, and the brief treatment is sufficient for the intelligent.

Key Terms
التكوين (takwin)
القدرة (power)
التمام (perfection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) God's power requires instruments to act.

Key Takeaway
God's power is perfect because it is unlimited in sphere and independent of instruments, unlike human power.

If God's power is perfect and independent, why do you still worry about outcomes as if they rest on your shoulders? Will you today surrender one specific worry to Him, acknowledging that His power is not limited by your circumstances?

Proof from Generated Acts
Maturidi proves takwin using the analogy of human generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the analogy of generated acts (like throwing an arrow) to prove that God's act of creation is real even if the effect is not temporally immediate.

Core Idea
Just as a thrower is called a killer after death, God's creation is real even if not instantaneous.

Maturidi presents a second proof for the reality of takwin using generated acts (al-fi'l al-mutawallad). In human experience, an act like throwing an arrow is attributed to the agent even after the immediate act; the agent is called killer even though the death occurs later. Similarly, God's creation is real even if it is not temporally immediate. The passage also responds to a potential objection by showing that denying this leads to the impossibility of generated acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the generated act example
Read the passage focusing on the example of throwing: how the person deserves the name 'killer' after the fact. Understand the structure of the argument.
2
Apply the analogy to God
Maturidi argues that if this holds in the witnessed world, then it is valid to say that God's creation (takwin) occurs even if the effect appears later in time.
3
Consider the rebuttal
The passage also refutes the objection that God's act cannot be like generated acts because it is not temporal; Maturidi responds that the principle of attribution does not require temporal immediacy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعله والذي يعدوه ويقع عنده [فله] وصف قدرة؛ كمن يكون منه فعل الشيء وضده المتمكن منه، إنه أتم من جهة فعله وأجد . وكذلك من لا يعدو فعْلُه حيّزه هو دونَ من يقع في كل حيز. كذلك وصف الله بالذي ذكرت، إذ هو وصف التمام. مع ما لا يقع فعل العبد لغير وقته لأنه عن شغله بالفعل يكون وبالآلات والله سبحانه بنفسه يفعل ؛ وذلك كما علم سبحانه بذاته وقدر بذاته، وكل من سواه [ يقدر ويفعل ] بغير الذي لولا ذلك ما قام به فعل، والله هو ينشئ من لا شيء؛ لذلك بطل التقدير بالذي قالوا. وعلى مثل ما ذكرت أمر القدرة والإرادة وجميع ما بينا . و دليل آخر أنه يوجد من العبد الفعل المتولد. يقع بعد العبد الفعل المتولّد. يقع بعد الفراغ بأوقات، كالرمي والجنايات، يستحق اسم القاتل والجاني والمصيب بعد انقضاء حقيقة فعله. فمثله مستقيم من الله وإن كان لا يوصف فعله بالطباع والتولد ؛ لِمَا أن خروج أحد الوجهين في الشاهد لم يمنع من تحقيق الفعل، فمثله في الغائب وإن لم يكن من ذلك الوجه " . على ما بينا من ثبات " شيء ليس بجسم، على جواز القول في الله بالشيء وإن لم يكن عَرَضاً، وكل شيء في الشاهد غير جسم فهو عَرَض بحق الوجود لا أن ذلك اسمه ؛ فمثله الأول " . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضاً إن الذي قالوا أمارة العجز، إذ لا يقدر العبد على ما لا يتحقق مفعوله معه، كما لا يقدر عليه دون استعمال نفسه بالتحريك والتسكين. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد، فإنه لا أحد أبى القول بأنه مأمور منهى في وقته من غير مجيئ أمرٍ في هذا الوقت، وكذلك الوعد والوعيد، فيصير بالمنزل على رسول الله له هو للحال مأمورًا منهياً . ما ينكر أن يكون هو للحال كائنا بالتكوين في الأزل. وكذلك الله سبحانه يوصف بكل كائن أنه عالم به كائنا، وإن كان / يُوصف من قبل بعلمه، والكون والحدث كله على الكائن دونه. وبالله التوفيق. على أن معنى التكوين، وإن كان لا يبلغه فهم البشر ، لأمكن الأداء بأيسر قول يحتمله : من القول بـ كُن . كل شيء على ما علم أنه يكون فيكون به، مكوناً كل شيء على ما عليه كونه في وقت كونه من غير تكرار . وفيه يدخل الأمر كله والنّهى والوعد والوعيد، ويصير إخبارا عن كائن وعما يكون على اختلاف أحوال الكائنات بأوقاتها وأمكنتها أبدا؛ لكن وسع الخلق لا يحتمل دَرْك التكوين الذي لا يُشغل ولا يتعب . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وهذا باب لو استقصى فيه لشغل عن بلوغ النهاية إلى المقصود، ونرجو أن يكون فيما أشرنا إليه مقنع لذي اللب والفهم.
His act — and that which goes beyond it and occurs with him — has the description of power; like one from whom the act of a thing and its opposite that he is capable of occurs, he is more perfect in terms of his act and more complete. And likewise, one whose act does not exceed his own sphere is inferior to one who occurs in every sphere. Thus God is described by what you mentioned, for it is the description of perfection. Along with the fact that the act of the servant does not occur outside its time because it is through his occupation with the act and through instruments, while God, exalted, acts by Himself; and that is as He, exalted, knows by His essence and has power by His essence, and everyone other than Him has power and acts by something without which no act would occur from him, while God creates from nothing; therefore the estimation of what they said is invalid. And upon similar to what I mentioned is the matter of power, will, and all that we have explained. And another proof: that from the servant occurs the generated act. It occurs after the servant the generated act. It occurs after vacating by times, like throwing and injuries, deserving the name of killer, injurer, and accurate shooter after the end of the reality of his act. So its equivalent is valid from God, even if His act is not described by nature and generation; because the emergence of one of the two aspects in the witnessed does not prevent the realization of the act, so its equivalent in the unseen, even if it is not from that aspect. Upon what we have explained of the establishment of something that is not a body, on the permissibility of saying about God "thing" even if it is not an accident, and everything in the witnessed that is not a body is an accident by the right of existence, not that that is its name; so its first equivalent. And there is no power except with God. And also, what they said is a sign of incapacity, since the servant cannot do what his effect is not realized with him, just as he cannot do it without applying himself by moving and stilling. And there is no power except with God. And further, there is no one who refutes the statement that he is commanded and prohibited in his time without the coming of a command at this time, and likewise the promise and the threat, so it becomes by what was sent down to the Messenger of God that he is now commanded and prohibited. What prevents that he is now existent by the takwin (eternal bringing into existence) in pre-eternity? And likewise God, exalted, is described with every existent that He is knowing of it as existent, although He is described beforehand by His knowledge, and all existence and occurrence are upon the existent besides Him. And with God is success. Indeed, the meaning of takwin (divine bringing into existence), even if human understanding cannot reach it, can be expressed by the simplest statement that can bear it: by the saying "Be." Everything is upon what He knows that it will be, and it comes to be by it, making everything be upon what its being is at the time of its being without repetition. And into it enters all command and prohibition, promise and threat, and it becomes information about what exists and what will be, upon the differing states of existents at their times and places forever; but the capacity of creation is not capable of grasping the takwin that does not occupy nor tire. And there is no power except with God. And this is a topic that, if one pursued it fully, would distract from reaching the end to the intended goal, and we hope that what we have indicated is sufficient for the one of intellect and understanding.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument from Imam al-Maturidi establishing the reality of the divine attribute of creation (takwin). It begins by contrasting the perfection of God's power with human power: human acts are confined to a specific sphere and require instruments, whereas God's act is not so limited. The author then presents a second proof from generated acts (al-fi'l al-mutawallad). In human experience, an act like throwing an arrow is attributed to the agent even after the immediate act; the agent is called killer even though the death occurs later. Similarly, God's creation is real even if it is not temporally immediate. Maturidi then argues against those who deny this attribute by noting that their position implies insufficiency: a servant cannot produce an effect without his action being realized. He further draws an analogy with command and prohibition: a command given in the past applies now, so likewise takwin (divine bringing into existence) is eternal yet applies to temporal things. Finally, he explains the meaning of takwin: it is encompassed by the command 'Be' (kun), and it brings everything into existence at its proper time without repetition. The passage concludes that this is a deep topic, and the brief treatment is sufficient for the intelligent.

Key Terms
الفعل المتولد (generated act)
الرمي (throwing)
القاتل (killer)
الغائب (the unseen)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's act of creation must be temporally immediate, like a human direct act.
Correction: Maturidi shows that in the witnessed world, generated acts prove that an act can be attributed to an agent even if the effect comes later. Thus, God's takwin is real even if its effects occur in time.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi use to prove that an act can be attributed even if its effect comes later?
A) Building a house
B) Throwing an arrow and being called a killer after death
C) Writing a book
D) Giving birth

Key Takeaway
Generated acts show that attribution does not require temporal immediacy; so God's takwin is real even if effects appear later.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something and don't see an immediate result, do you doubt that God is truly acting? How does this lesson's analogy of the thrower and the delayed death challenge that doubt?
?
If God's takwin is real even before the created thing appears, what does that imply about His constant creative involvement in your life right now? How can you live today with that awareness?
?
Are you tempted to judge the reality of God's actions by your own timeline? What would it look like for you to trust that God's 'throwing' is already effective, even if the 'death' (outcome) is delayed?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

The Eternal Command 'Be' and the Meaning of Takwin
Maturidi explains takwin as the eternal command 'Be' and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains the meaning of takwin as the eternal command 'Be' (kun) and how it encompasses all temporal events without repetition.

Core Idea
Takwin is the eternal command 'Be'; it brings everything into existence at its proper time without repetition.

In the final part of the argument, Maturidi addresses the nature of takwin itself. He draws an analogy with divine commands and prohibitions: just as a past command applies in the present, so takwin in pre-eternity brings things into existence at their appointed times. He then explains that takwin is best understood through the simple word 'Be' (kun), which brings everything into existence exactly as God knows it, without need for repetition. This passage concludes by acknowledging the depth of the topic but affirming that the explanation suffices for the intelligent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the command analogy
Read the part where Maturidi compares takwin to divine commands: a command given in the past applies now. Similarly, takwin in pre-eternity makes things exist now.
2
Learn the meaning of 'Be'
Maturidi explains that takwin can be expressed by the command 'Be' (kun). This shows that God's bringing into existence is instantaneous and without effort.
3
Appreciate the scope
The command 'Be' encompasses all events, including commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats. It informs about all existents at their times and places forever, without repetition.

Divine Attributes

Al-Ka'bi's First Criterion: Variation by State or Person
Al-Ka'bi's first criterion for classifying attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's first criterion for distinguishing divine attributes: whether an attribute can vary according to state or person.

Core Idea
Attributes that change with state or person are actions; those that don't are essence.

Al-Ka'bi, a leading Mu'tazilite theologian, proposed a classification of divine attributes based on their susceptibility to variation. He argued that attributes like mercy and speech vary with circumstance, while power, knowledge, and life do not. This distinction is foundational for the Mu'tazilite denial of eternal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage where al-Ka'bi presents his first criterion. Notice how he uses examples to illustrate what he means by 'variation in state and person.'
The passage uses the examples of providing, mercy, and speech for attributes of action.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two categories: attributes of action (صفات الفعل) and attributes of essence (صفات الذات). Note the defining feature: ability to vary.
Power, knowledge, and life are given as attributes of essence.
3
Reflect on the criterion's implication
Ask yourself: why does al-Ka'bi think variation is a valid criterion? Think about what it means for an attribute to be 'action' versus 'essence' in Mu'tazilite thought.
This connects to the broader Mu'tazilite view that attributes of action are created, not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة آراء الكعبي في صفات الذات وصفات الفعل والرد عليها. ونذكر بعض ما ذكر الكعبي لتعلموا مبلغه في معرفة الله والعلم به، فيكون في ذلك الإحاطة بمبلغ مذهب الاعتزال؛ إذ هو عندهم كان إمام أهل الأرض. ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال: ما احتمل اختلاف الحال والشخص فهو صفة الفعل، نحو القول: يرزق فلانًا، ويرحم في حال ولا يرحم في حال، وكذلك صفة الكلام في الأحوال ومثله في الأشخاص. ومثله في القدرة والعلم والحياة لا يُحتمل، فهو صفة الذات. والثاني قال: كل ما يقع عليه القدرة فهو صفة الفعل نحو الرحمة والكلام وما لا يقع عليه فهو صفة الذات أن لا يقال: أيقدر أن يعلم أو لا؟. ثم يسأل عن صفة الذات أنه لم لا يجب الوصف بضده؟ قال: لأنه يرجع إلى ذاته وذاته غير مختلف، وذلك يوجب الاختلاف. ثم قال: وإذا كان ذاته غير مختلف لم يجز الاختلاف ما بقيت نفسه، كالشيء الذي يجب لعِلّة يدوم بدوامها. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: ومن قوله: أن ليس لله في الحقيقة صفة، وإنما هو نحو
A question: The opinions of al-Ka'bi on attributes of essence and attributes of action, and the refutation of them. And we mention some of what al-Ka'bi mentioned so that you may know his attainment in knowledge of God and knowing Him, so that there may be in that an encompassing of the extent of the Mu'tazilite school; for he was, according to them, the imam of the people of the earth. And there is no power except with God. He said: Whatever admits of difference of state and person is an attribute of action, like the saying: He provides for so-and-so, and He has mercy in one state and does not have mercy in another, and likewise the attribute of speech in states and its like in persons. And as for power, knowledge, and life, they do not admit of difference, so they are attributes of essence. And the second [criterion] he said: Whatever falls under power is an attribute of action, like mercy and speech, and whatever does not fall under power is an attribute of essence, such that it cannot be said: Can He know or not? Then he was asked about the attribute of essence: why is it not necessary to describe it by its opposite? He said: Because it refers back to His essence, and His essence is not different, and that would necessitate difference. Then he said: And if His essence is not different, difference is not permissible as long as His self remains, like a thing that is necessary for a cause that endures as long as it endures. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And among his statements is that God in reality has no attribute, but rather it is like...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the views of al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazilite theologian, on the classification of divine attributes, followed by the beginning of Maturidi's refutation. Al-Ka'bi distinguishes two criteria: first, attributes that admit of variation according to state or person (like providing, mercy, speech) are attributes of action; attributes that do not vary (like power, knowledge, life) are attributes of essence. Second, he says that whatever falls under God's power (i.e., can be created or not) is an attribute of action, while what does not (like knowledge, which cannot be subject to power) is an attribute of essence. He then defends this by arguing that attributes of essence cannot have opposites because essence is unchanging. Maturidi's refutation begins by pointing out that al-Ka'bi's scheme ultimately leads to the denial of real attributes for God, because he reduces attributes of action to mere relative descriptions and attributes of essence to negative or identity statements. The passage cuts off, but it sets up the critique that the Mu'tazilite position is inconsistent with affirming that God truly has attributes.

Key Terms
صفات الذات (attributes of essence)
صفات الفعل (attributes of action)
القدرة (power)
العلم (knowledge)
الحياة (life)
الكلام (speech)
الرحمة (mercy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi's first criterion, which of the following would be an attribute of action?
A) Knowledge
B) Life
C) Mercy
D) Power

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi uses the ability to vary as a test: if an attribute can differ with state or person, it is an action; otherwise, it belongs to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ka'bi uses variation as a test. Think of a time you felt God's mercy was absent. Does that moment imply God's mercy is not an essential part of who He is, or does it reveal something about His actions towards you at that state? How does this distinction comfort or unsettle you?
?
You freely call God 'All-Knowing', but do you live as if His knowledge truly never changes—that He knows your every secret, even your worst moments, and still loves you? If His knowledge is essential, what does that mean for your hope in repentance? Are you willing to let that unchanging knowledge expose your hidden sins today?

Al-Ka'bi's Second Criterion: Falling Under Divine Power
Al-Ka'bi's second criterion: power as a test

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's second criterion: attributes that fall under God's power are actions, while those that do not are attributes of essence.

Core Idea
An attribute is an action if God can bring it about or not.

Al-Ka'bi provides a second, distinct criterion based on the scope of divine power. He argues that attributes like mercy and speech are subject to God's will and can be enacted or withheld, while attributes like knowledge are not subject to such causation. This further refines his classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage where al-Ka'bi presents his second criterion. Notice how it differs from the first: instead of variation, it focuses on what God can cause.
The phrase 'يقع عليه القدرة' (fall under power) is key.
2
Compare the two criteria
Compare the examples from lesson 1 with this new criterion. Mercy and speech appear in both, but now the reasoning is different: they are actions because God can will their existence or non-existence.
3
Understand what is excluded
Knowledge cannot be subject to power: one cannot ask 'Can He know or not?' because knowledge is necessarily present. This makes it an attribute of essence.
Al-Ka'bi is implying that what is necessary for God cannot be willed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة آراء الكعبي في صفات الذات وصفات الفعل والرد عليها. ونذكر بعض ما ذكر الكعبي لتعلموا مبلغه في معرفة الله والعلم به، فيكون في ذلك الإحاطة بمبلغ مذهب الاعتزال؛ إذ هو عندهم كان إمام أهل الأرض. ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال: ما احتمل اختلاف الحال والشخص فهو صفة الفعل، نحو القول: يرزق فلانًا، ويرحم في حال ولا يرحم في حال، وكذلك صفة الكلام في الأحوال ومثله في الأشخاص. ومثله في القدرة والعلم والحياة لا يُحتمل، فهو صفة الذات. والثاني قال: كل ما يقع عليه القدرة فهو صفة الفعل نحو الرحمة والكلام وما لا يقع عليه فهو صفة الذات أن لا يقال: أيقدر أن يعلم أو لا؟. ثم يسأل عن صفة الذات أنه لم لا يجب الوصف بضده؟ قال: لأنه يرجع إلى ذاته وذاته غير مختلف، وذلك يوجب الاختلاف. ثم قال: وإذا كان ذاته غير مختلف لم يجز الاختلاف ما بقيت نفسه، كالشيء الذي يجب لعِلّة يدوم بدوامها. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: ومن قوله: أن ليس لله في الحقيقة صفة، وإنما هو نحو
A question: The opinions of al-Ka'bi on attributes of essence and attributes of action, and the refutation of them. And we mention some of what al-Ka'bi mentioned so that you may know his attainment in knowledge of God and knowing Him, so that there may be in that an encompassing of the extent of the Mu'tazilite school; for he was, according to them, the imam of the people of the earth. And there is no power except with God. He said: Whatever admits of difference of state and person is an attribute of action, like the saying: He provides for so-and-so, and He has mercy in one state and does not have mercy in another, and likewise the attribute of speech in states and its like in persons. And as for power, knowledge, and life, they do not admit of difference, so they are attributes of essence. And the second [criterion] he said: Whatever falls under power is an attribute of action, like mercy and speech, and whatever does not fall under power is an attribute of essence, such that it cannot be said: Can He know or not? Then he was asked about the attribute of essence: why is it not necessary to describe it by its opposite? He said: Because it refers back to His essence, and His essence is not different, and that would necessitate difference. Then he said: And if His essence is not different, difference is not permissible as long as His self remains, like a thing that is necessary for a cause that endures as long as it endures. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And among his statements is that God in reality has no attribute, but rather it is like...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the views of al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazilite theologian, on the classification of divine attributes, followed by the beginning of Maturidi's refutation. Al-Ka'bi distinguishes two criteria: first, attributes that admit of variation according to state or person (like providing, mercy, speech) are attributes of action; attributes that do not vary (like power, knowledge, life) are attributes of essence. Second, he says that whatever falls under God's power (i.e., can be created or not) is an attribute of action, while what does not (like knowledge, which cannot be subject to power) is an attribute of essence. He then defends this by arguing that attributes of essence cannot have opposites because essence is unchanging. Maturidi's refutation begins by pointing out that al-Ka'bi's scheme ultimately leads to the denial of real attributes for God, because he reduces attributes of action to mere relative descriptions and attributes of essence to negative or identity statements. The passage cuts off, but it sets up the critique that the Mu'tazilite position is inconsistent with affirming that God truly has attributes.

Key Terms
القدرة (power)
الرحمة (mercy)
الكلام (speech)
العلم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi's second criterion, which of the following is an attribute of essence?
A) Mercy
B) Speech
C) Knowledge
D) Will

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's second criterion uses divine power as a litmus test: if an attribute can be voluntarily enacted, it is an action; otherwise it is essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ka'bi argues that mercy is an attribute of action because God can choose whether to show it. When you think about God's mercy in your life, do you take it for granted as a given, or do you recognize it as a deliberate act of His will? How should this affect your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
The passage asserts that knowledge is an essential attribute—God cannot not know. Reflect on a time you acted as if God were unaware of your thoughts or actions. How does affirming His omniscience change your perspective on that situation? What will you do differently now?
?
If speech is an attribute of action (God can speak or remain silent), then the Qur'an is His spoken word brought into being by His power. Does your reverence for the Qur'an reflect this truth? How can you deepen your engagement with it knowing it is a deliberate act of divine communication?

Al-Ka'bi's Defense and Maturidi's Refutation Opening
Al-Ka'bi's defense and Maturidi's opening critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's defense of his classification based on the immutability of essence, and Maturidi's initial critique that this leads to denying real attributes.

Core Idea
If essence is unchanging, attributes of essence cannot have opposites – but this ultimately denies attributes reality.

Al-Ka'bi is asked why attributes of essence cannot be described by their opposites. He argues that since essence is unchanging, it cannot admit of difference; thus attributes of essence are necessary and immutable. Maturidi begins his refutation by pointing out that this framework, when followed through, actually denies that God has any real attributes at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialogue
Read the passage where al-Ka'bi is asked a question and responds. Notice the logic connecting essence's immutability to the impossibility of opposite attributes.
The question is: why must we not describe the attribute of essence by its opposite?
2
Understand al-Ka'bi's reasoning
Al-Ka'bi argues that if essence is one and unchanging, then its attributes must also be unchanging; attributing an opposite would introduce variation. He compares it to a thing caused by a cause that lasts as long as the cause lasts.
This is a form of argument from the unity and simplicity of God.
3
Identify Maturidi's critique
Maturidi begins his refutation by noting that al-Ka'bi's position ultimately means God has no real attribute in reality. The Arabic cuts off, but he sets up the critique that Mu'tazilites reduce attributes to mere descriptions.
Maturidi's comment shows that al-Ka'bi's classification is a step toward denying the reality of attributes.

Source Text

?
Al-Ka'bi's reasoning seems airtight: if essence is unchanging, attributes cannot have opposites. But Maturidi exposes the disastrous implication. In your own faith, are you willing to accept a perfectly logical conclusion if it contradicts what you know about Allah from revelation? Where is the line between reason and faith for you?
?
The debate here is about the nature of God. If you were asked to defend the reality of Allah's attributes, could you do so without falling into the trap of denying them altogether? What steps will you take to ensure your understanding remains sound?

The Unity of Essence and Action Attributes in Description
Maturidi states the unity of describing God with essence and action attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that describing God with essence and action attributes is fundamentally the same act of description, despite the different names of the attributes.

Core Idea
Describing God with any attribute is one unified act.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing that when we describe God with attributes like knowledge, power, and action, the act of description itself is identical. Regardless of whether the attribute pertains to essence or action, the reality of describing God is the same. This sets the foundation for understanding how divine attributes are spoken of without distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice that the passage uses 'all' in 'the two matters are both found'.
2
Identify the two types of attributes
The passage refers to attributes of knowledge and power (essence) and action (act). See how they are described together.
The phrase 'without any difference from the perspective of description' is key.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If describing God with essence and action attributes is the same, what does that say about the reality of these attributes?
The answer is that their reality goes back to agreed-upon principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وجد الأمران جميعاً في وصف الواصفين إذا وصفوه بالعلم والقدرة والفعل على غير اختلاف من حيث الوصف، ثم سُمّي هو في الحقيقة عالما خالقاً قادرًا في التحقيق؛ فلا وجه لتفريقه من حيث الوصف، إذ حقيقتهما يرجع إلى ما فيه الوفاق. ثم قد يُقال: سمع دعاء فلان ولم يسمع دعاء فلان؛ ويقول الرجل: ما علم الله ذلك ويقول: علم مني في وقت كذا ولم يعلم مني في وقت كذا؛ ثم لم يجب به أن السمع والعلم لا يكونان من صفات الذات، فما مَنَع ذلك في التكليم والرحمة؟ فإن قال: يريد نفي المعلوم والمسموع قيل له: كذلك في الأول، يريد نفي فرعون من بره وإكرامه بذكر نفي الكلام، وهو شيء يريد به بره، وذلك معروف بما بشر المؤمنين بالكلام وأيأس الكفار، وذلك عندنا على ذلك. وبعد، فإن المسألة ساقطة، لأنه علق الحكم بجواز القول، وقد بينا ثَمَّ المسألة. قد عرفنا بما سبق أن لا يجوز أن يوصف الله بحادث؛ ولو جاز ذلك لجاز الوصف بمصلح ومفسد وخير وشرير، وذاك باطل. فثبت أنه لا بما ظن يوصف. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إن غير الصوت لا يتكلم فيه بـ «يسمع»، وجائز أن يتكلم فيه بـ «يعلم»؛ ثم لم يجب التفريق بينهما بالاختلاف في حرف الإثبات، ولم يوجب في ذاته اختلافا؛ فما منع ذلك في حق النفي؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The two matters are both found in the description of describers when they describe Him with knowledge, power, and act without any difference from the perspective of description, then He is truly called knowing, creating, powerful in reality; so there is no basis for distinguishing them from the perspective of description, since their reality goes back to what is agreed upon. Then it may be said: He heard the prayer of so-and-so and did not hear the prayer of so-and-so; and a man says: God did not know that, and says: He knew from me at a certain time and did not know from me at a certain time; then it does not require that hearing and knowledge are not from the attributes of essence, so what prevents that in the case of speech and mercy? If he says: he intends the negation of the known and the heard, it is said to him: likewise in the first, he intends the negation of Pharaoh from His kindness and honoring by mentioning the negation of speech, and it is something by which He intends to honor him, and that is known by what He gave glad tidings to the believers of speech and disappointed the disbelievers, and that with us is upon that. Moreover, the question is invalid, because he attached the judgment to the permissibility of the statement, and we have explained the matter there. We have known from what preceded that it is not permissible to describe God with an accident; if that were permissible, then description with a reformer and corrupter, good and evil, would be permissible, and that is false. So it is established that He is not described with what he thought. And there is no power except with God. Also, something other than sound is not spoken of with 'He hears', but it is permissible to speak of it with 'He knows'; then the distinction between them by the difference in the letter of affirmation did not necessitate a difference in His essence; so what prevents that in the case of negation? And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the relationship between attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). It begins by stating that both types are used to describe God without any difference in the act of description itself—He is called Knowing, Creating, Powerful. The reality of both goes back to the same agreement. Then an objection is raised: people say God heard one person's prayer but not another, implying hearing is not an essential attribute. The response is that such statements negate only the object (the heard or known thing), not the attribute itself. Similarly, when the Qur'an says God does not speak to Pharaoh, it means He does not honor him with speech. The objector's question is invalid because it assumes a false premise. The passage then reiterates that God cannot be described by an accident (ḥādith), as that would lead to describing Him with reformer/corrupter, etc. Finally, another point is made: something other than sound is not spoken of with 'yusma' (He hears) but can be with 'yu'lam' (He knows); this difference in linguistic usage does not imply a difference in the divine essence. The same logic applies to negation of attributes.

Key Terms
صفة الذات
صفة الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about describing God with knowledge and power versus action?
A) They are different in the act of description.
B) There is no difference in the act of description.
C) Knowledge and power are temporal, action is eternal.
D) Action attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The act of describing God with any attribute, whether of essence or action, is unified in its reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you ever treat His mercy as somehow less 'real' than His power? The teaching says all descriptions are one unified act of affirming perfection. How does this challenge your tendency to rank attributes in your heart?
?
If describing Allah with any attribute is one unified act, then your description of Allah as 'Creator' is no different in reality from describing Him as 'Knowing'. Does your heart feel the same awe when you say 'Allah is Knowing' as when you say 'Allah is Creator'? What does your answer reveal about the consistency of your worship?
?
The passage says there is 'no basis for distinguishing them from the perspective of description'. Are there any attributes of Allah that you find easier to accept or dwell on than others? What would it mean to describe Allah with all attributes without distinction in your daily remembrance?

Responding to the Objection About Specific Hearing and Knowledge
Maturidi refutes an objection about temporal hearing/knowledge and argues against accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that temporal expressions of hearing and knowledge imply those attributes are accidental, and why describing God with accidents is impossible.

Core Idea
Negating a specific object does not negate the eternal attribute.

After establishing the unity of description, Maturidi addresses a subtle objection: if God 'hears' one prayer but not another, does that mean hearing is a temporal act? He responds that such language only negates the object, not the attribute itself. The same applies to speech and mercy. He then argues that describing God with accidents is false and uses a linguistic point to further refute the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
Read the part where someone says 'God heard X but not Y' and Maturidi's response that this negates the object, not the attribute.
The example of Pharaoh shows negation of honor, not attribute.
2
Understand the invalidity of the objection
Maturidi states the objection is invalid because it attaches a judgment to a permissible statement without basis.
He also reiterates that God cannot be described with an accident.
3
Examine the linguistic argument
Notice that things other than sound are not described as 'heard' but can be 'known', yet this does not imply a difference in essence.
This further supports that differences in language do not affect the nature of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وجد الأمران جميعاً في وصف الواصفين إذا وصفوه بالعلم والقدرة والفعل على غير اختلاف من حيث الوصف، ثم سُمّي هو في الحقيقة عالما خالقاً قادرًا في التحقيق؛ فلا وجه لتفريقه من حيث الوصف، إذ حقيقتهما يرجع إلى ما فيه الوفاق. ثم قد يُقال: سمع دعاء فلان ولم يسمع دعاء فلان؛ ويقول الرجل: ما علم الله ذلك ويقول: علم مني في وقت كذا ولم يعلم مني في وقت كذا؛ ثم لم يجب به أن السمع والعلم لا يكونان من صفات الذات، فما مَنَع ذلك في التكليم والرحمة؟ فإن قال: يريد نفي المعلوم والمسموع قيل له: كذلك في الأول، يريد نفي فرعون من بره وإكرامه بذكر نفي الكلام، وهو شيء يريد به بره، وذلك معروف بما بشر المؤمنين بالكلام وأيأس الكفار، وذلك عندنا على ذلك. وبعد، فإن المسألة ساقطة، لأنه علق الحكم بجواز القول، وقد بينا ثَمَّ المسألة. قد عرفنا بما سبق أن لا يجوز أن يوصف الله بحادث؛ ولو جاز ذلك لجاز الوصف بمصلح ومفسد وخير وشرير، وذاك باطل. فثبت أنه لا بما ظن يوصف. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إن غير الصوت لا يتكلم فيه بـ «يسمع»، وجائز أن يتكلم فيه بـ «يعلم»؛ ثم لم يجب التفريق بينهما بالاختلاف في حرف الإثبات، ولم يوجب في ذاته اختلافا؛ فما منع ذلك في حق النفي؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The two matters are both found in the description of describers when they describe Him with knowledge, power, and act without any difference from the perspective of description, then He is truly called knowing, creating, powerful in reality; so there is no basis for distinguishing them from the perspective of description, since their reality goes back to what is agreed upon. Then it may be said: He heard the prayer of so-and-so and did not hear the prayer of so-and-so; and a man says: God did not know that, and says: He knew from me at a certain time and did not know from me at a certain time; then it does not require that hearing and knowledge are not from the attributes of essence, so what prevents that in the case of speech and mercy? If he says: he intends the negation of the known and the heard, it is said to him: likewise in the first, he intends the negation of Pharaoh from His kindness and honoring by mentioning the negation of speech, and it is something by which He intends to honor him, and that is known by what He gave glad tidings to the believers of speech and disappointed the disbelievers, and that with us is upon that. Moreover, the question is invalid, because he attached the judgment to the permissibility of the statement, and we have explained the matter there. We have known from what preceded that it is not permissible to describe God with an accident; if that were permissible, then description with a reformer and corrupter, good and evil, would be permissible, and that is false. So it is established that He is not described with what he thought. And there is no power except with God. Also, something other than sound is not spoken of with 'He hears', but it is permissible to speak of it with 'He knows'; then the distinction between them by the difference in the letter of affirmation did not necessitate a difference in His essence; so what prevents that in the case of negation? And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the relationship between attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). It begins by stating that both types are used to describe God without any difference in the act of description itself—He is called Knowing, Creating, Powerful. The reality of both goes back to the same agreement. Then an objection is raised: people say God heard one person's prayer but not another, implying hearing is not an essential attribute. The response is that such statements negate only the object (the heard or known thing), not the attribute itself. Similarly, when the Qur'an says God does not speak to Pharaoh, it means He does not honor him with speech. The objector's question is invalid because it assumes a false premise. The passage then reiterates that God cannot be described by an accident (ḥādith), as that would lead to describing Him with reformer/corrupter, etc. Finally, another point is made: something other than sound is not spoken of with 'yusma' (He hears) but can be with 'yu'lam' (He knows); this difference in linguistic usage does not imply a difference in the divine essence. The same logic applies to negation of attributes.

العلم
التكليم
الرحمة
حادث
حرف الإثبات
النفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When God does not hear a specific prayer, it means He lacks the attribute of hearing.
Correction: Such negation only negates the object (the prayer), not the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the statement 'God heard X but not Y'?
A) It proves hearing is a temporal attribute.
B) It negates the object, not the attribute.
C) It is a valid theological argument.
D) It shows hearing is an attribute of action.

Key Takeaway
Temporal expressions in describing God's actions do not affect the eternity of His attributes; the negation refers to the object, not the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that God has not heard your most desperate prayer, does your heart accuse Him of lacking care or attention? How does today’s lesson—that the negation is of the object, not the attribute—force you to examine the real source of your doubt? Will you repent of any thought that limits His eternal hearing and trust that He has heard you perfectly, even when the answer is hidden?
?
You often speak of God in terms of time: 'He heard me yesterday' or 'He knew my need before I asked.' Does this language ever lead you to unconsciously imagine God as reactive or changing? How will you deliberately correct your own words and thoughts to honor His eternal attributes, starting today?
?
The lesson warns against describing God with accidents—temporary qualities that appear and disappear. In what subtle ways do you treat God’s mercy or anger as if they fluctuate based on your performance? How can you anchor your hope in the unchanging reality of His attributes, regardless of your failings?

The Impossibility of Denying Justice to God
Maturidi argues that denying justice to God is impossible and the opponent's reasoning is unsound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that denying justice to God is impossible and how he refutes the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Denying justice to God is impossible because justice must be an essential attribute.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a crisp logical argument against an opponent who avoids calling justice an attribute of God's essence. The argument is short but decisive: since we cannot deny justice to God, the opponent's refusal to affirm it as an essential attribute is self-defeating. Let's see how the argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the brevity and logical structure.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Understand key terms
Identify the terms 'negation of justice' (نفي العدل) and 'attribute of essence' (صفة الذات). Understand what each means.
Justice here is being discussed as an attribute of God.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The argument has three parts: premise (cannot negate justice), opponent's omission (did not call it an attribute of essence), conclusion (his estimation is corrupt). Trace how the conclusion follows.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for divine attributes. If justice can be denied, it might be considered an attribute of action. But Maturidi shows it must be essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لا يجوز وصف الله تعالى بنفي العدل، ثم لم يقل: هو صفة الذات عندهم، ثبت أن تقديره فاسد.
Also, it is not permissible to describe God Almighty with negation of justice, yet he did not say: it is an attribute of essence according to them; it is established that his estimation is corrupt.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a concise argument against an opponent who denies that justice is an attribute of essence. Maturidi states that it is impermissible to describe God with the negation of justice. If the opponent refrains from calling justice an attribute of essence, then his reasoning (or estimation) is proven corrupt. The argument is brief but forms a complete logical point: since justice cannot be negated from God, and the opponent does not classify it as an essence attribute, his position is contradictory.

Key Terms

نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Justice is merely an attribute of action and can be denied or affirmed depending on context.
Correction: Maturidi argues that denying justice to God is impermissible, implying that justice must be an essential attribute inherent to God's nature, not just an action.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi establish about the opponent who does not call justice an attribute of essence?
A) His position is valid.
B) His estimation is corrupt.
C) He must call it an attribute of action.
D) Justice is not an attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that justice must be an essential attribute of God, as denying it is impermissible and the opponent's refusal to affirm it leads to logical corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Are you willing to affirm that justice is an essential attribute of God, not just something He does when He chooses? How does that change the way you understand the hardships in your life? What commitment can you make today to trust God's justice even when you don't understand?
?
The opponent's reasoning is called 'corrupt' because they refused to call justice essential. Is there an area in your faith where you avoid making a firm affirmation? How does this passage call you to be clear and decisive in your beliefs?
