The Dilemma of Action Attributes: Act or Not?
Maturidi presents a dialectical dilemma about whether the attribute of action is the act itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dialectic about whether the attribute of action is the act itself (like creation) and why that leads to the conclusion that action attributes are actually essence attributes.

Core Idea
If creation were an attribute, it would imply imperfection; thus action attributes reduce to essence.

In this passage, Māturīdī engages an opponent who distinguishes between attributes of essence and attributes of action. He first questions what is meant by 'attribute of action'—is it the act itself, such as creation? Through a series of logical steps, he shows that whether the opponent says yes or no, the attribute of action ultimately must be an attribute of the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dialectical structure: a series of conditional questions.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The opponent is given two options: the attribute of action is the act (creation) or something else. Consider the consequences.
3
Analyze the first option
If it is creation, then creation involves imperfection (corruption, ugliness, etc.) and cannot be an attribute of God.
4
Analyze the second option
If it is something else, then it is an act, and since God is transcendent above creation, the attribute must be of the essence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage establishes that what is called attribute of action is actually an attribute of essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: تعني بصفة الفعل الفعل نفسه وهو الخلق، أهو عندك فعل أو غيره؟ فإن قال: الخلق، قيل: لِمَ قلت: إن الخلق صفة، وهو صفة مَنْ؟ إذ لا صفة إلا لموصوف. فإن قال: هو صفة الله أعظم القول بأن يجعل الخلق الله صفة، والخلق فساد وقبح وضرورة وعجز وأنجاس وخبائث. وكل بصفته موصوف. وهذه الأوصاف مما يأبى كل من عقل أن يوصف بها، فكيف يُوصف بها الله؟ وإن قال: غير الخلق، لزمه القول: إن المراد أن صفته هي فعل، وقد بينا تعالي الله عن الوصف بخلقه، فثبت أن صفته التي هي الفعل هي صفة ذاته. وكذلك يقال: الله خالق رحمن رحيم، فإنما سمى به ذاته، فمثله صفة الفعل - أي الفعل - وتوصف به ذاته، وذلك كما يقال: كلام حكمةٌ وصدق وكذب، على أنه كذلك، وهو لصاحبه فمثله يضاف إلى الله. وبعد، فإنه يقال له: قولك رحمة ومغفرة صفة الفعل، ولعنة وشتم أيضا عندك صفة الفعل، فما الفعل الذي سُمّي رحمة ولعنة حتى يوصف الله به؟ فإن قال: جنة ونار، وقبول ورد ونحو ذلك، بطل قوله في المسائل التي ذكرت في الأصلح والتعديل والتجوير: «إن الله لا يفعل بعباده ذلك»، وكل ذلك مما فعل بعباده. وإن أثبت معنى سوى ذلك فصارا غير خلقه، بهما يوصف؛ على أن قوله شتم، كلام قبيح لا يوصف به الله. ثم يقال له: لِمَ اعتبرت بالذي ذكرت في صفة الذات والفعل؟ وقد رأيت صفات الذات مختلفة في الاستعمال من وجه الإثبات؛ نحو أن يُقال بالعلم في أشياء لا يوصف بالقدرة فيها، وبالقدرة على أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع فيها، وبالرؤية في أشياء لا يوصف بالكرم فيها، وبالجود وبالحكمة في أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع لها، ونحو ذلك مما يكثر الاختلاف به؛ لم يجب بها الفرق، بل هو الموصوف بها في الأزل. لِمَ لا قُلتَ كذلك في جميع ما يُوصف به؟ إذ هو
Then it is said to him: Do you mean by attribute of action the act itself which is creation? Is it according to you an act or other than it? If he says: creation, it is said: Why did you say that creation is an attribute, and whose attribute is it? For there is no attribute except of a described being. If he says: It is an attribute of God – the greatest of statements is to make creation an attribute of God, and creation is corruption, ugliness, necessity, incapacity, impurities, and filth. And everything is described by its attribute. And these descriptions are such that every rational person refuses to be described with them, so how can God be described with them? And if he says: other than creation, then the implication is that he intends that His attribute is an act, and we have already shown God's transcendence above being described by His creation, so it is established that His attribute which is the act is an attribute of His essence. And likewise it is said: God is Creator, Most Merciful, Merciful; He is only named thereby in reference to His essence; so likewise the attribute of action – i.e., the act – and His essence is described by it, just as it is said: speech is wisdom, truth, and falsehood, as it is so, and it belongs to its speaker; so likewise it is attributed to God. Moreover, it is said to him: Your statement that mercy and forgiveness are attributes of action, and curse and insult are also according to you attributes of action – so what is the act which is called mercy and curse such that God is described by it? If he says: Paradise and Hell, and acceptance and rejection and the like, then his statement is invalidated in the issues he mentioned concerning the optimal, rectification, and unjust attribution: 'God does not do that to His servants,' and all of that is something done to His servants. And if he affirms a meaning other than that, then they become other than His creation, and He is described by them; given that his statement 'curse' is ugly speech which God is not described with. Then it is said to him: Why did you consider what you mentioned concerning the attribute of essence and action? And you have seen the attributes of essence varied in usage from the perspective of affirmation; such as being said with knowledge of things in which He is not described with power, and with power over things in which He is not described with hearing, and with seeing of things in which He is not described with generosity, and with munificence and wisdom in things in which He is not described with hearing, and the like of that which abounds in variation; it did not necessitate division, rather He is described with them in pre-eternity. Why then did you not say likewise concerning all that He is described with? For He is
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the nature of attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). It begins by asking the opponent whether by 'attribute of action' he means the act itself, such as creation. If he says yes, then creation is full of imperfection and cannot be an attribute of God. If he says it is something other than the act, then it follows that the attribute is actually an act (i.e., something God does), and since God is transcendent above being described by His creation, this attribute must be an attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt). Then it is clarified that names like Creator and Merciful refer to the essence, and the attribute of action is really a description of the essence through the act. Then the opponent's distinction between mercy (attribute of action) and curse (also attribute of action) is examined: if mercy and curse refer to Paradise and Hell, then God does do these to His servants, contradicting the opponent's own claims. If they refer to something else, then they are not creation and thus attributes of essence. Finally, the argument shifts: even essence attributes vary in their application (e.g., knowledge without power, power without hearing), yet they are all essential. So the same logic should apply to all attributes—variation in usage does not imply a division between essence and action.

Key Terms
صفة الفعل
صفة الذات
الخلق
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributes of action are completely separate from the essence and refer to acts that are not eternal.
Correction: This argument shows that whether the act itself or something else, the attribute ends up being an attribute of the essence, thus maintaining God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if the opponent says the attribute of action is creation?
A) Creation is a perfect attribute.
B) Creation implies imperfection and cannot be an attribute.
C) Then it is not an attribute at all.
D) It becomes an attribute of essence automatically.

Key Takeaway
Whether the attribute of action is the act itself or something else, it must ultimately be an attribute of God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Māturīdī argues that creation is full of imperfection and cannot be an attribute of God. Reflect on a time you attributed a hardship or evil to God without acknowledging His absolute perfection. How does this lesson correct your perspective? Commit to remembering God's transcendence in all circumstances.
?
If all of God's attributes must ultimately be of His essence, then His actions are not separate from His nature. In your worship, do you treat God's blessings as mere events or as direct manifestations of His eternal attributes? How would viewing them as rooted in His essence change your gratitude and love for Him?
?
The opponent's dilemma forces a logical choice. Have you ever held a belief about God that, upon honest reflection, leads to an absurdity? Are you willing to follow the argument to its conclusion, even if it challenges your comfort? Resolve today to let reason and revelation guide your understanding of God, as Maturidi does.

Mercy, Curse, and the Unity of Divine Attributes
Maturidi critiques the classification of mercy and curse as action attributes and argues for the unity of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Māturīdī's critique of classifying mercy and curse as distinct action attributes and his argument that variation in application does not imply division in essence.

Core Idea
Even if attributes vary in usage, they all refer to the same eternal essence.

After establishing that action attributes are essence attributes, Māturīdī addresses specific cases: mercy, forgiveness, curse, and insult. He challenges the opponent to specify what actions these names refer to. If they refer to Paradise and Hell, then God does indeed do those things to His servants, contradicting the opponent's own doctrine. If they refer to something else, then they are not creation and thus essence attributes. Finally, he argues that just as essence attributes like knowledge and power vary in their application but are all essential, so too should all attributes be considered essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Understand the context: the opponent claims mercy and curse are both attributes of action.
2
Focus on the question
Māturīdī asks: what is the specific act that is called mercy and what is the act called curse?
3
Analyze the first possibility
If the act is Paradise and Hell (or acceptance and rejection), then God does these things to His servants, which the opponent denies. This invalidates the opponent's claim.
4
Analyze the second possibility
If the act is something else, then those things are not created entities and thus become attributes of essence.
5
Consider the final argument
Essence attributes themselves have varied applications (e.g., knowledge of things without power), yet they are one essence. So variation does not justify a division into essence and action.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And you have seen the attributes of essence varied in usage from the perspective of affirmation; such as being said with knowledge of things in which He is not described with power, and with power over things in which He is not described with hearing, and with seeing of things in which He is not described with generosity, and with munificence and wisdom in things in which He is not described with hearing, and the like of that which abounds in variation; it did not necessitate division, rather He is described with them in pre-eternity. Why then did you not say likewise concerning all that He is described with? For He is
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the nature of attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). It begins by asking the opponent whether by 'attribute of action' he means the act itself, such as creation. If he says yes, then creation is full of imperfection and cannot be an attribute of God. If he says it is something other than the act, then it follows that the attribute is actually an act (i.e., something God does), and since God is transcendent above being described by His creation, this attribute must be an attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt). Then it is clarified that names like Creator and Merciful refer to the essence, and the attribute of action is really a description of the essence through the act. Then the opponent's distinction between mercy (attribute of action) and curse (also attribute of action) is examined: if mercy and curse refer to Paradise and Hell, then God does do these to His servants, contradicting the opponent's own claims. If they refer to something else, then they are not creation and thus attributes of essence. Finally, the argument shifts: even essence attributes vary in their application (e.g., knowledge without power, power without hearing), yet they are all essential. So the same logic should apply to all attributes—variation in usage does not imply a division between essence and action.

Key Terms

صفة الذات
الرحمة
اللعنة

Checkpoint

A) Then curse becomes an attribute of essence.
B) Then curse is not an attribute at all.
C) Then it contradicts the opponent's claims.
D) Then it proves God does not curse.

Key Takeaway
All divine attributes, whether called action or essence, ultimately describe the same eternal essence.

Refutation of Attribute of Action as the Act Itself
Maturidi refutes the claim that attribute of action is the act itself, concluding it is attribute of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the attribute of action cannot be the act itself and must be an attribute of essence.

Core Idea
Describing Allah with His creation leads to attributing imperfection; thus actions are attributes of essence.

Imam Maturidi here refutes a key Mu'tazili claim that attributes of action are identical to the acts themselves. He presents a dilemma that forces the opponent to admit that such a description would attribute imperfection to Allah, or else accept that the attribute is of the essence. This argument preserves divine transcendence while maintaining the reality of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise and argumentative style.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents a dilemma: either the attribute of action is the act itself (creation) or it is not. Find where these two options are laid out.
The opponent's answer determines the consequence.
3
Understand the problem with 'creation' as attribute
If the attribute is creation, then Allah would be described with corruption, ugliness, etc., which is impossible for a perfect being.
This highlights the absurdity of the first option.
4
Follow the second option and its implication
If the opponent says it is not creation, then the attribute is simply an act, which we already know is not a separate attribute of action but an attribute of essence.
The conclusion collapses the distinction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the attribute of action is actually an attribute of essence. Consider how this protects Allah from imperfection.
This is a key Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لا يجوز وصف الله تعالى بنفي العدل، ثم لم يقل: هو صفة الذات عندهم، ثبت أن تقديره فاسد. ثم يقال له: تعني بصفة الفعل الفعل نفسه وهو الخلق، أهو عندك فعل أو غيره؟ فإن قال: الخلق، قيل: لِمَ قلت: إن الخلق صفة، وهو صفة مَنْ؟ إذ لا صفة إلا لموصوف. فإن قال: هو صفة، أعظم القول بأن يجعل الخلق صفة لله، والخلق فساد وقبح وضرورة وعجز وأنجاس وخبائث. وكل بصفته موصوف. وهذه الأوصاف مما يأبى كل من عقل أن يوصف بها، فكيف يُوصف بها الله؟ وإن قال: غير الخلق، لزمه القول: إن المراد أن صفته هي فعل، وقد بينا تعالي الله عن الوصف بخلقه، فثبت أن صفته التي هي الفعل هي صفة ذاته.
And also, it is not permissible to describe Allah Almighty by the negation of justice. Then he (the opponent) did not say that it (justice) is an attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt) according to them. So it is established that his estimation is corrupt. Then it is said to him: Do you mean by 'attribute of action' (ṣifat al-fi‘l) the act itself, which is creation (al-khalq)? Is it, according to you, an act or other? If he says: creation, it is said: Why did you say that creation is an attribute? And it is an attribute of whom? For there is no attribute except of an described entity. If he says: It is an attribute — the saying is grave that makes creation an attribute of Allah, while creation is corruption, ugliness, necessity, incapacity, impurities, and filth. And everything is described by its attribute. These descriptions are such that every rational person refuses to be described by them. So how can Allah be described by them? And if he says: other than creation, then it follows that he says: The intended meaning is that His attribute is an act. And we have shown the transcendence of Allah from being described by His creation. So it is established that His attribute, which is the act, is an attribute of His essence.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer refutation. It begins by stating that it is impermissible to describe Allah by the negation of justice, and that the opponent's estimation is corrupt. Then it presents a dilemma: if the attribute of action is the act itself (creation), then describing Allah with creation attributes to Him imperfection and corruption, which is impossible. If it is not the act itself, then the attribute must be an attribute of essence. Thus the distinction between attribute of essence and attribute of action collapses.

صفة الفعل
خلق
عدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributes of action are separate from the essence and imply change or temporality in God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such attributes (like creation) cannot be literal acts separate from the essence, because that would attribute imperfection. Instead, they are attributes of essence, meaning they are eternal aspects of God's perfect nature.

B) He would be described with imperfection and deficiency
C) He would be described with power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the idea that attributes of action are separate acts, showing they must be attributes of essence to preserve divine perfection.

Refutation of Mercy and Curse as Attributes of Action
Maturidi refutes the opponent's classification of mercy and curse as attributes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that describing mercy and curse as attributes of action leads to contradictions in the opponent's own theological positions.

Core Idea
Classifying mercy and curse as attributes of action forces contradictory conclusions about God's acts.

Imam Maturidi engages in a dialectical refutation of those who claim that mercy, forgiveness, curse, and insult are attributes of action (ṣifat al-fi‘l). He demonstrates that this classification either forces the opponent to name specific acts that conflict with their own doctrines or leads to absurdity. This passage sharpens the definition of divine attributes and guards against anthropomorphic implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the beginning of the passage where the opponent asserts that mercy, forgiveness, curse, and insult are all attributes of action.
Note that the opponent considers both positive and negative relational terms as attributes of action.
2
Posit Maturidi's key question
Identify the crucial question: 'What is the act that is called mercy and curse?' This forces the opponent to specify a concrete act.
The question exposes the need for a clear definition of what 'action' means in this context.
3
Consider the first possible answer
Examine the opponent's potential answer: Paradise and Hellfire, acceptance and rejection. Maturidi points out that this contradicts the opponent's own position that 'Allah does not do that to His servants' (from the issues of al-aṣlaḥ, al-ta‘dīl, and al-tajwīr).
This reveals an internal inconsistency in the opponent's theology.
4
Consider the second possible answer
If the opponent posits a meaning other than those acts, then mercy and curse are not created entities but attributes of essence, and describing Allah with 'curse' becomes impossible.
Maturidi concludes that 'insult' (shatm) is vile speech that cannot be attributed to Allah.
5
Summarize the refutation
Synthesize the argument: The opponent's classification fails in both scenarios; therefore mercy and curse cannot be attributes of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه يقال له: قولك رحمة ومغفرة صفة الفعل، ولعنة وشتم أيضا عندك صفة الفعل، فما الفعل الذي سُمّي رحمة ولعنة حتى يوصف الله به؟ فإن قال: جنة ونار، وقبول ورد ونحو ذلك، بطل قوله في المسائل التي ذكرت في الأصلح والتعديل والتجوير: «إن الله لا يفعل بعباده ذلك»، وكل ذلك مما فعل بعباده. وإن أثبت معنى سوى ذلك فصارا غير خلقه، بهما يوصف؛ على أن قوله شتم، كلام قبيح لا يوصف الله.
Then after that, it is said to him: Your saying that mercy (raḥma) and forgiveness (maghfira) are attributes of action, and also curse (la‘na) and insult (shatm) according to you are attributes of action. So what is the act that is called mercy and curse so that Allah is described by it? If he says: Paradise and Hellfire, and acceptance and rejection, and similar things, then his statement is invalidated in the issues mentioned about the optimal (al-aṣlaḥ), modification (al-ta‘dīl), and declaring unjust (al-tajwīr) that 'Allah does not do that to His servants'. And all of that is something He does to His servants. And if he affirms a meaning other than that, then they become other than His creation, and He is described by them; despite that, his saying 'insult' is vile speech that cannot describe Allah.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's claim that mercy, forgiveness, curse, and insult are attributes of action. The argument asks what the specific acts named by these terms are. If the opponent says Paradise and Hellfire, etc., then this contradicts his own position that Allah does not do certain things to His servants. If he posits a different meaning, then those attributes are not created acts, and curse cannot be attributed to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes that involve relation to creation are necessarily attributes of action.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that this classification leads to contradictions because the act named must be specified, and it conflicts with the opponent's other claims, or posits an absurd meaning.

Checkpoint

B) The act they name must be identified
C) They are not attributes of God
D) They imply change in God

Key Takeaway

Diversity in Application Does Not Affect Essentiality
Maturidi uses examples to argue diversity doesn't imply non-essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that attributes of essence can have different scopes of application without ceasing to be eternal and essential.

Core Idea
Attributes differ in scope, not in essence.

Imam Maturidi begins by addressing an objection: if attributes of essence can be applied to different things (e.g., knowledge to objects beyond power's reach), does that make them contingent? He argues that such diversity does not undermine their eternity, and challenges the opponent to apply the same reasoning consistently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice how Maturidi lists examples of differing applications of attributes.
Focus on the logical pattern: diversity → not a problem.
2
Identify the examples
Find each pair: knowledge vs power, power vs hearing, vision vs generosity, etc. Understand that each attribute applies to a different set of things.
This shows that application scope varies.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that such differences do not imply the attributes are created or changeable. They are still described in eternity.
The opponent's logic would force a false conclusion.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Maturidi challenges the opponent to apply his own criterion consistently, asking why he does not accept the same for other attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: لِمَ اعتبرت بالذي ذكرت في صفة الذات والفعل؟ وقد رأيت صفات الذات مختلفة في الاستعمال من وجه الإثبات؛ نحو أن يُقال بالعلم في أشياء لا يوصف بالقدرة فيها، وبالقدرة على أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع فيها، وبالرؤية في أشياء لا يوصف بالكرم فيها، وبالجود وبالحكمة في أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع لها، ونحو ذلك مما يكثر الاختلاف به؛ لم يجب بها الفرق، بل هو الموصوف بها في الأزل. لِمَ لا قُلتَ كذلك في جميع ما يُوصف به؟ إذ هو من إرادة يتعالى عن الاستحالة والفساد؛ إنهما آيتان للحدث أمارتان للكون بعد أن لم يكن. وأيضاً يقال له: رأيت الخلق أقسامًا يُسمَّى الله عندك ببعض الخلق ولا يُسمى ببعض؛ ثم لَمْ يدل على اختلاف في حق الصفة، وما منع ذلك في أمر الصفات؟ وبالله التوفيق. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم قوله: ما يوصف بالقدرة عليه فليس من صفات الذات؛ فهو عند خصمه لا يوصف بالقدرة على شيء من صفاته إلا على مجاز اللغة المفعول في ذلك، كما يُسمى ما يُفعل بالأمر أمرًا ونحو ذلك. وبعد، فإنّنا قد بينا اختلاف أحوال الصفات في التوسيع والتضييق في أشياء، على الاتفاق في أنها صفات الذات، فلنقل فيما ذكر كذلك. ثم من مذهبه أن الله تعالى كان غير خالق ولا رحمن، وقدر على أن يجعل ذاته خالقًا رحمانًا، ويجوز أن نعبد الرحمن الخالق، فيكون على قوله قدر على أن يجعل للخلق معبودًا، وذلك اسم تقع عليه القدرة؛ فيصير في الحقيقة يُعبد غير الله. وهو أيضا من وجه هذه الأسماء محدث، من حيث كانت مما تقع عليه القدرة. ثم يقال له: أيقدر الله أن لا يخلق الخلق؟ فإن قال: لا، صيّره خالقًا بالضرورة أو بنفسه، وبطل قوله. وإن قال: يقدر، فيلزمه أن يجعل غير المخلوق خلقًا بوقوع القدرة عليه، وفي ذلك إثبات قدم صفة الخلق. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is said to him: Why did you consider that which you mentioned concerning attributes of essence and action? And you have seen that attributes of essence differ in usage from the perspective of affirmation; such that one speaks of knowledge (al-ʿilm) concerning things in which He is not described with power (al-qudra), and power concerning things in which He is not described with hearing (al-sam‘), and vision (al-ru’ya) concerning things in which He is not described with generosity (al-karam), and munificence (al-jūd) and wisdom (al-ḥikma) concerning things in which He is not described with hearing for them, and similar things that increase in difference. That did not necessitate a difference concerning the attributes; rather, He is described by them in eternity (al-azal). Why then did you not say likewise in all that He is described by? For it is from will that is exalted above dissolution and corruption; they are signs of temporality (al-ḥadath) and indications of existence after non-existence. And also it is said to him: You have seen that creation is of types; Allah is called by some creation according to you and not called by others. Then it did not indicate a difference concerning the attribute. So what prevented that in the matter of attributes? And success is from Allah. The jurist Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then his saying: That which is capable of being brought under power (al-qudra) is not from the attributes of essence. So according to his opponent, He is not described with power over anything of His attributes except by a linguistic metaphor (majāz al-lugha) of the effect in that, just as what is done by command is called command, and similar things. Then after that, we have shown the differing states of attributes in expansion and restriction in things, while agreeing that they are attributes of essence. So let us say likewise concerning what was mentioned. Then from his doctrine is that Allah Almighty was not Creator (khāliq) nor Merciful (raḥmān), and He was able to make His essence Creator and Merciful, and it is permissible that we worship the Merciful, the Creator; so according to his statement, He was able to make for creation an object of worship, and that is a name on which power falls; thus in reality, another than Allah is worshipped. And it is also, from the perspective of these names, created (muḥdath), insofar as they are things on which power falls. Then it is said to him: Is Allah able not to create creation? If he says: No, he makes Him Creator necessarily or by Himself, and his statement becomes invalid. And if he says: Yes, then it requires him to make non-created creation by the applicability of power upon it, and in that is the affirmation of the eternity of the attribute of creation. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This is an extended argument and a direct statement from Imam Maturidi. It begins by noting that attributes of essence vary in their application: knowledge pertains to things power does not, etc., but that does not make them non-eternal or non-essential. The opponent's distinction based on capacity is invalid. Maturidi then refutes the view that God was not Creator or Merciful and could become so, showing it leads to worship of other than God. The final question about God's ability not to create shows the absurdity of the opponent's position, leading to either necessitating God's creation or affirming eternity of the attribute of creation.

Misconception: If an attribute applies to different things, it must be created.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even essential attributes have varying scopes, so diversity does not imply creation.

8 min
Refuting the Opponent on Power and Creation
Maturidi refutes opponent's view on power and attributes, showing it leads to absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that attributes subject to divine power are not attributes of essence, and that this leads to absurdities like God becoming Creator after not being.

Core Idea
If attributes subject to power were created, God would change.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi directly engages with the opponent's assertion that whatever falls under divine power cannot be an attribute of essence. He argues that this leads to a linguistic metaphor, then shows the devastating consequence: if God was not always Creator or Merciful, He would be subject to change, and even worship might be directed to other than God. The passage ends with a powerful logical question that forces the opponent into a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Start by reading the full Arabic and translation. Note the shift to a direct refutation.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent says that what can be brought under power (qudra) is not from attributes of essence.
This implies that attributes like 'Creator' are not eternal.
3
Follow Maturidi's response
Maturidi explains that this reduces to a metaphor, and that if God could become Creator, He would change, leading to worshipping another.
He also points out that God not being Creator in eternity contradicts His nature.
4
Analyze the final question
Maturidi asks if God can choose not to create. Either answer leads to absurdity: forced creation or eternal attribute of creation.
This shows the opponent's position is untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: لِمَ اعتبرت بالذي ذكرت في صفة الذات والفعل؟ وقد رأيت صفات الذات مختلفة في الاستعمال من وجه الإثبات؛ نحو أن يُقال بالعلم في أشياء لا يوصف بالقدرة فيها، وبالقدرة على أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع فيها، وبالرؤية في أشياء لا يوصف بالكرم فيها، وبالجود وبالحكمة في أشياء لا يوصف بالسمع لها، ونحو ذلك مما يكثر الاختلاف به؛ لم يجب بها الفرق، بل هو الموصوف بها في الأزل. لِمَ لا قُلتَ كذلك في جميع ما يُوصف به؟ إذ هو من إرادة يتعالى عن الاستحالة والفساد؛ إنهما آيتان للحدث أمارتان للكون بعد أن لم يكن. وأيضاً يقال له: رأيت الخلق أقسامًا يُسمَّى الله عندك ببعض الخلق ولا يُسمى ببعض؛ ثم لَمْ يدل على اختلاف في حق الصفة، وما منع ذلك في أمر الصفات؟ وبالله التوفيق. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم قوله: ما يوصف بالقدرة عليه فليس من صفات الذات؛ فهو عند خصمه لا يوصف بالقدرة على شيء من صفاته إلا على مجاز اللغة المفعول في ذلك، كما يُسمى ما يُفعل بالأمر أمرًا ونحو ذلك. وبعد، فإنّنا قد بينا اختلاف أحوال الصفات في التوسيع والتضييق في أشياء، على الاتفاق في أنها صفات الذات، فلنقل فيما ذكر كذلك. ثم من مذهبه أن الله تعالى كان غير خالق ولا رحمن، وقدر على أن يجعل ذاته خالقًا رحمانًا، ويجوز أن نعبد الرحمن الخالق، فيكون على قوله قدر على أن يجعل للخلق معبودًا، وذلك اسم تقع عليه القدرة؛ فيصير في الحقيقة يُعبد غير الله. وهو أيضا من وجه هذه الأسماء محدث، من حيث كانت مما تقع عليه القدرة. ثم يقال له: أيقدر الله أن لا يخلق الخلق؟ فإن قال: لا، صيّره خالقًا بالضرورة أو بنفسه، وبطل قوله. وإن قال: يقدر، فيلزمه أن يجعل غير المخلوق خلقًا بوقوع القدرة عليه، وفي ذلك إثبات قدم صفة الخلق. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is said to him: Why did you consider that which you mentioned concerning attributes of essence and action? And you have seen that attributes of essence differ in usage from the perspective of affirmation; such that one speaks of knowledge (al-ʿilm) concerning things in which He is not described with power (al-qudra), and power concerning things in which He is not described with hearing (al-sam‘), and vision (al-ru’ya) concerning things in which He is not described with generosity (al-karam), and munificence (al-jūd) and wisdom (al-ḥikma) concerning things in which He is not described with hearing for them, and similar things that increase in difference. That did not necessitate a difference concerning the attributes; rather, He is described by them in eternity (al-azal). Why then did you not say likewise in all that He is described by? For it is from will that is exalted above dissolution and corruption; they are signs of temporality (al-ḥadath) and indications of existence after non-existence. And also it is said to him: You have seen that creation is of types; Allah is called by some creation according to you and not called by others. Then it did not indicate a difference concerning the attribute. So what prevented that in the matter of attributes? And success is from Allah. The jurist Abū Manṣūr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then his saying: That which is capable of being brought under power (al-qudra) is not from the attributes of essence. So according to his opponent, He is not described with power over anything of His attributes except by a linguistic metaphor (majāz al-lugha) of the effect in that, just as what is done by command is called command, and similar things. Then after that, we have shown the differing states of attributes in expansion and restriction in things, while agreeing that they are attributes of essence. So let us say likewise concerning what was mentioned. Then from his doctrine is that Allah Almighty was not Creator (khāliq) nor Merciful (raḥmān), and He was able to make His essence Creator and Merciful, and it is permissible that we worship the Merciful, the Creator; so according to his statement, He was able to make for creation an object of worship, and that is a name on which power falls; thus in reality, another than Allah is worshipped. And it is also, from the perspective of these names, created (muḥdath), insofar as they are things on which power falls. Then it is said to him: Is Allah able not to create creation? If he says: No, he makes Him Creator necessarily or by Himself, and his statement becomes invalid. And if he says: Yes, then it requires him to make non-created creation by the applicability of power upon it, and in that is the affirmation of the eternity of the attribute of creation. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This is an extended argument and a direct statement from Imam Maturidi. It begins by noting that attributes of essence vary in their application: knowledge pertains to things power does not, etc., but that does not make them non-eternal or non-essential. The opponent's distinction based on capacity is invalid. Maturidi then refutes the view that God was not Creator or Merciful and could become so, showing it leads to worship of other than God. The final question about God's ability not to create shows the absurdity of the opponent's position, leading to either necessitating God's creation or affirming eternity of the attribute of creation.

Key Terms
القدرة
المفعول
مجاز اللغة
خالق
رحمن
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because 'Creator' is an attribute that applies to created things, it must be a created attribute itself.
Correction: Maturidi shows that attributes of essence can apply to created effects without being created, and that denying this leads to absurdities like a changing God.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the opponent's claim that God was not always Creator?
A) It shows God's power is limited
B) It leads to worshipping other than God
C) It proves attributes are literal
D) It affirms divine simplicity

Key Takeaway
Denying the eternity of attributes like 'Creator' leads to logical absurdities and contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you held the false belief that God only became 'Creator' at the moment He created the universe, how would that affect your confidence that He is truly sovereign over all things, including your life? Would you feel secure in His lordship?
?
When you recite 'Allah is Al-Khaliq (The Creator)', do you truly feel that He has always been that, or does your heart slip into thinking He only acquired that title later? How can you train your heart to affirm the eternity of His attributes?
?
The opponent's view leads to the absurdity that God could create another object of worship. Examine your own worship: do you ever attribute creative power to anything other than Allah, even in subtle ways? What steps will you take to purify your tawhid?

Refuting the Argument that Speech is Temporal from 'Coming' Verses
Maturidi refutes the argument that speech is temporal because God is described with 'coming'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the argument that God's speech is temporal based on Quranic descriptions of God's 'coming'.

Core Idea
Describing God with 'coming' does not imply change or temporality.

Some argued that because the Quran attributes 'coming' to God, this suggests change and thus that His speech is created. Maturidi refutes this by establishing that God is transcendent above change, so such descriptions do not entail temporality. In this lesson, we examine his concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading Maturidi's original words and the English translation. Notice the structure: he presents the opponent's argument first, then his rebuttal.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to key terms.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that speech is temporal (ḥadath) because God is described with 'coming' (ityān and majī‘), which they take to imply change.
This is a common argument from those who believe the Quran is created.
3
Understand Maturidi's principle
Maturidi states that God is exalted above change (taghayyur) and passing away (zawāl). So attributing 'coming' to Him does not imply that He undergoes change.
This principle applies to all attributes of God, including speech and action.
4
Apply the principle to speech
Just as 'coming' does not imply temporality for God, speech also does not entail change or temporality. Maturidi concludes that the opponent's argument fails.
This shows that anthropomorphic expressions must be understood in light of God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتَجَّ في حدَث الكلام بذكر الإتيان والمجيء، وهو من ذلك الوجه محدث. وقد بينا أن الله تعالى إذ وصف بالكلام فهو على تعاليه عن احتمال التغير والزوال، فمثله في صفة الكلام والفعل وما ذكرت على أن الله قد أضاف المجيء إلى نفسه، ثم لم يجب أنه حدث.
He argued for the temporality (ḥadath) of speech (al-kalām) by mentioning coming (al-ityān) and arriving (al-majī‘), and it is from that perspective temporal (muḥdath). And we have shown that when Allah is described by speech, He is, according to His exaltedness above possibility of change (taghayyur) and passing away (zawāl), so similarly concerning the attribute of speech and action and what I mentioned, because Allah has attributed arriving (al-majī‘) to Himself, yet it did not necessitate that it is temporal.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who assert that speech (al-kalām) is created (muḥdath) because the Quran describes God with 'coming' (al-majī‘), which suggests change and temporality. Maturidi responds that God is transcendent above change and passing away, so describing Him with 'coming' does not imply that He is subject to temporality. Similarly, the attribute of speech, like action, does not entail change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses that describe Allah 'coming' (e.g., Sūrat al-Fajr), does your heart ever imagine a physical movement, even if your mind affirms transcendence? What does that reveal about your attachment to worldly metaphors, and how can you train yourself to think of Allah's actions without change? Will you commit to reciting the Qur'an with the awareness that every attribute of Allah is eternal and beyond resemblance?
?
Maturidi argues that Allah's speech is not temporal because He is above change. Yet, in your daily life, do you treat the Qur'an as a timeless address to you, or as a historical document? If you truly believed that these words are uncreated and eternal, how would your urgency to understand and obey them change? Pause and ask yourself: am I giving the Qur'an the reverence due to an eternal attribute of Allah?
?
Consider the opponents who used 'coming' verses to argue that speech is created. Have you ever used the 'apparent' meaning of a verse to support a weak position because it suited your desires or arguments? How does Maturidi's insistence on the principle of transcendence challenge you to prioritize definitive principles over surface meanings? Resolve today to seek deeper understanding before forming conclusions about Allah's attributes.

The Opponent's View on Divine Attributes and Ability
Maturidi presents the opponent's view and his own prior explanation about attributes of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's claim that God cannot be described by ability over His own attributes, and Maturidi's reminder that all attributes are attributes of essence despite their differences.

Core Idea
The opponent limits God's ability over His own attributes to a metaphor.

Maturidi begins by quoting an opponent who argues that anything describable by ability (qudra) cannot be an attribute of essence. The opponent holds that God's ability over His own attributes is only metaphorical. Maturidi then recalls his earlier explanation that attributes differ in scope but share the status of being attributes of essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the opponent's statement and Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
What does the opponent say about attributes that are subject to ability? Why does he consider ability over attributes metaphorical?
The opponent uses an analogy: what is done by command is called 'command' metaphorically.
3
Understand Maturidi's prior point
Maturidi references his previous explanation that attributes vary in scope but are all attributes of essence. How does this set up his refutation?
This establishes a shared ground before addressing the specific issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ثم قوله : ما يوصف بالقدرة عليه فليس من صفات الذات؛ فهو عند خصمه لا يوصف بالقدرة على شيء من صفاته إلا على مجاز اللغة المفعول في ذلك، كما يُسمى ما يُفعل بالأمر أمرا ونحو ذلك. وبعد، فإنّنا قد بينا اختلاف أحوال الصفات في التوسيع والتضييق في أشياء، على الاتفاق في أنها صفات الذات، فلنقل فيما ذكر كذلك ثم من مذهبه أن الله تعالى كان غير خالق ولا رحمن، وقدر على أن يجعل ذاته خالقا رحمانًا، ويجوز أن نعبد الرحمن الخالق ، فيكون على قوله قَدر على أن يجعل للخلق معبودا ، وذلك اسم تقع عليه القدرة ؛ فيصير في الحقيقة يُعبد غير الله . وهو أيضا من وجه هذه الأسماء محدث، من حيث كانت مما تقع عليه القدرة. ثم يقال له : أيقدر الله أن لا يخلق الخلق ؟ فإن قال : لا ، صيّره خالقا بالضرورة أو بنفسه، وبطل قوله ، وإن قال : يقدر ، فيلزمه أن يجعل غير المخلوق خلقا بوقوع القدرة عليه، وفي [ ذلك ] إثبات قدم [صفة ] الخلق. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then his statement: 'What is described by ability is not from the attributes of essence; according to his opponent, he is not described by ability over any of his attributes except metaphorically as the object of language in that, just as what is done by command is called command and the like.' After that, we have indeed explained the difference in states of attributes in expansion and restriction in things, despite agreement that they are attributes of essence. So let us say likewise regarding what was mentioned. Then from his doctrine is that Allah Almighty was not creator nor merciful, and He had the ability to make His essence creator and merciful, and it is permissible that we worship the merciful creator, so according to his statement He had the ability to make for creation an object of worship, and that is a name upon which ability falls; thus in reality something other than Allah is worshipped. And it is also from this aspect of these names created, since they are among those over which ability falls. Then it is said to him: Is Allah able not to create creation? If he says: No, he makes Him creator by necessity or by Himself, and his statement is false. If he says: He is able, then it necessitates that He makes non-created creation by the occurrence of ability upon it, and in that is the establishment of the pre-eternity of the attribute of creation. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim about divine attributes. Maturidi begins by stating the opponent's position: that anything described by ability (qudra) is not an attribute of essence. The opponent holds that God cannot be described by ability over His own attributes except metaphorically, like calling what is done by command 'command'. Maturidi then notes that he has already explained that while attributes differ in their scope and application, they all agree in being attributes of essence. He then turns to a logical consequence of the opponent's view: that God was not creator or merciful before creation, and had the ability to make His essence creator and merciful. This would mean that worship is directed to a creator who is not eternal, and that God could make something other than Himself an object of worship. Maturidi then poses a dilemma: Is God able not to create? If the opponent says no, then God is forced to create, which contradicts His omnipotence. If yes, then God has power over non-creation and can bring about creation from non-existence, which implies the pre-eternity of the attribute of creation. Thus, the opponent's position leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
صفات الذات
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the opponent quoted by Maturidi, how is God described by ability over His attributes?
A) Actually and literally.
B) Not at all.
C) Only metaphorically.
D) By analogy to human ability.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi sets up the refutation by reminding that all attributes are attributes of essence despite differences in their scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously limit His ability over them by thinking of them as beyond His control? How would trusting that God has full, real ability over all His attributes change your reliance on Him in your daily struggles?
?
If you were to examine your own beliefs, do you sometimes treat God's attributes as mere concepts rather than real, essential aspects of His being? What practical step can you take today to deepen your reverence for one of His attributes?

Consequences of Denying God's Ability Over His Attributes
Maturidi explains the logical consequences of the opponent's view and presents a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical consequences of the opponent's view, including the implication that worship would be directed to a non-eternal being and the dilemma about God's ability not to create.

Core Idea
If God could make His essence creator, then a non-eternal being would be worshipped.

Maturidi now draws out the implications of the opponent's position. If God was not creator or merciful prior to creation, and could make His essence such, then worship would be directed to a being that is not eternally God. Furthermore, the opponent is faced with a dilemma about whether God can refrain from creating, leading to either necessitating creation or affirming pre-eternal creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the flow from the opponent's doctrine to the dilemma.
2
Identify the first consequence
Maturidi argues that if God could make His essence creator and merciful, then He could make an object of worship other than Himself. Why is this problematic?
It implies that what is worshipped is not eternally God, but a created entity.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Maturidi poses a question: Is God able not to create? Explore both answers and their implications.
Both answers lead to contradictions within the opponent's framework.
4
Understand the conclusion
The opponent's view leads to absurdities, thus it must be rejected.
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is no power except with Allah.

Interpreting Anthropomorphic Descriptions of God's Speech and Action
Maturidi defends divine transcendence by reinterpreting anthropomorphic verbs metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi reinterprets Quranic terms like 'coming' and 'going' to preserve God's transcendence, redirecting their meaning from literal human change to a metaphorical sense befitting lordship.

Core Idea
When God is described in human terms, we must redirect the meaning to what befits His majesty, not His change.

The Quran describes God with words like 'coming' and 'arriving.' Some took these literally and concluded that God changes or moves. Maturidi, however, explains that such terms must be understood metaphorically, consistent with God's transcendence. He draws on the example of Prophet Ibrahim and the principle of preserving God's boundaries, concluding that all divine attributes of speech and action are expressed metaphorically in relation to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argumentative flow come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the shift from the opponent's claim to Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the redirection strategy
See how Maturidi does not deny the term 'coming' but insists it must be 'redirected' (sarf) to an aspect befitting God, not literal movement.
This is the key move: metaphorical interpretation without denying the text.
3
Analyze the Ibrahim analogy
Reflect on Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'I do not like those that set' and why Maturidi uses it to argue that change implies imperfection, which cannot apply to God.
This reinforces that God is above any state-to-state change.
4
Understand the conclusion about metaphor
Notice that Maturidi concludes that all Quranic attributions of speech to God among creation are metaphorical (majaz), based on agreement with the true attribute, not literal.
This principle extends to other terms like 'covenant' and 'victory of the Lord'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتَجَّ في حَدَث الكلام بذكر الإتيان والمجيئ ، وهو من ذلك الوجه محدث. وقد بينا أن الله تعالى إذ وصف بالكلام [ فهو ] على تعاليه عن احتمال التغير والزوال، فمثله في صفة الكلام والفعل وما ذكرتُ على أن الله قد أضاف المجيئ إلى نفسه ، ثم لم يجب أنه حدث، بل صرف إلى جهته ، فمثله الأول. وكذلك إذا وجب صرف الإتيان إلى الوجه الذي يحق بالربوبية، لا إلى ما عُرف به الخلق من التغيّر والزوال، فمثله في حقيقة الفعل والكلام، على ما قال إبراهيم: لا أحب الأفلين ﴾ [الأنعام ، ٧٦/٦] ، ومَنْ يكون على حال ثم على أخرى فهو من الآفلين بالتحقيق. والله أعلم. واحتج بما يُحفظ ؛ وقد يُحفظ الله ؛ وقد يكون ذلك على حفظ حدوده و ما اشتمل عليه الكلام. وما يضاف إلى الله من الكلام بين الخلق فهو مجاز على الموافقة بما يُعرف به الكلام الذي صفته . وذلك كما ذكرنا من المجيئ وغيره والعهد ونصر الرَّب ونحو ذلك مما لا يُحقق ذلك المعنى لذاته، فمثله القرآن .
And he argued for the temporality of speech by mentioning 'coming' and 'arriving', and it is in that respect temporal. And we have already shown that when Allah Almighty is described by speech, He is above the possibility of change and cessation, so likewise regarding the attribute of speech and action and what I mentioned, that Allah has attributed 'arriving' to Himself, and then it is not necessary that it is temporal, rather it is redirected to His station, so likewise the first. And similarly, if it is necessary to redirect 'coming' to the aspect befitting lordship, not to what is known of change and cessation among creation, then likewise regarding the reality of action and speech, as Ibrahim said: 'I do not like those that set' (Al-An'am 6:76). And whoever is in one state and then another is among those that set in reality. And Allah knows best. And he argued by what is preserved; and Allah may be preserved; and that may be regarding the preservation of His boundaries and what the speech contains. And what is attributed to Allah of speech among creation is metaphorical, based on agreement with what is known by the speech that is His attribute. And that is as we mentioned of 'arriving' and other things, and the covenant and the victory of the Lord and the like, which do not realize that meaning for themselves, and likewise the Quran.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument concerning the attribute of speech (al-kalam). The opponent uses the occurrence of terms like 'coming' (ityan) and 'arriving' (maji') in the Quran to argue that God's speech is temporal (hadith) because such actions imply change. Maturidi responds by stating that when God is described by speech, He is transcendent above change and cessation. Therefore, terms like 'arriving' must be redirected (sarf) to a meaning befitting God's lordship, not human movement. He draws an analogy with the statement of Prophet Ibrahim (Abraham): 'I do not like those that set' (Al-An'am 6:76), implying that if something changes state, it is subject to setting or change, which cannot apply to God. Maturidi also addresses the argument that speech is 'preserved' (hifz), noting that preservation of God's speech refers to preserving His boundaries, not physical preservation. He then concludes that any attribution of speech from God to creation is metaphorical (majaz), based on agreement (muwafaqa) with the true attribute of speech. Examples include 'arriving', 'covenant', 'victory of the Lord', and similar terms. The Quran itself, as speech attributed to God, must be understood in this analogical manner.

Key Terms
الكلام
الإتيان
المجيء
صرف
مجاز
حفظ
الحدود
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Quranic descriptions of God's 'coming' imply literal movement or change in God.
Correction: Maturidi explains that such terms must be redirected (sarf) to a meaning befitting God's lordship, not literal human change.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi interpret the term 'coming' (ityan) when attributed to God?
A) As a literal movement from place to place.
B) As a metaphorical expression that must be understood in a way befitting God's lordship.
C) As a proof that God's speech is temporal.
D) As referring to the preservation of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that any Quranic term suggesting change in God must be interpreted metaphorically, preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that describes God in a way that seems to imply change or limitation, like 'God comes' or 'God descends', what is your immediate emotional reaction? Do you feel unsettled by a metaphorical interpretation, or does it strengthen your trust in God's transcendence? How will you train your heart to immediately redirect such thoughts to what befits His majesty?
?
Think of a time you unintentionally described God using a human-like quality in your prayer or conversation. This lesson shows that even the Prophet Ibrahim rejected anything that changes. Will you now consciously examine your own language about God and ask for forgiveness if you have fallen short? What specific phrase will you correct today?
?
Maturidi says that attributions like 'covenant' and 'victory of the Lord' are metaphorical. Will you allow every apparent anthropomorphism to drive you deeper into awe rather than confusion? What is one verse you will revisit with this new lens?

Refuting the Comparison of Speech to Knowledge
Maturidi refutes the analogy between speech and knowledge as attributes of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that speech is an attribute of essence like knowledge, and the error of applying observable analogies to divine attributes.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are unique; analogies from creation fail.

This short but dense passage captures Maturidi's rebuttal of an opponent who tries to equate God's speech with His knowledge. The opponent's reasoning is shown to be self-defeating because it relies on a concept of 'attribute of essence' that even he cannot consistently apply. Maturidi also exposes the flaw in analogizing divine speech to human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the concise argumentative style.
The text is incomplete; the argument may continue beyond what is given.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that speech is an attribute of essence like knowledge. But then he denies that God has knowledge 'in reality'—an inconsistency.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi points out that the opponent himself uses the term 'attribute of essence' for knowledge and hearing without affirming their reality in God, so he should apply the same logic to speech. This renders the comparison corrupt.
Maturidi also mentions he has already provided sufficient evidence for the intelligent.
4
See the false analogies
The opponent then analogizes speech to action (fi'l) and to observable phenomena. Maturidi rejects these by noting that according to the opponent's own principles, there is no difference, and divine attributes are not subject to created limitations.
5
Reflect on the uniqueness of divine attributes
This lesson shows that divine attributes cannot be understood through analogies with creation. Each attribute is known through revelation alone.
This connects to broader Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم رجع إلى أنه لو قيل: هو صفة الذات كالعلم، فزعم أنه لا يقول: له علم في الحقيقة. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } وما قاله فاسد، لأنه عورض بقوله صفة الذات، فليقل به كما قال " في العلم " بلا حقيقة، وكذا السمع " ونحوه " . ونحن قد بينا بحمد الله ما يكفي ذا العقل دونه. ثم عارض الكلام بالفعل، ولا فرق بينهما عند خصمه .. ثم عارض بما لا يخلو في الشاهد
Then he returned to saying that if it is said: It is an attribute of essence like knowledge, he claims that he does not say: He has knowledge in reality. Al-Faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And what he said is corrupt, because he was confronted by his statement 'attribute of essence'. So let him say by it as he said about 'knowledge' without reality, and likewise 'hearing' and the like. And we have already explained, praise be to Allah, what suffices the intelligent without it. Then he compared speech with action, and there is no difference between them according to his opponent. Then he made an analogy with what is not free in the observable world.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage contains a refutation by Maturidi (referred to as al-Faqih) of an opponent's argument regarding the attribute of speech. The opponent claims that if speech is said to be an attribute of essence like knowledge, then he (the opponent) would not say that God has knowledge 'in reality'. Maturidi responds that this is corrupt reasoning: the opponent himself uses the term 'attribute of essence' for other attributes like knowledge, so he should apply the same logic to speech. Maturidi notes that he has already provided sufficient evidence for the intelligent person that speech is a distinct attribute. The opponent then tries to analogize speech with action (fi'l), but Maturidi points out that according to the opponent's own principles, there is no difference between them. Finally, the opponent makes an analogy based on what is not free in the observable world, which is invalid because God is not subject to observable limitations. The text ends abruptly, but the argument clearly shows Maturidi's method of defending the uniqueness of divine attributes.

Checkpoint

B) The opponent admits that speech is temporal.
C) The opponent equates speech with action.
D) The opponent uses observable analogies.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the opponent's attempt to analogize divine attributes leads to inconsistency, affirming that divine attributes are known only through revelation and not by analogical reasoning from creation.

?
The opponent compares speech to action. In what ways do you reduce God's actions to human-like motives? What does that reveal about your reverence for Him, and how will you correct it?

Metaphorical Divine Descriptions
Maturidi defines metaphorical divine descriptions and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi holds that divine descriptions like 'coming' and 'covenant' are metaphorical and not literal.

Core Idea
Divine descriptions are metaphorical by agreement, not literal changes in God.

Maturidi introduces a key principle: when we attribute speech or actions to God, we use language metaphorically. This avoids implying change in the divine essence. He gives examples to clarify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi opens with a general rule and then gives examples.
The examples are 'coming', 'covenant', and 'help of the Lord'.
2
Identify the metaphorical terms
List the terms Maturidi considers metaphorical. Why do they not realize their literal meaning for God?
They would imply temporal change if taken literally.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on why Maturidi says these are 'majāz ʻalā al-muwāfaqah' (metaphor by agreement). What does this teach about divine attributes?
It preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يضاف إلى الله من الكلام بين الخلق فهو مجاز على الموافقة بما يُعرف به الكلام الذي صفته . وذلك كما ذكرنا من المجيئ وغيره والعهد ونصر الرب ونحو ذلك مما لا يُحقق ذلك المعنى لذاته، فمثله القرآن . وقد احتج بأنواع - هو من ذلك الوجه محدث مخلوق - من النسخ والستور والآيات ونحو ـ ومن ذلك الوجه لا يوصف الله به. ثم رجع إلى أنه لو قيل: هو صفة الذات كالعلم، فزعم أنه لا يقول: له علم في الحقيقة. ذلك، قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وما قاله فاسد، لأنه عورض بقوله صفة الذات، فليقل به كما قال في العلم بلا حقيقة، وكذا السمع ونحوه . ونحن قد بينا بحمد الله ما يكفي ذا العقل دونه.
And what is attributed to God of speech among creation is a metaphor by agreement according to what is known of speech that has a description. That is as we mentioned of 'coming' and other things, and 'covenant' and 'help of the Lord' and the like, which do not realize that meaning in essence, so the Qur'an is like that. And he argued with types – it is from that aspect originated created – from abrogation and veiling and verses and the like – and from that aspect God is not described. Then he returned to the claim that if it is said: it is an attribute of the essence like knowledge, he claimed that he does not say He has knowledge in reality. That. The jurist said, may God have mercy on him: And what he said is corrupt, because he was confronted by his saying 'attribute of the essence', so let him say it as he said concerning knowledge without reality, and likewise hearing and the like. And we have explained, praise be to God, what suffices the possessor of intellect without it.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the metaphorical nature of certain divine descriptions and a refutation of the claim that divine speech is created. Maturidi begins by stating that what is attributed to God in terms of speech among creation is metaphorical ('مجاز'), based on agreement with the known meaning of speech. He gives examples such as 'coming', 'covenant', and 'help of the Lord' – these terms do not realize their literal meaning for God's essence. The Qur'an is similar: it is described as created from the aspect of being a temporal phenomenon (like abrogation, veiling, verses), but from the aspect of being God's speech, it is not described with temporal attributes. Then he reports an opponent who, when told that speech is an attribute of the essence like knowledge, claimed that he does not say God has knowledge in reality. The jurist (likely Maturidi's teacher) refutes this by pointing out inconsistency: if the opponent accepts that knowledge is an attribute of the essence without a 'reality' (i.e., without a separate entity), then he should say the same for speech, hearing, etc. The passage concludes that the explanation already given suffices for the rational person.

Key Terms
مجاز
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that terms like 'coming' and 'help' imply physical movement or change in God.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies they are metaphorical; they do not realize the literal meaning for God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why are terms like 'coming' and 'covenant' metaphorical when attributed to God?
A) Because they are used only for humans.
B) Because they do not realize their literal meaning for God's essence.
C) Because they are created.
D) Because they indicate change in God.

Key Takeaway
Divine descriptions like 'coming' are metaphorical, preserving God's transcendence from change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon God using names like 'The Helper' or 'The Coming', do you ever subconsciously imagine Him acting like a human helper or moving from place to place? How will you actively train your mind to think metaphorically instead of literally?
?
Maturidi says these descriptions are metaphorical 'by agreement'—agreement among the Muslim community. What is your personal responsibility in upholding this agreed-upon understanding? Are you willing to correct yourself or others when the descriptions are taken literally?
?
If even the Qur'an itself is considered metaphorical in one sense, how does that change the way you approach its verses about God's actions? What specific practice will you adopt today to remind yourself that God transcends the literal meanings of His descriptions?

Refutation of Created Speech and Attribute Consistency
Maturidi refutes the createdness of Quran and exposes inconsistency in opponent's attribute theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that the Quran is created, and his argument for consistency in attributing attributes to God.

Core Idea
The Quran is not created in its essence; the opponent's denial of real attributes is inconsistent.

Maturidi addresses an opponent who argues that the Quran is created because it has temporal aspects like abrogation. He then refutes another opponent who denies that attributes like speech have reality, exposing a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the two parts: the opponent's argument about creation, and the refutation about attributes.
The opponent uses abrogation, veiling, verses as evidence.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
What is the opponent's evidence for the Quran being created? Note the distinction: 'from that aspect it is originated created'.
They focus on temporal manifestations.
3
Analyze the refutation
The opponent says speech is an attribute of essence like knowledge, but then denies that God has knowledge in reality. Why is this inconsistent?
If knowledge is accepted as attribute of essence without a separate 'reality', so should speech.

Divine Attributes Are Eternal Descriptions of Perfection
Maturidi refutes objections and establishes that divine attributes indicate perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine attributes are eternal descriptions of God's perfection, not dependent on creation, and that they indicate freedom from defects.

Core Idea
God's attributes describe His eternal perfection, not dependence on anything.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses objections raised by opponents about divine speech and action. He establishes the foundational principle that when we describe God as speaking, knowing, or acting, we are affirming His absolute perfection — freedom from any defect. This lesson unpacks that principle and its implications for understanding the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the flow of objections and responses.
Notice the repeated phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' as a marker of section breaks.
2
Identify the objections and responses
Find where Maturidi responds to the opponent's argument about speech and the objection about a child. Notice how he pivots to the principle of attributes.
3
Focus on the central principle
Locate the statement: 'الوصف بالكلام والعلم والفعل والحمد عليه إنما هو وصف بالبراءة من الآفات والتعالي عن العيوب'. Understand that this is the key to the entire discussion.
This principle applies to all divine attributes.
4
Understand the clarification about attributes
Read the final part where Maturidi explains that by 'attributes' he does not mean they are identical to the essence, but that they negate opposites and are real descriptions.
This guards against both anthropomorphism and denial of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من يجوز منه الكلام من خرس أو سكوت، وقد أخطأ في السؤال، إنما هو من عجز أو سكوت وعارض بالفعل، وهو كذلك عند الخصم. مع ما ذكر فيه الفعل أو الترك، وما الترك إلا الفعل، لكن الحيرة تعمل به ما ذكر. ثم عارض بالصبي أنه ليس بأخرس، وقد بينا أنه يعجز عن ذلك مع ما كان من عظم عجزه أن لا يجد لنفسه مثلا يعرف به الرب إلا الصبيان والمجانين. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأجاب لما عورض بما لا يخلو القادر - مما له القدرة من فعل وكلام به ـ في حال حدوث القدرة من فعل . وذلك جهل المعتزلة جعله دليلا، فبورك له في توحيده الذي ذلك دليله . {قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } والأصل في ذلك أن الوصف بالكلام والعلم والفعل والحمد عليه إنما هو وصف بالبراءة من الآفات والتعالي عن العيوب، وهو كذلك في الأزل. مع ما لو كان بغيره خالقا رحمانا متكلما يجوز أن يصير لا كذلك والقول بـ «يا من ليس برحمن ولا رحيم ولا خالق قول ذمّ وإلحاق بغيره من الخلائق، فثبت أنه بذاته رحمن رحيم خالق . ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنه لو كان يسمى بما يحل في غيره ليجب أن يسمى بكل شيء يحل في غيره؛ مع ما لو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يكون أحد يحتمل مثله في الشاهد. وفي امتناع ذلك في محتمل التغير وصف له بالعلو عن ذلك. والله الموفق. ثم قال : نريد بصفات أن لا يثبت ثم غير ، ولم نرد أنها ذاته، بل كل صفة لقديم أو حديث فهي غيره؛ وهي قول أو كتاب. وصفات الله هي قولنا الذي يصفه، أو قوله وكتابه.
Who from whom speech is possible from dumbness or silence, and he erred in the question, it is only from incapacity or silence, and he objected with action, and that is likewise with the opponent. Along with what was mentioned concerning action or abandoning, and abandoning is only action, but confusion works with what was mentioned. Then he objected with the child that he is not dumb, and we have explained that he is incapable of that along with what was of the greatness of his incapacity that he does not find for himself an example by which to know the Lord except children and the insane. And there is no power except with God. And he answered when he was confronted with what does not lack the capable – of what has capacity from action and speech in it – in the state of occurrence of capacity from action. And that is the ignorance of the Mu'tazila who made it a proof, so blessed be he in his tawhid for which that is its proof. Abu Mansur said, may God have mercy on him: And the principle in that is that description with speech and knowledge and action and praise upon it is only a description of freedom from defects and exaltation above faults, and it is thus in pre-eternity. With what if He were by other than Him creator, merciful, speaker, it would be possible that He become not like that, and the saying 'O He who is not merciful nor compassionate nor creator' is a saying of blame and attaching Him to other than Him of creatures. So it is established that He is by His essence merciful, compassionate, creator. And there is no power except with God. And further, if He were named by what inheres in other than Him, then it would be necessary that He be named by everything that inheres in other than Him; with what if that were permissible, then it would be permissible that someone be capable of the like of it in the witnessed. And in the impossibility of that in that which is susceptible to change, it is a description for Him of being exalted above that. And God is the giver of success. Then he said: We intend by attributes that there be established there no other, and we did not intend that they are His essence, but every attribute of a pre-eternal or originated is other than Him; and it is a saying or a book. And the attributes of God are our saying which describes Him, or His saying and His book.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage addresses objections about divine speech and action, and establishes the principle that divine attributes are eternal and indicate perfection. Maturidi responds to an opponent who argues that one who can speak must be free from dumbness or silence; the opponent errs because dumbness implies incapacity, not relevant to God. Then an objection about children: a child is not dumb but cannot speak due to incapacity. Maturidi then states that the capable agent (al-qadir) does not lack the ability to act or speak when power occurs – this is a Mu'tazili proof, but it is ignorance. Then Abu Mansur (Maturidi) states the principle: describing God with speech, knowledge, action, and praise is describing Him as free from defects and exalted above flaws, and this applies in pre-eternity. If God were creator, merciful, speaker through another, He could cease to be so – that would be blameworthy. Therefore, He is by His essence merciful, compassionate, creator. Furthermore, if God were named by what inheres in another, He would have to be named by everything that inheres in another, which is absurd. So His exaltation above change is established. Finally, Maturidi clarifies that by 'attributes' he means that there is no other (i.e., no opposite like ignorance or incapacity) established, not that the attributes are identical to His essence. Every attribute of a pre-eternal or originated being is other than that being; God's attributes are our speech describing Him, or His speech and His book.

Key Terms
صفات
الأزل
القدرة
البراءة من الآفات
التعالي عن العيوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies the reality of attributes by saying they are 'our saying' or that they are not identical to the essence, implying attributes are merely subjective.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that attributes are real descriptions: they affirm that God is free from defects. They are not the essence itself but are real attributes of God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it necessary that God is described by attributes like speech and power in pre-eternity?
A) Because He acquired them through created objects.
B) Because describing Him with them indicates freedom from defects and exaltation above flaws.
C) Because He needs them to create the world.
D) Because humans cannot conceive of God without attributes.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are eternal descriptions of God's perfection, indicating His freedom from all defects and not dependent on created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say 'Ar-Rahman' daily—but do you truly believe His mercy is eternal and not dependent on your worthiness? How would your life change if you acted on that belief today?
?
The passage says describing God with attributes is a description of freedom from defects. What defect in your own character are you most reluctant to admit, and how does confessing it to God purify your understanding of His perfection?
?
Maturidi argues that if God's attributes were through another, He could cease to be them. Do you ever treat God's attributes as 'on' and 'off' depending on your circumstances? What would it look like to trust that He is always merciful, even when you feel abandoned?
