Did God Speak to Moses? The Objection Answered
Maturidi answers an objection about Moses hearing God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when God spoke to Moses, Moses heard a created representation of God's uncreated speech, not the speech itself.

Core Idea
What Moses heard was a created sound, not God's eternal speech.

The verse 'And God spoke to Moses directly' raises a theological puzzle: can the uncreated speech of God be heard as physical sound? Imam Maturidi answers this objection by distinguishing between God's eternal attribute of speech and the created means through which it was conveyed to Moses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: an objector poses a question, followed by an answer that clarifies what Moses actually heard.
The objection is based on a literal reading of the verse.
2
Identify the key distinction
The answer distinguishes between the created medium (tongue of Moses, letters, sound) and the uncreated speech itself.
This preserves the uncreatedness of God's speech while explaining sensory experience.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction is important for Maturidi's theology—it affirms both God's attribute of speech and the createdness of physical phenomena.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: هل أسمع الله كلامه موسى حيث قال: {وكلم الله موسى تكليما}؟ قيل أسمعه بلسان موسى وبحروف خلقها وصوت أنشأه، فهو أسمعه ما ليس بمخلوق. والقول بالوقف يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما أن يقال: ليس هو الله ولا غيره، فيكون وقفا عن علم، وهو حق على ما ثبت في العلم والقدرة. والثاني أن لا يعلم أخلق هو أو غيره، فإنه بعيد؛ لما لا يخلو من أن يذهب مذهب التقليد، وأكثر القوم على نفي ذلك، بل أجمع على لزوم العلم أنه الخلق أو غيره. وبعد، فإنه لا يعدو من أن: أ) يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم، فيكون بمعنى ما ذكرت؛ أو لا بذاته، فهو غيره، وكل الأغيار الله خلق: ما روي فيه سمع أو لا. ثبت أن القول بالغيرية الله يوجب الحدث، والحدث والخلق إذ هو منه؛ ب) أو لا يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم أو لا [بذاته]، فيكون الوقف وقفا للجهل به فحق مثله التعلم؛ لأنه لا دليل دفععه إلى ذلك القول ليتكلم فيه، إنما هو الجهل. ج) أو أن يكون الوقف بما لا يعلم مراد السائل فيه: إنه ما يعني بكلام الله والقرآن، أهو هذا المتبعض
If someone says: Did God make Moses hear His speech when He said 'And God spoke to Moses directly' (4:164)? It is said: He made him hear it through the tongue of Moses and by letters He created and a sound He originated, so He made him hear that which is not created. And the statement of suspending judgment (waqf) emerges in two ways: one is that it is said: It is not God nor other than Him, and this is a suspension from knowledge, and it is true as established in knowledge and power. The second is that one does not know whether it is created or not, and this is far-fetched; for it is not free from tending toward blind following, and most of the community deny this, rather they are unanimous on the necessity of knowing whether it is created or not. Furthermore, it does not go beyond that: a) one knows that He is speaking by Himself, so it is in the sense I mentioned; or not by Himself, so it is other than Him, and all other-than-God are created: whether it is reported by hearing or not. So it is established that the statement of otherness necessitates temporality, and temporality and creation are from Him; b) or one does not know whether He is speaking by Himself or not, so the suspension is a suspension of ignorance, and it is obligatory for such a person to learn; because there is no proof that drove him to that statement so that he may speak about it, it is only ignorance; c) or that the suspension is due to not knowing the intent of the questioner: what he means by the speech of God and the Qur'an, whether it is this piecemeal [thing]...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage deals with two interconnected issues: the objection about God's speech to Moses, and the correct position of waqf (suspending judgment) on the ontological status of the Qur'an. First, an objector asks: Did God make Moses hear His own speech, as the verse 'And God spoke to Moses directly' indicates? The answer is that God made Moses hear His speech through created means: the tongue of Moses, created letters, and an originated sound. Thus, what Moses heard was not the uncreated speech itself but a created representation. This preserves the uncreatedness of God's speech while explaining the sensory experience. Second, the passage discusses the stance of waqf. It distinguishes two types: (1) a legitimate waqf based on knowledge, where one says the heard speech is neither God Himself nor something else (i.e., it is a created representation of the uncreated speech); (2) an illegitimate waqf based on ignorance, where one does not know whether the Qur'an is created or not—this is condemned because it leads to blind following. Then three logical possibilities are enumerated (a, b, c) that refine the waqf position: a) knowing that God is speaking by Himself (affirming the uncreated speech) or not (which would imply temporality); b) not knowing, which is ignorance and requires learning; c) suspending judgment due to ambiguity in the questioner's intent. The passage emphasizes that a definite position (created or uncreated) is necessary.

تكليم
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that the verse about Moses proves that God's speech is literally heard as sound.
Correction: Moses heard a created sound that represents God's uncreated speech, not the speech itself.

Checkpoint

A) God's uncreated speech directly
B) A created sound through letters and a voice
C) Nothing at all
D) The Qur'an itself

Key Takeaway
God's uncreated speech can only be known through created means, as with Moses.

The Types of Waqf: Knowledge vs. Ignorance
Maturidi classifies two types of waqf and three logical possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main types of waqf on the createdness of the Qur'an and why one is valid while the other is condemned.

Core Idea
Suspending judgment is only valid when based on knowledge, not ignorance.

The concept of 'waqf' or suspending judgment on whether the Qur'an is created or uncreated was a point of debate. Imam Maturidi clarifies that not all waqf is equal: one type is based on knowledge and is acceptable, while another based on ignorance is condemned. He then outlines three logical possibilities that help determine the correct theological stance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of waqf
Read the passage that defines two types of waqf: one based on knowledge (saying it is neither God nor other) and one based on ignorance (not knowing).
The first is considered valid; the second is far-fetched and leads to blind following.
2
Examine the three possibilities (a, b, c)
The passage then presents three possibilities: knowing God speaks by Himself (affirms uncreated speech), not knowing (ignorance, requiring learning), or suspension due to ambiguous intent.
These refine the waqf position and show that a definite position is necessary.
3
Reflect on the theological stance
Consider how this classification supports the Maturidi position that the Qur'an is uncreated speech of God, and that waqf based on ignorance is not permissible.

Source Text

فإن قال قائل: هل أسمع الله كلامه موسى حيث قال: {وكلم الله موسى تكليما}؟ قيل أسمعه بلسان موسى وبحروف خلقها وصوت أنشأه، فهو أسمعه ما ليس بمخلوق. والقول بالوقف يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما أن يقال: ليس هو الله ولا غيره، فيكون وقفا عن علم، وهو حق على ما ثبت في العلم والقدرة. والثاني أن لا يعلم أخلق هو أو غيره، فإنه بعيد؛ لما لا يخلو من أن يذهب مذهب التقليد، وأكثر القوم على نفي ذلك، بل أجمع على لزوم العلم أنه الخلق أو غيره. وبعد، فإنه لا يعدو من أن: أ) يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم، فيكون بمعنى ما ذكرت؛ أو لا بذاته، فهو غيره، وكل الأغيار الله خلق: ما روي فيه سمع أو لا. ثبت أن القول بالغيرية الله يوجب الحدث، والحدث والخلق إذ هو منه؛ ب) أو لا يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم أو لا [بذاته]، فيكون الوقف وقفا للجهل به فحق مثله التعلم؛ لأنه لا دليل دفععه إلى ذلك القول ليتكلم فيه، إنما هو الجهل. ج) أو أن يكون الوقف بما لا يعلم مراد السائل فيه: إنه ما يعني بكلام الله والقرآن، أهو هذا المتبعض
If someone says: Did God make Moses hear His speech when He said 'And God spoke to Moses directly' (4:164)? It is said: He made him hear it through the tongue of Moses and by letters He created and a sound He originated, so He made him hear that which is not created. And the statement of suspending judgment (waqf) emerges in two ways: one is that it is said: It is not God nor other than Him, and this is a suspension from knowledge, and it is true as established in knowledge and power. The second is that one does not know whether it is created or not, and this is far-fetched; for it is not free from tending toward blind following, and most of the community deny this, rather they are unanimous on the necessity of knowing whether it is created or not. Furthermore, it does not go beyond that: a) one knows that He is speaking by Himself, so it is in the sense I mentioned; or not by Himself, so it is other than Him, and all other-than-God are created: whether it is reported by hearing or not. So it is established that the statement of otherness necessitates temporality, and temporality and creation are from Him; b) or one does not know whether He is speaking by Himself or not, so the suspension is a suspension of ignorance, and it is obligatory for such a person to learn; because there is no proof that drove him to that statement so that he may speak about it, it is only ignorance; c) or that the suspension is due to not knowing the intent of the questioner: what he means by the speech of God and the Qur'an, whether it is this piecemeal [thing]...

This passage deals with two interconnected issues: the objection about God's speech to Moses, and the correct position of waqf (suspending judgment) on the ontological status of the Qur'an. God's Speech is Heard Through Created Mediums
Maturidi argues that God's speech is heard through created letters and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God's speech is heard through created sounds and letters, not directly from His essence.

Core Idea
What we hear is a created expression of God's eternal speech.

This passage establishes one of Maturidi's key arguments about the nature of divine speech. He proves that when we hear the Qur'an recited, what reaches our ears is a created sound, not the uncreated attribute itself. The reasoning involves logic about accidents, bodies, and location, and is supported by analogies about how we hear others' speech and recognize God's attributes through creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed proof style.
2
Identify the main claim and the logical structure
The passage begins with the claim that the heard speech is a creation. Then it presents a dichotomy: the heard speech is either an accident or a body, or neither. Consider why each option leads to an impossibility regarding location.
This is a classic kalam proof by disjunction.
3
Understand the conclusion about attribution
After ruling out the impossibility that the heard speech can be directly from God's essence, Maturidi concludes that the speech is attributed to God as its Creator, not as its location.
4
Reflect on the analogies provided
Maturidi gives two analogies: one about hearing another person's speech through a medium, and another about knowing God's attributes through creation. Think about how these support the main argument.
These analogies show that God can convey His speech through created mediums without that medium becoming His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقاويل. دليله أن ذلك خلق من الخلق، ولا يُحتمل أن يكون الله بذاته. مع ما لا يخلو من أن يكون المسموع عرضا فمحال كونه في مكانين وكذلك الجسم؛ أو لا هما، فمحال كونه في مكان؛ وعن المكان يُسمع. فثبت أن وجه الإضافة إليه على ما ذكرنا. مع ما يجوز أن يُسمعنا الله كلامه بما ليس بكلامه، كما أسمع كل منا آخر كلامه وإن لم يكن ذلك بعينه كلامه، وكما أعْلَمنا قدرته وعلمه وربوبيته بخلقه وإن لم يكن هو هو. وبالله التوفيق.
And statements. Its proof is that that is a creation from creation, and it is not possible that God [is] by His essence. Considering that what is heard must either be an accident - then it is impossible for it to be in two places - and likewise a body; or [it is] neither of them, then it is impossible for it to be in a place; and from a place it is heard. So it is established that the aspect of attribution to Him is as we mentioned. Along with the fact that it is permissible for God to make us hear His speech through that which is not His speech, just as He made each of us hear the speech of another even though that [speech] is not the same as his speech, and just as He made known to us His power, knowledge, and lordship through His creation even though it is not He Himself. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's speech, when heard by creatures, is through a created medium. The author begins by noting that the speech heard (by humans) is a created thing (خلق من الخلق). He argues that it is impossible for God's own essence to be the direct source of the audible speech, because what is heard must either be an accident (عرض) or a body (جسم), both of which are impossible to be in two places at once—i.e., both in God's essence and in the reciter's location. If the heard speech were neither accident nor body (i.e., an abstract entity), it could not exist in a place at all, yet it is heard from a specific place (like the mouth of a reader). Therefore, the attribution of speech to God is in the sense that He creates sounds and letters that convey His meaning, not that His eternal attribute becomes located in space. The author then supports this by analogy: God can make us hear His speech through something that is not His speech itself, just as one person can communicate another's speech without that utterance being the original speaker's own. Similarly, God makes known His power, knowledge, and lordship through His creation, even though those creations are not identical to His attributes. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine help.

Key Terms

عرض
جسم
بذاته
كلام الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an recited by Muslims is the uncreated attribute of God directly.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the audible Qur'an is a created expression, not the eternal attribute itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Maturidi gives for why God's speech cannot be heard directly from His essence?
A) Because God's essence is invisible
B) Because what is heard must exist in a place, but God's essence is not in a place
C) Because God does not speak to humans
D) Because Moses only heard a created sound

Key Takeaway
The audible Qur'an is a created expression of God's eternal speech, not the uncreated attribute itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Qur'an recited, do you feel you are hearing God's eternal speech directly? How does Maturidi's argument that the sounds are created change your understanding of that moment? Will you now listen with a greater awareness of the medium through which God speaks to you?

When you see His signs in the universe, do you pause to reflect on the Creator behind them, or do you get absorbed in the created itself? How can you train yourself to see through the sign to the One signified?

Moses Hearing God's Speech: The Answer to an Objection
Maturidi answers an objection about Moses hearing God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains that God made Moses hear His uncreated speech through created means.

Core Idea
God's speech is uncreated, but Moses heard it through created letters and sounds.

This lesson addresses a common objection: If God's speech is eternal and uncreated, how could Moses hear it? Maturidi clarifies that God made Moses hear His speech via created means, thus the heard speech is not the eternal attribute itself but its expression.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. The objection is in the form of a question; the answer begins with 'qīla'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that Maturidi distinguishes between the uncreated attribute (kalam Allah) and the created means (tongue, letters, sound) through which Moses heard it.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this distinction matters for preserving both God's transcendence and the historical event of Moses hearing His speech.
This relates to the broader debate on God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: هل أسمع الله كلامه موسى حيث قال: وكلم الله موسى تكليما؟ قيل أسمعه بلسان موسى وبحروف خلقها وصوت أنشأه، فهو أسمعه ما ليس بمخلوق. والقول بالوقف يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما أن يقال: ليس هو الله ولا غيره، فيكون وقفاً عن علم، وهو حق على ما ثبت في العلم والقدرة. والثاني أن يكون لا يعلم أخلق هو أو غيره، فإنه بعيد؛ لما لا يخلو من أن يذهب مذهب التقليد، وأكثر القوم على نفي ذلك، بل أجمع على لزوم العلم أنه الخلق أو غيره. وبعد، فإنه لا يعدو من أن: أ) يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم، فيكون بمعنى ما ذكرت؛ أو لا بذاته، فهو غيره، وكل الأغيار الله خلق: ما روي فيه سمع أو لا. ثبت أن القول بالغيرية الله يوجب الحدث، والحدث والخلق إذ هو منه؛ ب) أو لا يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم أو لا [بذاته]، فيكون الوقف وقفاً للجهل به فحق مثله التعلم؛ لأنه لا دليل دفعه إلى ذلك القول ليتكلم فيه، إنما هو الجهل. ج) أو أن يكون الوقف بما لا يعلم مراد السائل فيه: إنه ما يعني بكلام الله والقرآن، أهو هذا المتبعض المتجزئ، أو الذي لا يوصف بشيء من ذلك؟ وذلك على الوصف الذي بينا. فهو أحق أن لا يجيب لأحد يسأله عن كلام متوجه حتى يعلم ما يريد به. والله أعلم.
If a questioner says: Did God make Moses hear His speech where He said: 'And Allah spoke to Moses with [direct] speech'? He is told: He made him hear it by the tongue of Moses and by letters He created and a sound He originated, so He made him hear that which is not created. And the statement of suspension (al-waqf) comes out in two ways: One of them is that it is said: It is neither God nor other than Him, so it is a suspension based on knowledge, and it is true as established in [matters of] knowledge and power. The second is that one does not know whether it is created or other than that, and it is far-fetched; because it cannot avoid following the path of blind imitation, and most of the people deny that, rather they agree on the necessity of knowing whether it is creation or other. Then it does not go beyond that: (a) He knows that He is a speaker by essence, so it is in the sense I mentioned; or not by essence, so it is other than Him, and everything other than God is created: whether [the report] about it being heard or not. It is established that the statement of otherness regarding God necessitates temporal origination, and temporal origination and creation are from Him. (b) Or he does not know whether He is a speaker by essence or not, then the suspension is a suspension of ignorance about Him, and the like of it must be learning; because no argument pushed him to that statement to speak about it, it is only ignorance. (c) Or that the suspension is because he does not know the intention of the questioner in it: [in that] he does not know what is meant by the speech of God and the Qur'an, whether it is this divisible and partitionable [thing], or that which is not described by any of that? And that is according to the description we clarified. So it is more appropriate that he not answer anyone who asks him about speech generally until he knows what he means by it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a classification of the positions of waqf (suspension of judgment) regarding the nature of God's speech. First, an objector asks: Did God make Moses hear His speech directly, as the verse (4:164) says? Maturidi answers: God made Moses hear His speech through the tongue of Moses, using created letters and sound; thus, what Moses heard was the created expression, not the uncreated attribute. Then Maturidi discusses the waqf position. He says it can be of two types: (1) Waqf 'an 'ilm (suspension based on knowledge) – one says the heard speech is neither God Himself nor something else (i.e., not a separate created entity), and this is valid because the attributes of God are neither identical to His essence nor separate. (2) Waqf resulting from ignorance – one does not know whether the speech is created or not; this is blameworthy because it leads to blind imitation, and the community agrees that one must know whether it is created or not. Maturidi then breaks down the possibilities into three cases: (a) If one knows that God is a speaker by His essence, then the heard speech is a manifestation (as earlier explained); if not, then it is other than God and thus created. (b) If one does not know whether God is a speaker by essence, then the waqf is due to ignorance, and such a person must seek knowledge. (c) If one suspends judgment because the meaning of the question is unclear (e.g., whether 'speech of God' refers to the created Qur'an or the eternal attribute), then it is proper to refrain from answering until the question's intent is clarified. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
كلام الله
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Moses heard God's speech, then God's speech must be created.
Correction: God's speech is uncreated, but the physical sounds and letters Moses heard are created. Maturidi affirms the uncreated attribute while explaining the created medium.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how did Moses hear God's speech?
A) He heard God's uncreated essence directly.
B) He heard it through created letters and sound.
C) He heard it in a dream.
D) He did not hear it; the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Moses heard God's uncreated speech through created means, affirming both God's transcendence and the historical event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you feel a direct connection to the uncreated speech of Allah, or do you often get distracted by the created sounds you hear? What changes would you make to ensure you are truly engaging with the divine attribute?
?
If God’s speech is uncreated yet reaches you through created means, what does that imply about your responsibility in transmitting His words to others? Will you allow your own created tongue to distort or beautify what is uncreated?
?
The objection assumes God's speech cannot be heard unless it is created. Are there areas in your faith where you struggle to accept divine realities without reducing them to human-like terms? Will you surrender to the mystery or insist on full comprehension?

The Two Types of Waqf and Their Implications
Maturidi explains the two types of waqf and the three possibilities under the second type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of waqf into two types and the three possible cases under the second type.

Core Idea
Waqf is only valid when based on knowledge; ignorance-based waqf is blameworthy and must be resolved.

After clarifying how Moses heard God's speech, Maturidi turns to the positions of waqf (suspension of judgment) held by some groups. He distinguishes between a legitimate suspension based on knowledge and a blameworthy one resulting from ignorance, then elaborates on three scenarios that fall under the latter.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of waqf
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'wal-qawl bil-waqf yakhruju 'ala wajhayn'. Identify the two types: knowledge-based and ignorance-based.
2
Understand the first type
The first type is waqf 'an 'ilm: one says the heard speech is neither God nor other than Him. This is acceptable and based on proper knowledge.
This aligns with the earlier explanation of attributes.
3
Examine the second type and its three cases
The second type is ignorance-based. Maturidi then breaks it down into three sub-cases: (a) knowing God as speaker by essence, (b) not knowing that, and (c) suspending because the question's meaning is unclear.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice Maturidi's final point: it is better not to answer a vague question until its intent is understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: هل أسمع الله كلامه موسى حيث قال: وكلم الله موسى تكليما؟ قيل أسمعه بلسان موسى وبحروف خلقها وصوت أنشأه، فهو أسمعه ما ليس بمخلوق. والقول بالوقف يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما أن يقال: ليس هو الله ولا غيره، فيكون وقفاً عن علم، وهو حق على ما ثبت في العلم والقدرة. والثاني أن يكون لا يعلم أخلق هو أو غيره، فإنه بعيد؛ لما لا يخلو من أن يذهب مذهب التقليد، وأكثر القوم على نفي ذلك، بل أجمع على لزوم العلم أنه الخلق أو غيره. وبعد، فإنه لا يعدو من أن: أ) يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم، فيكون بمعنى ما ذكرت؛ أو لا بذاته، فهو غيره، وكل الأغيار الله خلق: ما روي فيه سمع أو لا. ثبت أن القول بالغيرية الله يوجب الحدث، والحدث والخلق إذ هو منه؛ ب) أو لا يعلم أنه بذاته متكلم أو لا [بذاته]، فيكون الوقف وقفاً للجهل به فحق مثله التعلم؛ لأنه لا دليل دفعه إلى ذلك القول ليتكلم فيه، إنما هو الجهل. ج) أو أن يكون الوقف بما لا يعلم مراد السائل فيه: إنه ما يعني بكلام الله والقرآن، أهو هذا المتبعض المتجزئ، أو الذي لا يوصف بشيء من ذلك؟ وذلك على الوصف الذي بينا. فهو أحق أن لا يجيب لأحد يسأله عن كلام متوجه حتى يعلم ما يريد به. والله أعلم.
If a questioner says: Did God make Moses hear His speech where He said: 'And Allah spoke to Moses with [direct] speech'? He is told: He made him hear it by the tongue of Moses and by letters He created and a sound He originated, so He made him hear that which is not created. And the statement of suspension (al-waqf) comes out in two ways: One of them is that it is said: It is neither God nor other than Him, so it is a suspension based on knowledge, and it is true as established in [matters of] knowledge and power. The second is that one does not know whether it is created or other than that, and it is far-fetched; because it cannot avoid following the path of blind imitation, and most of the people deny that, rather they agree on the necessity of knowing whether it is creation or other. Then it does not go beyond that: (a) He knows that He is a speaker by essence, so it is in the sense I mentioned; or not by essence, so it is other than Him, and everything other than God is created: whether [the report] about it being heard or not. It is established that the statement of otherness regarding God necessitates temporal origination, and temporal origination and creation are from Him. (b) Or he does not know whether He is a speaker by essence or not, then the suspension is a suspension of ignorance about Him, and the like of it must be learning; because no argument pushed him to that statement to speak about it, it is only ignorance. (c) Or that the suspension is because he does not know the intention of the questioner in it: [in that] he does not know what is meant by the speech of God and the Qur'an, whether it is this divisible and partitionable [thing], or that which is not described by any of that? And that is according to the description we clarified. So it is more appropriate that he not answer anyone who asks him about speech generally until he knows what he means by it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a classification of the positions of waqf (suspension of judgment) regarding the nature of God's speech. First, an objector asks: Did God make Moses hear His speech directly, as the verse (4:164) says? Maturidi answers: God made Moses hear His speech through the tongue of Moses, using created letters and sound; thus, what Moses heard was the created expression, not the uncreated attribute. Then Maturidi discusses the waqf position. He says it can be of two types: (1) Waqf 'an 'ilm (suspension based on knowledge) – one says the heard speech is neither God Himself nor something else (i.e., not a separate created entity), and this is valid because the attributes of God are neither identical to His essence nor separate. (2) Waqf resulting from ignorance – one does not know whether the speech is created or not; this is blameworthy because it leads to blind imitation, and the community agrees that one must know whether it is created or not. Maturidi then breaks down the possibilities into three cases: (a) If one knows that God is a speaker by His essence, then the heard speech is a manifestation (as earlier explained); if not, then it is other than God and thus created. (b) If one does not know whether God is a speaker by essence, then the waqf is due to ignorance, and such a person must seek knowledge. (c) If one suspends judgment because the meaning of the question is unclear (e.g., whether 'speech of God' refers to the created Qur'an or the eternal attribute), then it is proper to refrain from answering until the question's intent is clarified. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
الوقف
الوقف عن علم
الوقف عن جهل
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All suspension of judgment (waqf) regarding God's speech is permissible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that only waqf based on knowledge is acceptable; waqf from ignorance is blameworthy and requires seeking knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is wrong with the second type of waqf?
A) It is forbidden because it leads to disbelief.
B) It relies on blind imitation and the person must learn the truth.
C) It is the same as the first type.
D) It only applies to the created speech.

Key Takeaway
Waqf is only valid when based on knowledge; ignorance-based waqf is blameworthy and must be resolved through learning, and one should clarify the question before answering.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I don't know' when asked about matters of faith. Is your waqf based on knowledge or on ignorance? Will you commit to learning one specific aspect of God's attributes this week?
?
When you are uncertain about a theological issue, do you honestly seek knowledge or do you passively rely on the opinions of others without verification? What step will you take today to move from taqlid to true certainty?
?
In what area of your belief are you afraid to dig deeper because it might challenge comfortable assumptions? What if your ignorance is blameworthy before God—are you willing to confront it?

Refuting 'God's Actions Are by Choice'
Maturidi critiques al-Ka'bi's statement via logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'God's actions are by choice' is philosophically problematic within its own framework and how Maturidi resolves it through the concept of eternal will.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi's phrase leads to a logical dilemma: redundancy or infinite regress.

In this passage, Maturidi engages with al-Ka'bi's claim that God's actions are by choice. While affirming the underlying intent to uphold divine agency, he identifies a critical flaw in the wording that undermines the claim from within al-Ka'bi's own system. By analyzing the logical implications, Maturidi clarifies the proper way to speak about divine will and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: a dilemma with two horns.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Maturidi presents two possibilities: either creation is identical to God's choice or it is other than His choice. Find these in the text.
The Arabic uses the conditional 'إن' to introduce each option.
3
Analyze each horn's consequence
For the first horn (identical), the phrase becomes redundant and wrong. For the second horn (other), it leads to an infinite regress if the other is an action, or a contradiction if not. Trace these consequences in the commentary.
4
Understand Maturidi's resolution
Maturidi concludes that the proper formulation is to say God has eternal will, which is the choice of a thing's existence at its time. This avoids the dilemma.
This connects to the broader discussion of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ في أن أفعال الله باختيار ] وقال الكعبي: أفعال الله باختيار لأن المطبوع يكون فعله نوعا [واحد]. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وما قاله حسن، وذلك مذهب أهل التوحيد. لكنه لا معنى له على مذهبه؛ لما يقال: الخلق اختياره أو غيره، وكذلك ما يقال في سائر أفعاله؟ فإن قال: اختیاره، فمعنى أفعاله إذا اختياره، فالقول بأن أفعال الله باختيار خطأ، بل هو اختيار، ولا غير هنالك ليقال الذي قال. وإن قال: غيره، فإما أن يكون فعله، فيجب أن يكون باختيار إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك محال، لأن الخلق متناه؛ أو هو فعل بلا اختيار فيبطل قوله. وذلك يلزم من يصف الله بالإرادة في الأزل، وهي اختيار كون شيء في وقته.
Question [concerning that God's actions are by choice]. And al-Ka'bi said: God's actions are by choice because the natural (al-matbu') has its action [as] one type. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: What he said is good, and that is the doctrine of the people of tawhid. However, it has no meaning according to his school; because it is said: Is creation His choice or other than that? And likewise regarding His other actions. If he says: His choice, then the meaning of His actions is His choice, so the statement 'God's actions are by choice' is wrong; rather, it is choice, and there is no other[thing] there for which he said what he said. And if he says: Other than it, then either it is His action, then it must be by choice ad infinitum, and that is impossible because creation is finite; or it is an action without choice, so his statement is invalid. And that applies to one who describes God with will in eternity, and it is choice of a thing being in its time.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Ka'bi's formulation that 'God's actions are by choice.' Maturidi first notes that al-Ka'bi's intention is good (since it affirms God's voluntary agency), but the phrasing is problematic within al-Ka'bi's own system. The problem: if one asks whether 'creation' is identical to God's choice or something else, two options arise. First, if one says creation is His choice, then 'actions' would be synonymous with 'choice,' making the phrase 'actions by choice' redundant and incorrect—it should simply say 'God is choice.' Second, if one says creation is other than His choice, then either that other thing is an action (requiring another choice ad infinitum, which is impossible) or it is an action without choice (contradicting al-Ka'bi's claim). Thus the proper formulation is that God has will in eternity, which is the choice of a thing's existence at its appointed time.

Key Terms
اختيار
فعل الله
إرادة
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'God's actions are by choice' is a correct and unproblematic way to affirm God's voluntary agency.
Correction: Maturidi argues that this phrasing leads to a logical dilemma: either it is redundant (if action equals choice) or it leads to an infinite regress or contradiction (if action is separate from choice). The proper formulation is that God has will in eternity, which is the choice of a thing's existence at its time.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main objection to al-Ka'bi's statement 'God's actions are by choice'?
A) Al-Ka'bi denies God's will
B) The phrasing leads to redundancy or infinite regress
C) Actions cannot be attributed to God
D) Choice is only for humans

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the phrase 'God's actions are by choice' as logically problematic and instead affirms that God's eternal will is the choice of a thing's existence at its appointed time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God chooses', do you unconsciously imagine a process similar to human decision-making? How does Maturidi's identification of God's will as eternal choice challenge that mental image, and what does it demand of your understanding of God's timelessness?
?
If you have ever thought of God's actions as separate from His will (like a builder choosing to build), consider the consequence of infinite regress. How does this logical refutation unsettle your assumptions, and are you willing to accept that God's will and action are one in eternity?
?
Maturidi says that the phrase 'actions by choice' is actually wrong when pressed logically. How does this make you reconsider the way you speak about God? Are there other common phrases about God that you might need to examine more carefully for hidden logical pitfalls?

The Names of Allah: Distinct in Meaning, Uncreated Attributes
Maturidi distinguishes between names as distinct in meaning and denies created mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the names we use for Allah refer to distinct attributes and that Allah's attributes, such as mercy, are not created.

Core Idea
Allah's names are many, each unique; His mercy is uncreated.

Maturidi introduces the topic of Allah's names, emphasizing that each name denotes a different attribute. He uses the example of created mercy to clarify that Allah's attribute of mercy is eternal and not a created thing, guarding against misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of Maturidi's argument. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the key points.
The passage is incomplete but carries a complete argument.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that Maturidi says names like 'Knower' and 'Powerful' are distinct (aghyar). They are not synonyms but refer to different attributes.
This rejects the idea that all names are equivalent.
3
Analyze the mercy example
Maturidi mentions that Allah created mercy, but He is not merciful with that created mercy. Understand that the created mercy is a separate entity from Allah's eternal attribute of mercy.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: why does Maturidi stress this distinction? How does it protect the belief in Allah's transcendence?
It maintains that Allah's attributes are not dependent on creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في أسماء الله عز وجل قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : القول في أسماء الله عز وجل عندنا على أقسام في مفهوم قسم منها يرجع إلى تسميتنا له بها وهن أغيار ; لأن قولنا عليم غير قولنا قدير; وعلى هذا المروي: إن الله تعالى كذا وكذا اسما. وذلك نحو ما ذكر من أنه خلق كذا وكذا رحمة، لا أنه كان رحيما بتلك الرحمة المخلوقة، إذ لا يُحتمل أن يكون في أول خلقه غير
A question concerning the names of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The statement concerning the names of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, according to us, is divided into categories in concept: one category of them goes back to our naming Him with them, and they are distinct (aghyar); because our saying 'Knower' (ʿAlim) is different from our saying 'Powerful' (Qadir). And upon this is the transmitted report: 'Indeed Allah, exalted, has such-and-such names.' And that is like what is mentioned that He created such-and-such mercy, not that He was merciful with that created mercy, for it is inconceivable that He should be, at the beginning of His creation, other than
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new section on the divine names (asmāʾ Allāh). Maturidi (Abu Mansur) states that the discussion concerning Allah's names, according to us (the Sunni school), is divided into categories in terms of their concept. One category pertains to our naming Him with these names; these names are distinct from one another (aghyar), meaning that each name has a distinct meaning. For example, our saying 'Knower' (ʿAlim) is not the same as our saying 'Powerful' (Qadir); they refer to different attributes (knowledge and power). This is consistent with the hadith that Allah has ninety-nine names. Maturidi then gives an example: Allah created mercy (al-rahma), but He was not merciful with that created mercy. The mercy He created is a created thing, separate from His attribute of mercy. It is inconceivable that Allah would be, at the beginning of His creation, other than what He eternally is. In other words, His attribute of mercy is uncreated and eternal, not dependent on a created entity. The passage is incomplete but establishes the distinction between the names we use (which are many and distinct) and the fact that Allah's attributes are not created. This guards against anthropomorphism and the idea that Allah acquires new attributes through creation.

تسمية
عليم
قدير
الرحمة المخلوقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's mercy is a created attribute acquired through creation.
Correction: Maturidi explains that Allah created mercy, but He was not merciful with that created mercy; His attribute of mercy is uncreated and eternal.

Checkpoint

D) That mercy was created for the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Allah's names are distinct in meaning, each referring to a unique attribute, and His attributes like mercy are uncreated and eternal.

First Category of Divine Names: Names as Designations
Maturidi defines the first category of divine names as designations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first category of divine names consists of those we give to Allah, which are distinct appellations derived from His actions and not essential attributes.

Core Idea
Divine names like 'Rahim' are designations from His mercy, not the mercy itself.

Maturidi begins his classification of divine names by identifying a category that might be surprising: names that are our designations. These names are not identical to Allah's essence but are derived from His actions and created things. Understanding this category is key to avoiding anthropomorphism and attributing created qualities to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very condensed style here.
2
Identify the examples given
The passage gives several examples of this first category: 'Alim' vs 'Qadir', 'Rahim' and created mercy, 'Paradise', 'rain', and acts of worship. Notice how each is a designation based on something created or commanded.
3
Grasp the underlying principle
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi insist that these names are 'distinct things' (أغيار) and not essential attributes? The key is to avoid thinking that Allah's names imply He is composed of parts or that He acquires new attributes over time.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في أسماء الله عز وجل عندنا على أقسام في مفهوم قسم منها يرجع إلى تسميتنا له بها وهن أغيار؛ لأن قولنا عليم غير قولنا قدير؛ وعلى هذا المروي: إن الله تعالى كذا وكذا اسما. وذلك نحو ما ذكر من أنه خلق كذا وكذا رحمة، لا أنه كان رحيما بتلك الرحمة المخلوقة، إذ لا يُحتمل أن يكون في أول خلقه غير رحيم، أو كان كذلك غير رحيم حتى خلق تلك الرحمة وجعلها واحدة بين خلقه، ولكن بما برحمته سميت به؛ وكذلك اسم الجنة والمطر ونحوه وعلى ذلك قيل في العبادات: هي أمره، وإنما كانت به، لا أنها هو. ومثله يتكلم بعلمه وقدرته على إرادة معلومه ومقدوره؛ إذ ذلك سببه، فمثله الأول ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The statement concerning the names of God, Mighty and Majestic, with us is upon divisions in meaning. One division returns to our naming Him thereby, and they are distinct things; because our saying 'Alim (Knowing)' is other than our saying 'Qadir (Powerful)'. And upon this is the narrated [hadith]: 'God Almighty has such-and-such a name.' That is like what was mentioned that He created such-and-such mercy, not that He was Merciful with that created mercy, for it is not plausible that He was in the beginning of His creation not Merciful, or that He was similarly not Merciful until He created that mercy and made it one among His creation, but rather by His mercy it is named. Likewise the name of Paradise and rain and the like, and upon that it is said concerning acts of worship: they are His command, and they are only through Him, not that they are He. And similarly He speaks by His knowledge and power concerning the intention of His known and His willed; for that is their cause, so the first is like it. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine names. Maturidi states that the names of God are divided into categories in meaning. The first category consists of names that we give to God, and these names are distinct from each other (e.g., 'Alim is not the same as Qadir). He refers to the hadith that God has a certain number of names. He then explains that names like 'Rahim' (Merciful) are derived from a created mercy; God is not called 'Rahim' because He possesses that particular created mercy, but because He is merciful. It is not plausible that He was not merciful until He created mercy. Rather, the name is given based on His mercy. The same applies to names like 'Paradise' and 'rain' which are used metaphorically. Similarly, acts of worship are described as His command; they exist through Him, but they are not identical to Him. He also speaks using His knowledge and power to bring about what He wills, as they are the means. Thus the first category is explained.

أسماء
تسمية
أغيار
عليم
قدير
رحمة مخلوقة
رحيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this denies the attribute of mercy; rather it distinguishes between a created mercy and the essential attribute of being merciful.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny that Allah is Merciful; he clarifies that the name 'Rahim' is not derived from a created mercy but from Allah's inherent mercy, and the created mercy is a separate entity named after His mercy.

Checkpoint

C) It means the same as 'Qadir'

Key Takeaway
The first category of divine names are designations from Allah's actions and creations, not essential attributes, thus avoiding attributing created qualities to Allah.

Second Category of Divine Names: Names of the Essence
Maturidi describes the second category of divine names that refer to God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second category of divine names according to Imam Maturidi — those that refer to God's essence itself and how they differ from attribute names.

Core Idea
Divine essence names point directly to God's reality, beyond human language and understanding.

Maturidi now turns to the second category of divine names: those that refer to God's essence itself. These names are not attributes but names that indicate the very reality of God. They are unique because creation cannot fully grasp the intended meaning of the essence except through these names, even though God transcends letters and sounds. Let's examine this important distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to get the gist. Notice that Maturidi says these names 'return to His essence.'
2
Identify the key point about essence names
Maturidi explains that these names are the only means by which creation can refer to God's essence, but they are limited by language. He says 'though He is exalted above letters.'
God is beyond phonetic sounds, yet we use these names.
3
Look at the examples given
Maturidi lists six names: al-Wahid, Allah, al-Rahman, al-Mawjud, al-Qadim, al-Ma'bud. Reflect on what they have in common — all directly relate to God's being.
4
Understand the implication for theology
This category clarifies that some names are not about actions or attributes but about God's very existence and identity. It distinguishes essence names from attribute names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني يرجع معناه إلى ذاته مما عجز الخلق عن الوقوف على مراد ذاته إلا به، وإن كان يتعالى عن الحروف التي بها يفهم. وذلك أيضا يختلف باختلاف الألسن على إرادة حقيقة ذاته به، وذلك نحو الواحد الله الرحمن الموجود، والقديم، والمعبود، ونحو ذلك.
And the second returns in meaning to His essence, from which the creation is incapable of arriving at the intended meaning of His essence except through it, even though He is exalted above letters by which it is understood. And that also differs according to the difference of tongues concerning intending the reality of His essence by it. And that is like al-Wahid (the One), Allah, al-Rahman (the Most Merciful), al-Mawjud (the Existent), al-Qadim (the Ancient), al-Ma'bud (the Worshipped), and the like.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage describes the second category of divine names: those that refer to God's essence itself. Maturidi explains that these names indicate the reality of His essence, though the creation cannot fully grasp the intended meaning of His essence except through these names. He is exalted above letters (i.e., above phonetic sounds) by which things are normally understood. These names vary according to languages, but they all intend the same reality of His essence. Examples given: al-Wahid (the One), Allah, al-Rahman (the Most Merciful), al-Mawjud (the Existent), al-Qadim (the Ancient), al-Ma'bud (the Worshipped), and similar names.

Key Terms
ذات
الواحد
الله
الرحمن
الموجود
القديم
المعبود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That names like Allah and al-Wahid are attributes rather than names of the essence.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that these names 'return in meaning to His essence,' meaning they directly refer to God's being, not a separate attribute.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a name from the second category?
A) Alim
B) Qadir
C) Allah
D) Rahim

Key Takeaway
Maturidi identifies a distinct category of divine names that refer directly to God's essence, demonstrating that not all divine names describe attributes or actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allah' in prayer, do you recognize that you are calling upon His very essence, which is beyond your full comprehension? How does this awareness affect the sincerity of your worship? Will you pause before His name today, acknowledging your own limitation?
?
Maturidi says creation cannot grasp the intended meaning of the essence except through these names. Have you ever assumed you fully understand who God is? What would change in your heart if you truly accepted that His reality transcends every letter and sound?
?
These names vary across languages but point to the same essence. Do you treat the Arabic names as mere labels, or do they connect you to the unutterable reality of God? How can you deepen your reverence for His names, knowing they are the only bridge between your limited mind and His infinite being?

Third category: Derived names and refutation of createdness
Maturidi enumerates the third category of divine names and refutes the claim that they are temporally created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that derived divine names like al-Alim and al-Qadir are not created but are eternal attributes of God.

Core Idea
If God's names were created, He would have been ignorant in pre-eternity.

Maturidi continues his discussion of divine names by focusing on those derived from attributes, such as al-Alim (the Knowing) and al-Qadir (the Powerful). He first establishes that if these names were distinct from God's essence, they would be subject to change. Then, he confronts the view that these names are created in time, using a logical dilemma about God's knowledge before creation. This passage solidifies the Maturidi position that God's attributes are eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source text and its literal English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage contains both a general principle and a specific refutation.
2
Understand the first conditional argument
Notice that Maturidi argues that if the names like 'Alim were actually other than God, they would be subject to change (تبديل). Also, if naming were arbitrary, any name could be applied. This reinforces that the names are real attributes.
3
Analyze the refutation of createdness
Focus on the dilemma about God's knowledge before creation. Maturidi asks: did God know His essence and actions or not? If He did not know, He would have been ignorant; if He knew, He knows Himself as knowing, so the name 'Alim' is necessary in pre-eternity. This proves the names are eternal.
This is the core argument against the createdness of divine names.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
See how this argument supports the Maturidi belief that God's attributes are not created but are eternal aspects of His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث يرجع من نحو العالم والقادر، مما لو كانت في التحقيق غيره لاحتمل التبديل. ولو جازت التسمية على غير تحقيق المعنى المفهوم الجازت تسميته بكل ما يسمى به غيره إذا لم يرد تحقيق المفهوم من معناه ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما يسأل من يجعل هذه الأسماء حادثة، ثم لا يحقق الله علما في الأزل: إذ كيف كان أمره قبل الخلق أكان يعلم ذاته أو ما يفعل، أو لا؟ وكذلك أكان يعلم ذاته شيئا أو لا يعلمه؟ فإن كان لا يعلمه، فهو إذا جاهل حتى كان يعلمه، فإذا يعلم ذاته عالما أو لا؟ فإن علمه عالما لزم القول بهذا الاسم في الأزل.
And the third returns from like al-Alim (the Knowing) and al-Qadir (the Powerful), such that if they were in reality other than Him, they would be liable to change. And if naming were permitted without realization of the understood meaning, it would permit calling Him by everything by which other than Him is called if one does not intend the realization of the meaning from its signification. And there is no power except in God. Along with what is asked of one who makes these names created: then he does not verify that God had knowledge in pre-eternity. For how was His affair before creation: did He know His essence or what He does, or not? And likewise, did He know His essence as something or not know it? If He did not know it, then He is ignorant until He knew it. Then if He knows His essence as knowing or not? If He knows it as knowing, then the statement concerning this name in pre-eternity is necessary.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage covers the third category of divine names: derived names such as al-Alim (the Knowing) and al-Qadir (the Powerful). Maturidi argues that if these names were in reality something other than God, they would be subject to change. Moreover, if naming were allowed without real meaning, then anything could be called by any name, which is absurd. He then addresses an objection from those who consider these names to be created in time. He asks: would the one who holds this view also deny that God had knowledge in pre-eternity? He poses a dilemma: before creation, did God know His essence and His actions or not? If He did not know, then He would have been ignorant until He learned, which is impossible. If He knows, then He knows Himself as knowing or not? If He knows Himself as knowing, then the name 'Knowing' (al-Alim) is necessarily applied to Him in pre-eternity. Thus the names are not created but eternal.

Key Terms
عالم
قادر
تبديل
حادثة
أزل
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about the names being physically created.
Correction: The objection is actually about their temporal origination (حادثة), implying that if they are created in time, then God lacked those attributes in pre-eternity, leading to deficiency.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue against those who say the names like 'Alim' are created?
A) That would imply Allah was ignorant before
B) That would make the names meaningless
C) That would contradict the hadith of ninety-nine names
D) That would necessitate anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that derived divine names like al-Alim are not created by showing that denying their eternality leads to absurd conclusions about God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-Alim', do you truly believe that God has known you with all your secrets and failures from before time began, or do you treat it as a title without real content? How would your daily consciousness change if you lived as though His knowledge of you is eternal and inescapable? Commit to one moment today where you consciously remember that God sees you exactly as you are.
?
Maturidi says that if God did not know His essence in pre-eternity, He would have been ignorant. Do you ever act as if God's knowledge is incomplete — that He is 'figuring out' your life as it unfolds? Where in your heart do you doubt that His knowledge of you was perfect before you were born? Repent of that subtle disbelief right now.
?
The argument shows that denying the eternality of God's names leads to absurdity. Have you ever, even unconsciously, limited God by thinking He 'acquired' a quality after creation? What would it look like for you to worship a God whose attributes never increase or decrease — and how does that demand total reliance on Him?

Three Categories of Divine Names in Maturidi's Theology
Maturidi classifies divine names into three categories according to their meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of divine names into three categories based on their meaning and how this refutes arbitrary naming.

Core Idea
Divine names are not arbitrary but reflect real connections to God's essence, attributes, or actions.

Maturidi presents a careful distinction among the names of Allah. Some names derive from His actions and effects, others point directly to His essence, and still others come from His attributes. This passage establishes that names have a basis in reality, preventing any superficial or metaphorical understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the rhythm and the three categories introduced.
The text uses 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث' to mark categories.
2
Identify the three categories
List each category as described: names of effects/actions, names of essence, and names of attributes. Note examples for each.
First category includes 'رحميم' and 'الجنة'. Second includes 'الواحد', 'الله'. Third includes 'العالم', 'القادر'.
3
Understand the refutation of arbitrary naming
Notice the final sentence where Maturidi argues that if naming were arbitrary, any name could be used. This shows the necessity of a real connection.
The phrase 'ولو جازت التسمية على غير تحقيق المعنى' underscores the point.
4
Connect to the common misunderstanding
Reflect on the misconception that all names are conventional. Maturidi's classification provides a framework for understanding their objective basis.
This aligns with the common misunderstanding stated in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت رحيم، أو كان كذلك غير رحيم حتى خلق تلك الرحمة وجعلها واحدة بين خلقه، ولكن بما برحمته سُمّيت به؛ وكذلك اسم الجنة والمطر ونحوه وعلى ذلك قيل في العبادات: هي أمره، وإنما كانت به، لا أنها هو. ومثله الأول ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثاني يرجع معناه إلى ذاته مما عجز الخلق عن الوقوف على مراد ذاته إلا به، وإن كان يتعالى عن الحروف التي بها يُفهم. وذلك أيضاً يختلف باختلاف الألسن على إرادة حقيقة ذاته به، وذلك نحو الواحد الله الرحمن الموجود، والقديم، والمعبود، ونحو ذلك. والثالث يرجع من نحو العالم والقادر، مما لو كانت في التحقيق غيره لاحتمل التبديل. ولو جازت التسمية على غير تحقيق المعنى المفهوم لجازت تسميته بكل ما يسمى به غيره إذا لم يُرد تحقيق المفهوم من معناه ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He was merciful, or would he not have been merciful until He created that mercy and made it one among His creation? But it is by His mercy that it is named. Likewise the name of paradise and rain and the like. And upon that it is said concerning acts of worship: they are His command, and they exist by Him, not that they are He. And the like is the first. And there is no power except with Allah. The second returns in its meaning to His essence, from that which the creation is incapable of grasping the intended meaning of His essence except by it, although He is exalted above the letters by which it is understood. And that also differs according to tongues concerning the intention of the reality of His essence by it. That is like 'the One', 'Allah', 'the Merciful', 'the Existent', 'the Eternal', 'the Worshipped', and the like. The third returns from that like 'the Knower' and 'the Powerful', such that if they were in reality other than Him, they would be subject to change. And if naming were allowed without verifying the understood meaning, then it would be allowed to name Him with everything by which others are named if one does not intend the verification of the understood meaning. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines a classification of divine names into three categories. First, names that refer to God's actions or effects, like 'Merciful' which derives from His mercy, but not that He is mercy itself. Similarly, names like 'paradise' and 'rain' refer to created things. Acts of worship are His command, existing by Him, not identical to Him. Second, names that refer to His essence directly, such as 'One', 'Allah', 'Merciful' as an essential name? Actually careful: The second category includes names like 'al-Wahid', 'Allah', 'ar-Rahman', 'al-Mawjud', 'al-Qadim', 'al-Ma'bud'. These refer to His essence beyond comprehension. Third, names derived from attributes like 'al-'Alim' and 'al-Qadir', which if they were other than Him would imply change. The overall point is that names are not arbitrary; they have a basis in reality. If naming were arbitrary, it would be permissible to call Him by any name used for others, which is false.

Key Terms
الاسم
الصفة
الذات
الرحمة
العلم
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all names of God are merely conventional and have no real connection to His essence.
Correction: Maturidi stresses that some names are based on actual meaning: the three categories show that names are tied to actions, essence, or attributes, and arbitrary naming is rejected.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is NOT an example of the second category of names (those referring to essence)?
A) al-Wahid
B) al-Qadir
C) Allah
D) al-Mawjud

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's three categories of divine names ensure they are grounded in reality, preventing arbitrary or merely conventional naming.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call God 'Merciful,' do you truly believe His mercy preceded all creation and is not like human mercy? How does that change your trust in His mercy today?
?
Think of a name of God you use often in du'a. Does your understanding of that name align with Maturidi's categories, or have you been using it casually without reflection? What step will you take to rectify that?
?
If divine names are not arbitrary but grounded in reality, how must this affect the way you speak about God in your daily conversations? Will you be more careful? What will you do differently starting this moment?

Divine Attributes

6 min
Refutation of the Createdness of Divine Names
Maturidi refutes created-names view by showing entailment of ignorance in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that divine names are created leads to the corruption of tawhid and implies that God was ignorant before creation.

Core Idea
If God's names are created, then God was ignorant before they existed.

Imam Maturidi presents a logical refutation against those who assert that the divine names are created in time. By examining the implications of such a claim, he shows that it forces us to say either that God was ignorant of His own essence before creation, which is impossible, or that the name is eternal. This passage demonstrates how the names are not mere labels but are rooted in God's eternal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical questions and the logical structure.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the opponent's position
The opponent claims that divine names like 'Knower' are created. Maturidi begins by questioning this view.
3
Understand the dilemma
Maturidi asks: before creation, did God know His essence or not? If not, He was ignorant until He knew, which is impossible. If yes, then the name 'Knower' applies in eternity, contradicting its createdness.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the otherness of the name (ghayriyya) corrupts tawhid, because it introduces a distinction between God and His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما يُسأل من يجعل هذه الأسماء حادثة، ثم لا يحقق الله علما في الأزل: إذ كيف كان أمره قبل الخلق أكان يعلم ذاته أو ما يفعل، أو لا؟ وكذلك أكان يعلم ذاته شيئا أو لا يعلمه؟ فإن كان لا يعلمه، فهو إذا جاهل حتى كان يعلمه، فاذا يعلم ذاته عالما أو لا؟ فإن علمه عالما لزم القول بهذا الاسم في الأزل. وفي غيرية الاسم فساد التوحيد.
Along with what is asked of one who makes these names created: then he does not verify that God has knowledge in eternity. For how was His affair before creation? Did He know His essence or what He does, or not? Likewise, did He know His essence as something or did He not know it? If He did not know it, then He was ignorant until He knew it. Then, does He know His essence as knowing or not? If He knows it as knowing, then the statement of this name (i.e., 'Knower') is necessitated in eternity. And in the otherness of the name lies the corruption of tawhid.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against those who claim that divine names like 'al-'Alim' are created events. The argument proceeds by examining the state of God before creation. If names are created, then before creation God did not have the name 'Knower'. But then one must ask: Did God know His essence and His actions before creation? If He did not know, then He was ignorant until He acquired knowledge. That would imply change in God. If He did know, then He was knowing, and the name 'Knower' must apply to Him in eternity, not as created. Hence, the view that names are created leads to the corruption of tawhid by allowing otherness in God's essence.

Key Terms
حادثة
الأزل
الجاهل
العالم
غيرية
التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is about Allah's knowledge being created.
Correction: The argument is specifically about the divine names being created, not about knowledge itself. The consequence is that if names are created, then knowledge would be acquired in time, implying ignorance in eternity.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of claiming that the name 'al-'Alim' is created?
A) God was always knowing
B) God was ignorant before creation
C) Tawhid is preserved
D) Names are arbitrary

Key Takeaway
Divine names are not created; they express eternal attributes of God, and claiming otherwise corrupts tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah’s knowledge of your essence and actions is eternal, how does that challenge your assumption that you can hide any part of yourself from Him? Will you live today with that awareness?
?
The argument shows that denying the eternity of a name leads to attributing ignorance to Allah. Are there any names or attributes you find difficult to affirm? What does that reveal about your heart?
?
The phrase 'otherness of the name corrupts tawhid'—do you ever treat Allah’s names as separate entities rather than expressions of His essence? How can you correct that in your worship?
